Sukkah



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A <i>sukka</i>,</b> i.e., its roofing, which is the main and most crucial element of the mitzva, <b>that is more than twenty cubits high is unfit. Rabbi Yehuda deems it fit.</b>
Similarly, a <i>sukka</i> <b>that is not</b> even <b>ten handbreadths high, and</b> one <b>that does not have three walls, and</b> one <b>whose sunlight</b> that passes through its roofing <b>is greater than its shade are unfit.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>We learned</b> a similar <i>halakha</i> in a mishna <b>there,</b> in tractate <i>Eiruvin</i> (2a): In the case of <b>an alleyway that is higher than twenty cubits,</b> i.e., the beam that was placed across the end of an alleyway that opens into a public domain in order to permit carrying within the alleyway on Shabbat is higher than twenty cubits, <b>one</b> must <b>diminish</b> the height of the beam in order to permit carrying within the alleyway. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says he need not</b> do so, and although the beam lies higher than twenty cubits, the alleyway is qualified to permit carrying within.
Given the seeming similarity between the two cases, that of the <i>sukka</i> and that of the alleyway, the Gemara asks: <b>What is different with regard to a <i>sukka</i> where</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> that it is <b>unfit, and what is different with regard to an alleyway where</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> the method of <b>rectification,</b> that one must diminish the height of the cross beam? Why was a solution not suggested in the case of a <i>sukka</i>?
The Gemara answers: With regard to <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> since it is a mitzva <b>by Torah law,</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> that it is <b>unfit,</b> as, if it is not constructed in the proper manner, no mitzva is fulfilled. However, with regard to <b>an alleyway,</b> where the entire prohibition of carrying is only <b>by rabbinic law,</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> the method of <b>rectification,</b> as the cross beam comes only to rectify a rabbinic prohibition but does not involve a mitzva by Torah law.
The Gemara suggests an alternative explanation: <b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that <b>even</b> with regard to matters prohibited <b>by Torah law,</b> it would have been appropriate for the mishna to teach a method of <b>rectification. However,</b> with regard to <b><i>sukka</i>, whose matters are numerous, it categorically teaches</b> that it is <b>unfit.</b> Merely diminishing the height of a <i>sukka</i> is insufficient to render it fit; the <i>sukka</i> must also satisfy requirements governing its size, its walls, and its roofing. Teaching the remedy for each disqualification would have required lengthy elaboration. With regard to <b>an alleyway,</b> however, <b>whose matters are not numerous,</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> the method of <b>rectification.</b> Once the height is diminished, it is permitted to carry in the alleyway.
§ After clarifying its formulation, the Gemara addresses the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna and asks: <b>From where are these matters,</b> i.e., the <i>halakha</i> that a <i>sukka</i> may not exceed a height of twenty cubits, derived?
<b>Rabba said</b> that it is derived <b>as the verse states: “So that your future generations will know that I caused the children of Israel to reside in <i>sukkot</i></b> when I took them out of the land of Egypt” (Leviticus 23:43). In a <i>sukka</i> <b>up to twenty cubits</b> high, even without a concerted effort, <b>a person is aware that he is residing in a <i>sukka</i>.</b> His eye catches sight of the roofing, evoking the <i>sukka</i> and its associated mitzvot. However, in a <i>sukka</i> that is <b>more than twenty cubits high, a person is not aware that he is residing in a <i>sukka</i> because his eye does not</b> involuntarily <b>catch sight</b> of the roof, as at that height, without a concerted effort one would not notice the roofing.
<b>Rabbi Zeira said</b> that it is derived <b>from here:</b> The verse states: <b>“And there shall be a <i>sukka</i> for shade in the daytime from the heat,</b> and for refuge and cover from storm and from rain” (Isaiah 4:6). In a <i>sukka</i> <b>up to twenty cubits</b> high, <b>a person is sitting in the shade of the <i>sukka</i>,</b> i.e., the shade of the roofing; in a <i>sukka</i> that is <b>more than twenty cubits high, a person is not sitting in the shade of</b> the roofing of <b>the <i>sukka</i> but rather in the shade of the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i>, as their considerable height provides constant shade, rendering the shade of the roofing irrelevant.
<b>Abaye said to him: But if</b> it is <b>so</b> that one is required to sit in the shade of the roofing of the <i>sukka</i>, then in the case of <b>one who makes his <i>sukka</i> in Ashterot Karnayim,</b> which is located between two mountains that prevent sunlight from reaching there, <b>so too,</b> it <b>is not a</b> fit <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> since he is not sitting in the shade of the roofing.
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him:</b> The two cases are not comparable; <b>there,</b> if one theoretically <b>removes</b> the <b>Ashterot Karnayim</b> mountains that obstruct the sunlight, <b>there is</b> still the <b>shade of the</b> roofing of the <b><i>sukka</i>.</b> In that case, the <i>sukka</i> is properly constructed and there are only external factors that affect the sunlight. However, <b>here,</b> in the case of a <i>sukka</i> that is more than twenty cubits high, if one theoretically <b>removes the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i>, <b>there is no shade</b> provided <b>by the</b> roofing of the <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> since throughout the day sunlight will enter the <i>sukka</i> beneath the roofing from where the walls used to be.
<b>Rava said</b> that the <i>halakha</i> is derived <b>from here: “In <i>sukkot</i> shall you reside seven days”</b> (Leviticus 23:42). <b>The Torah said:</b> For <b>the entire seven days, emerge from the permanent residence</b> in which you reside year round <b>and reside in a temporary residence,</b> the <i>sukka</i>. In constructing a <i>sukka</i> <b>up to twenty cubits</b> high, <b>a person</b> can <b>render his residence a temporary residence,</b> as up to that height one can construct a structure that is not sturdy; however, in constructing a <i>sukka</i> <b>above twenty cubits high, one cannot render his residence a temporary residence; rather,</b> he must construct a sturdy <b>permanent residence,</b> which is unfit for use as a <i>sukka</i>.
<b>Abaye said to him: But if</b> that is <b>so,</b> then if <b>he constructed</b> a <i>sukka</i> with <b>steel partitions and placed roofing over them, so too,</b> there, say that <b>it would not be a</b> fit <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> as any <i>sukka</i> constructed as a permanent residence would be unfit. However, there is no opinion that deems a <i>sukka</i> of that sort unfit.
Rava <b>said to him</b> in response that <b>this is what I am saying to you:</b> In a case where one constructs a <i>sukka</i> <b>up to twenty cubits</b> high, a height <b>that a person</b> typically constructs <b>a temporary residence, when he constructs</b> a structure of that height that is sturdy like <b>a permanent residence, he also fulfills</b> his obligation. However, in a case where one constructs a <i>sukka</i> <b>more than twenty cubits high,</b> a height <b>that a person</b> typically constructs <b>a permanent residence,</b> even <b>when he constructs it</b> in a less sturdy fashion <b>like a temporary residence, he does not fulfill his obligation.</b>

Daf 2b

The Gemara explains why each of the Sages cited his own source and did not accept the sources cited by the others. <b>All of them,</b> Rabbi Zeira and Rava, <b>did not say</b> that the fact that a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is unfit is derived from the verse: “So that your future generations will know that I caused the children of Israel to reside in <i>sukkot</i> when I took them out of the land of Egypt” (Leviticus 23:43), <b>as</b> did <b>Rabba,</b> because in their opinion <b>that</b> verse does not mandate one to be aware that he is sitting in a <i>sukka</i>; rather, it mandates <b>knowledge for</b> future <b>generations</b> of the exodus from Egypt.
Similarly, <b>they,</b> Rabba and Rava, <b>also did not say</b> that it is derived from the verse: “And there shall be a <i>sukka</i> for shade in the daytime from the heat” (Isaiah 4:6), <b>as</b> did <b>Rabbi Zeira,</b> because in their opinion <b>it is</b> with regard <b>to the messianic era that this</b> verse <b>is written.</b> It means that God will be a shield and a shelter for the Jewish people; it is not referring to the structure of a <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Zeira,</b> how would he respond to this objection? The Gemara answers that Rabbi Zeira could say: <b>If</b> it is <b>so</b> that the verse is merely a metaphor, <b>let the verse say: And there shall be a canopy for shade in the daytime from the heat,</b> which is the term used in the previous verse. <b>And what is</b> the meaning of: <b>“And there shall be a <i>sukka</i> for shade in the daytime from the heat”? Learn from it two</b> matters: One is the plain meaning of the verse, that God will be a canopy of glory for the Jewish people, and the second is that the essence of a <i>sukka</i> is to have the roofing provide shade.
<b>They,</b> Rabba and Rabbi Zeira, <b>also did not say</b> that it is derived from the verse: “In <i>sukkot</i> shall you reside seven days” (Leviticus 23:42), <b>as did Rava, due to the difficulty</b> raised by <b>Abaye</b> with regard to a <i>sukka</i> with steel partitions. Since there is a weakness in each of the sources, it is understandable why the other Sages did not accept it.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which Rabbi Yoshiya said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> The <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis with regard to the fitness of a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is specifically <b>in</b> a case <b>where the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i> <b>do not reach</b> up to <b>the roofing; however,</b> in a case where <b>the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i> <b>reach</b> up to <b>the roofing,</b> the Rabbis concede that <b>even</b> if the roofing is <b>more than twenty cubits high, it is fit. In accordance with whose</b> opinion is it?
It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabba, who says</b> that the reason that a <i>sukka</i> that high is unfit is <b>because the eye does not</b> automatically <b>catch sight of</b> the roofing. <b>And since the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i> <b>reach the roofing, the eye catches sight</b> of the roofing, as the person will follow the walls all the way up to the roofing despite their considerable height. However, if the roofing is not contiguous with the top of the walls, a person does not notice it without a concerted effort.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> The <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis with regard to the fitness of a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is specifically <b>in</b> a case <b>where there is not</b> an area of <b>four cubits by four cubits in</b> the <i>sukka</i>; <b>however,</b> in a case where <b>there is</b> an area of <b>more than four cubits by four cubits in</b> the <i>sukka</i>, the Rabbis concede that <b>even</b> if the roofing is <b>more than twenty cubits high, it is fit. In accordance with whose</b> opinion is it?
It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Zeira, who says that</b> a <i>sukka</i> that high is unfit <b>due to</b> the <b>shade</b> that is provided by the walls and not by the roofing; <b>and since</b> the <i>sukka</i> in this case <b>is spacious</b> and has a large area, <b>there is shade from the</b> roofing of the <b><i>sukka</i></b> and not only from the walls.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which Rav Ḥanan bar Rabba said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> The <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis with regard to the fitness of a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is specifically <b>in</b> a case <b>where</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>only</b> large <b>enough</b> to <b>hold one’s head, most of his</b> body, <b>and his table,</b> as, if the <i>sukka</i> were smaller, it would not qualify as a <i>sukka</i>; <b>however,</b> in a case where <b>it</b> is sufficiently large to <b>hold more than one’s head, most of his</b> body, <b>and his table, even if it is more than twenty cubits high, it is fit. In accordance with whose</b> opinion is it? It is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of</b> any <b>one</b> of them. This statement cannot be explained according to any of the rationales for the fact that a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is unfit.
With regard to the three aforementioned <i>halakhot</i>, the Gemara notes: <b>Granted,</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Yoshiya differs from</b> the statements <b>of Rav Huna and Rav Ḥanan bar Rabba, as they are providing the measure of the extent</b> of the <i>sukka</i> <b>while he is not providing a measure.</b> In Rabbi Yoshiya’s opinion, the <i>halakha</i> is based on whether the top of the walls reach the roofing, which indicates a fundamentally different understanding of the issue of a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high.
<b>However,</b> in terms of <b>Rav Huna and Rav Ḥanan bar Rabba, let us say that</b> it is <b>with regard to</b> the minimum size required for <b>fitness of a <i>sukka</i></b> that <b>they disagree; as</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rav Huna, <b>holds:</b> The minimum size required for <b>fitness of a <i>sukka</i> is four</b> by four <b>cubits, and</b> the other <b>Sage,</b> Rav Ḥanan bar Rabba, <b>holds:</b> The minimum size required for <b>fitness of a <i>sukka</i> is</b> one <b>that holds one’s head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table.</b>
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>No,</b> there is no need to explain their dispute that way, <b>as</b> it could be explained that <b>everyone,</b> i.e., Rav Huna and Rav Ḥanan bar Rabba, <b>agrees</b> that <b>the</b> minimum size required for <b>fitness of a <i>sukka</i> is</b> one <b>that holds one’s head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table. And here,</b> it is <b>with regard to this</b> that <b>they disagree:</b> One <b>Sage,</b> Rav Ḥanan bar Rabba, <b>holds that</b> Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis <b>disagree</b> only with regard to a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high in a case <b>where</b> it <b>holds the person’s head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table. However,</b> in a case where it is <b>larger than</b> one that holds <b>one’s head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table, everyone agrees</b> that the <i>sukka</i> is fit regardless of its height.
<b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rav Huna, <b>holds</b> that it is with regard to a <i>sukka</i> that ranges in size <b>from</b> one <b>that holds one’s head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table up to</b> one <b>that is four</b> by four <b>cubits</b> that <b>they disagree; however,</b> if the <i>sukka</i> is <b>more than four</b> by four <b>cubits, everyone agrees</b> that it is <b>fit.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A <i>sukka</i> that is more than twenty cubits high is unfit. Rabbi Yehuda deems</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>fit</b> even if it is <b>up to forty or fifty cubits</b> high.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda said:</b> There was <b>an incident involving Queen Helene in Lod where her <i>sukka</i> was more than twenty cubits high, and the Elders were entering and exiting</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>and did not say anything to her</b> about the <i>sukka</i> not being fit. <br>The Rabbis <b>said to him:</b> Is there <b>proof from there? She was,</b> after all, <b>a woman and</b> therefore <b>exempt from the</b> mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i>.</b> Consequently, the fact that her <i>sukka</i> was not fit did not warrant a comment from the Elders. <br>Rabbi Yehuda <b>said to them</b> in response: <b>Didn’t she have seven sons</b> and therefore require a fit <i>sukka</i>? <b>And furthermore, she performed all of her actions only in accordance with</b> the directives of <b>the Sages.</b>
Before analyzing the objection being raised from the <i>baraita</i>, the Gemara seeks to understand its content. <b>Why do I</b> need Rabbi Yehuda <b>to teach: And furthermore, she performed all of her actions only in accordance with</b> the directives of <b>the Sages?</b> His first contention was sufficient.
The Gemara answers that <b>this is what</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>is saying to them: If you say</b> that Helene’s sons <b>were minor sons and minors are exempt from the</b> mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> and that is why the Elders said nothing; <b>since they were seven</b> sons, then <b>it is not possible that there was not</b> at least <b>one among them who no longer needed his mother</b> to look after him. The <i>halakha</i> is that a minor who no longer needs his mother has reached the age of training and is required to fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> by rabbinic law. Even if she gave birth to them in consecutive years, the oldest would be seven years old, and at that age a child does not need his mother to constantly look after him.
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>a child who no longer needs his mother is obligated</b> in the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> only <b>by rabbinic law, and Queen Helene did not observe rabbinic law, come</b> and <b>hear</b> that which Rabbi Yehuda said: <b>And furthermore, she performed all of her actions only in accordance with</b> the directives of <b>the Sages.</b>
The Gemara explores the statements of the <i>amora’im</i> who quoted Rav in light of this <i>baraita</i>. <b>Granted, according to the one,</b> Rabbi Yoshiya, <b>who said that</b> it is specifically <b>in</b> a case <b>where the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i> <b>do not reach</b> up to <b>the roofing</b> that there is a <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis, the <i>baraita</i> can be explained as dealing with a <i>sukka</i> of that type, as <b>it is customary for a queen to reside in a <i>sukka</i> in which the walls do not reach</b> up to <b>the roofing,</b>

Daf 3a

<b>due to</b> the fresh <b>air</b> that circulates through the openings in the wall. <b>However, according to the one who said</b> that it is specifically in the case of <b>a small <i>sukka</i></b> that there is a <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis, in which case the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case where Queen Helene resided in a small <i>sukka</i>, <b>is it customary for a queen to reside in a small <i>sukka</i></b> whose area is less than four cubits squared? <b>Rabba bar Rav Adda said:</b> This ruling <b>is necessary only</b> in the case of <b>a <i>sukka</i> that is constructed</b> with several <b>small rooms [<i>kitoniyyot</i>].</b> The <i>sukka</i> was large, but it was subdivided into many small rooms, each of which was smaller than four square cubits.
Again, the Gemara asks: <b>Is it</b> then <b>customary for a queen to reside in a <i>sukka</i> constructed</b> with several <b>small rooms</b> without leaving a large room in which she could assemble her family and servants? <b>Rav Ashi said:</b> This ruling <b>is necessary only</b> with regard <b>to the compartments in</b> the <i>sukka</i>. It was indeed a large <i>sukka</i> with a large central room; however, there were many small rooms adjacent to the main room. It is with regard to this type of <i>sukka</i> that there is a tannaitic dispute.
<b>The Rabbis hold: Her sons were residing in a full-fledged <i>sukka</i></b> with a large central room, which everyone agrees was fit. <b>However, she</b> often <b>resided in the small rooms due to modesty,</b> to avoid being in the public eye. <b>And for that</b> reason the Elders <b>did not say anything to her,</b> as even if the small rooms were too small relative to the height of the <i>sukka</i>, there was no problem since her sons did not sit in them. <b>And Rabbi Yehuda holds: Her sons would</b> occasionally <b>reside with her</b> in the small room, <b>and even so,</b> the Elders <b>did not say anything to her,</b> indicating that a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is fit even in a small <i>sukka</i>. Now that the <i>Tosefta</i> can be explained according to all the statements cited by the <i>amora’im</i> in the name of Rav, no proof can be cited with regard to the essence of the dispute between Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis with regard to a small <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high.
§ <b>Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak said:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is that one’s <i>sukka</i> <b>must be</b> large enough to <b>hold his head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table. Rabbi Abba said to him,</b> astonished: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did you rule? Was it <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai?</b> This is the subject of a dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel, and according to Beit Hillel it is sufficient for the <i>sukka</i> to be large enough to hold one’s head and most of his body; it need not be large enough to hold his table as well. The halakhic ruling that you issued is in accordance with the opinion of Beit Shammai, despite the fact that in disputes between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel, the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Beit Hillel. Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak <b>said to him: Rather, in accordance with whose</b> opinion should I rule? Yes, my ruling is in accordance with the opinion of Beit Shammai because in this case that is the <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Some say</b> that the exchange between the <i>amora’im</i> was slightly different. <b>Rabbi Abba said: Who stated</b> that opinion <b>to you?</b> Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak <b>said to him: It was Beit Shammai, and</b> nevertheless <b>do not budge from it,</b> as that is the established <i>halakha</i>. Based on either version of the exchange, there is a dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel with regard to the minimum measure of a small <i>sukka</i>.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak strongly objects to this</b> assumption: <b>From where</b> do you conclude <b>that Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel disagree with regard to</b> the minimum measure of <b>a small <i>sukka</i>? Perhaps</b> it is <b>with regard to a large <i>sukka</i> that they disagree, and</b> in a case <b>where one is sitting at the entrance of the <i>sukka</i> and his table is inside the house. As Beit Shammai hold that we issue a decree</b> to prohibit one from sitting that way <b>lest he be drawn after his table</b> while eating, to the extent that his head and most of his body will be inside the house and not inside the <i>sukka</i>. <b>And Beit Hillel hold that we do not</b> issue that <b>decree.</b>
<b>And</b> the language of the mishna <b>is also precise, as it teaches:</b> In the case of <b>one whose head and most of his</b> body <b>were in the <i>sukka</i> and his table was in the house, Beit Shammai deem it unfit and Beit Hillel deem it fit. And if it is so</b> that the dispute is with regard to the minimum measure of the <i>sukka</i>, the formulation of the mishna is missing the essential point. The distinction between a <i>sukka</i> that <b>holds and</b> a <i>sukka</i> that <b>does not hold</b> his head and most of his body is <b>what</b> the mishna <b>needed</b> to say. Since the mishna does not make that distinction, apparently the dispute is not with regard to the minimum measure of a <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara questions this conclusion: <b>And in the case of a small <i>sukka</i> do</b> Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel <b>not disagree? But isn’t it taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: A <i>sukka</i> that <b>holds his head, and most of his</b> body, <b>and his table is fit. Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> It is unfit <b>until it measures</b> at least <b>four cubits by four cubits.</b>
<b>And it is taught in</b> yet <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: Any <i>sukka</i> that does not have</b> an area of at least <b>four cubits by four cubits is unfit. And the Rabbis say: Even if it holds only his head and most of his</b> body, <b>it is fit. However,</b> the term: <b>His table, is not taught</b> in this <i>baraita</i>. If so, these two tannaitic sources <b>contradict each other,</b> as each attributes a different opinion to the Rabbis. <b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from it that this</b> <i>baraita</i>, in which: His table, is taught, <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai,</b> while <b>that</b> <i>baraita</i>, in which: His table, is not taught, <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel?</b> Apparently, they do dispute the minimum measure of a small <i>sukka</i>.
<b>Mar Zutra said: And</b> the language of <b>the mishna is also precise,</b> and it indicates that Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel indeed dispute the minimum measure of a small <i>sukka</i> <b>from</b> the fact <b>that it teaches: Beit Shammai deem it unfit and Beit Hillel deem it fit. And if it is so</b> that the dispute is with regard to the conduct of one sitting at the entrance of a large <i>sukka</i>, then <b>Beit Shammai say: He did not fulfill</b> his obligation, <b>and Beit Hillel say: He fulfilled</b> his obligation, is <b>what</b> the mishna <b>needed</b> to say. However, the terms fit and unfit indicate that the dispute is with regard to the halakhic status of the <i>sukka</i> itself, not the individual’s behavior.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if that is so, the formulation of the mishna: <b>One whose</b> head and most of his body were in the <i>sukka</i>, is <b>difficult,</b> as it indicates that the dispute is with regard to where in the <i>sukka</i> he was sitting. The mishna does not say: A <i>sukka</i> that holds his head and most of his body, which would indicate that the dispute is with regard to the minimum measure of the <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, they disagree with regard to two</b> issues; <b>they disagree with regard to</b> the minimum measure of <b>a small <i>sukka</i>, and they disagree with regard to</b> where one may sit in <b>a large <i>sukka</i>.</b> And the mishna <b>is incomplete and this</b> is what <b>it is teaching: One whose head and most of his</b> body <b>were in the <i>sukka</i> and his table was in the house, Beit Shammai say</b> that <b>he did not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>and Beit Hillel say</b> that <b>he fulfilled</b> his obligation. <b>And</b> with regard to a <i>sukka</i> <b>that holds only his head and most of his</b> body, <b>Beit Shammai deem it unfit and Beit Hillel deem it fit.</b> In this way, the dispute in the mishna is understood as relating to the measure of a small <i>sukka</i> and the manner in which one fulfills his obligation in a large <i>sukka</i>.
§ Apropos the above discussion, the Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught that which the Sages taught:</b> The halakhic status of <b>a house in which there is not</b> an area of <b>four cubits by four cubits</b> is not that of a house? Therefore, <i>halakhot</i> in the Torah or the mishna that are relevant to a house do not apply to a house that size. Consequently, it is <b>exempt from</b> the mitzva of placing a <b><i>mezuza</i></b> on its doorpost; <b>and</b> it is exempt <b>from the</b> obligation of establishing a <b>parapet</b> around its roof; <b>and it does not become ritually impure with leprosy</b> of the house. <b>And</b> its sale is <b>not rendered final</b> in the same manner as the sale of <b>houses within walled cities.</b> The owner of a house in a walled city who sells his house has the option to buy it back from the purchaser within one year of the sale. If he fails to do so, the sale is rendered final and the house does not return to the original owner during the Jubilee Year (see Leviticus 25:29–31).
<b>And one does not return from</b> the ranks <b>of</b> soldiers <b>waging war for</b> a house that size, as would one who built a house with an area greater than four by four cubits (see Deuteronomy 20:5). <b>And one</b> need <b>not join</b> the houses in the courtyards <b>for</b> a house with that area. If there is more than one house in a courtyard, it is prohibited by rabbinic law to carry in that courtyard unless the residents of each of the houses contribute food that is placed in one of the houses, thereby rendering them joint-owners of the courtyard. The resident of a house with an area of less than four by four cubits need not participate in this joining of courtyards. <b>And one</b> need <b>not merge</b> the courtyards that open into an alleyway <b>for</b> a courtyard in which the area of its only house is less than four by four cubits. In this case, too, the resident of that courtyard need not participate in the merging of alleyways. <b>And one does not place the</b> food collected for the aforementioned <b>joining</b> [<b><i>eiruv</i></b>] of courtyards <b>in</b> this house but rather in a house with an area of at least four by four cubits.

Daf 3b

<b>And one does not render it an extension</b> of the city limits when it is located <b>between two cities.</b> Two cities between which there is a distance of more than 141⅓ cubits cannot be joined and considered as a single city for the purpose of measuring the Shabbat limit for one city from the edge of the second city. However if there is a house equidistant between the two cities, i.e., a bit more than seventy cubits from each town, the house joins the two cities together for the purpose of measuring the Shabbat limit. A house in which there is an area of less than four by four cubits cannot serve this function; <b>and brothers and partners do not divide it,</b> as it is too small to be divided.
In answer to the question with regard to the identity of the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i>, the Gemara says: <b>Let us say</b> that the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>is Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>and not the Rabbis,</b> as it is Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi who holds that a <i>sukka</i> with an area of less than four by four cubits is unfit. The Gemara rejects this contention: <b>Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> is <b>the Rabbis, the Rabbis say</b> that a structure with an area smaller than four by four cubits is fit <b>only there, with regard to a <i>sukka</i>, which is a temporary residence,</b> because in a temporary residence one is willing to confine himself to a small area. <b>However, with regard to</b> <i>halakhot</i> relating to <b>a house, which is a permanent residence, even the Rabbis concede that if it has</b> an area of <b>four cubits by four cubits, people reside in it,</b> as it is a functional house, <b>and if not, people do not reside in it,</b> and its legal status is not that of a house at all.
§ The Gemara briefly discusses the <i>halakhot</i> listed in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>The Master said</b> that a house in which there is an area of less than four by four cubits it <b>is exempt from</b> the mitzva of placing a <b><i>mezuza</i></b> on its doorpost, <b>and</b> it is exempt <b>from the</b> obligation of establishing a <b>parapet</b> around its roof, <b>and it does not become ritually impure with leprosy</b> of the house. <b>And</b> its sale is <b>not rendered final</b> in the same manner as the sale of <b>houses within walled cities, and one does not return from</b> the ranks <b>of</b> soldiers <b>waging war for</b> a house that size. <b>What is the reason</b> for these <i>halakhot</i>? It is due to the fact <b>that “house” is written</b> in the Torah <b>with regard to all</b> these <i>halakhot</i>. The legal status of a structure with an area of less than four by four cubits is not that of a house.
<b>And</b> by rabbinic law, <b>one</b> need <b>not join</b> the houses in the courtyards <b>for</b> a house with that area, <b>and one</b> need <b>not merge</b> the courtyards that open into an alleyway <b>for</b> a courtyard in which the area of its only house is less than four by four cubits. <b>And one does not place the</b> food collected for the <b>joining</b> of courtyards <b>in</b> this house. <b>What is the reason</b> for these <i>halakhot</i>? It is due to the fact <b>that it is not fit for residence.</b> The point of the joining of courtyards is to transform the courtyard into a residence shared by the residents of all its member households, and this can be accomplished only by placing the joint food in a place whose legal status is that of a house. The Gemara infers this from the fact that it is taught in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And one does not place</b> the food of <b>the joining of courtyards in</b> this house, <b>but the</b> food of the <b>merging</b> of alleyways, <b>one places in it.</b>
<b>What is the reason</b> for this distinction? It is due to the fact <b>that it is no less</b> a residence <b>than a courtyard in the alleyway.</b> An unroofed courtyard is not fit for residence, and nevertheless the food for the merging of alleyways may be placed there, <b>as we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i> in tractate <i>Eiruvin</i> (85b): <b>The joining of courtyards</b> may be placed <b>in the courtyard</b> and <b>the merging of alleyways</b> may be placed <b>in the alleyway.</b>
<b>And we discussed</b> this <i>halakha</i>: How can <b>the joining of courtyards</b> be placed <b>in the courtyard? Didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: With regard to <b>one who placed his joining</b> of courtyards <b>in a gatehouse</b> or in <b>a portico [<i>akhsadra</i>],</b> a roofed structure without walls or with incomplete walls, <b>or</b> on <b>a balcony,</b> it <b>is not a</b> fit <b><i>eiruv</i>. And one who resides there,</b> in any of these structures, <b>does not render it prohibited</b> for the homeowner and the other residents of the courtyard to carry, even if he did not contribute to the <i>eiruv</i>, as the legal status of these places is not that of a house.
<b>Rather,</b> emend the mishna and <b>say: The joining of courtyards</b> is placed <b>in</b> one of <b>the</b> full-fledged <b>houses that is in the courtyard, and the merging of alleyways</b> is placed even <b>in</b> one of <b>the courtyards that</b> opens <b>into the alleyway. And this</b> house whose area is less than four by four cubits <b>is no less</b> a residence <b>than</b> one of <b>the courtyards that</b> open <b>into the alleyway.</b>
It is taught in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And one does not render it an extension</b> of the city limits when it is located <b>between two cities.</b> The Gemara explains: This means that <b>we do not even render its</b> halakhic status <b>like</b> that of <b>huts [<i>burganin</i>]</b> used by grain watchmen in the fields, which join the two cities between which they are located for the purpose of measuring the Shabbat limit. <b>What is the reason</b> that it is considered less a residence than a watchman’s hut? The Gemara answers: Watchmen’s <b>huts,</b> even though they are not sturdy, <b>are suited for their matters, while this</b> house with an area less than four by four cubits <b>is not suited for its matter,</b> as it is not fit for residence.
It is taught in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And brothers and partners do not divide</b> a house that does not measure at least four by four cubits, as it is too small to be divided. The Gemara infers: <b>The reason</b> that a house that size is not divided <b>is</b> due to the fact <b>that there is not</b> an area of four by <b>four cubits in it; however, if there is</b> an area of four by <b>four cubits in it, they divide it.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna: <b>One divides the courtyard</b> at the request of one of the heirs or partners <b>only</b> if its area is sufficient so <b>that there will be in it</b> four by <b>four cubits for this</b> partner or heir <b>and</b> four by <b>four cubits for that</b> partner or heir? Apparently, in order to divide a courtyard it must be at least four by eight cubits.
<b>Rather,</b> emend the <i>baraita</i> and <b>say</b> that <b>the <i>halakha</i> of division like</b> that of <b>a courtyard</b> does <b>not</b> apply <b>to it. As Rav Huna said: A courtyard is divided according to</b> the number of <b>its entrances.</b> When the residents of the houses in a courtyard divide the courtyard between them, the division is not based on the number of houses in the courtyard, nor is it based on the size of the houses. Instead, it is divided based on the number of entrances that open into the courtyard. <b>Rav Ḥisda said: One gives</b> the homeowner <b>for each and every entrance four cubits, and the rest</b> of the courtyard <b>is divided equally</b> among the residents of the courtyard.
The principle that entrances are factored into the division of a courtyard <b>applies only</b> with regard to <b>a house that stands to endure,</b> as the owner needs use of the yard to ease access to his house, so <b>we provide him</b> with four cubits according to Rav Ḥisda, or part of the <b>courtyard</b> according to Rav Huna. However, in the case of <b>this</b> small house, <b>which stands to be leveled,</b> its owner has no need for the adjacent courtyard, so <b>we do not provide him</b> with any part of the <b>courtyard,</b> as if it were not even there.
§ With regard to the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna that a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high is unfit, the Gemara states: If the <i>sukka</i> <b>was more than twenty cubits high and one comes to diminish</b> its height <b>by</b> placing <b>cushions and blankets</b> on the floor, <b>it is not a decrease</b> of halakhic significance. It does not render the <i>sukka</i> fit, because in that case one is concerned that the bedding will be ruined and therefore does not intend to leave it there very long.

Daf 4a

<b>And even though he nullified them all,</b> intending that for the duration of the Festival the halakhic status of these cushions and blankets is nothing more than that of dirt, it is not deemed a fit nullification <b>because his intention is</b> rendered <b>irrelevant by</b> the opinions of <b>all other people.</b> People do not typically do so, so the action of one who does so is discounted.
If one placed <b>straw</b> on the floor of his <i>sukka</i> in order to diminish its height, <b>and</b> verbally <b>nullified it</b> by saying that he will not use it for another purpose, <b>it is a decrease</b> of halakhic significance, as the halakhic status of adding straw is like that of adding dirt to the <i>sukka</i> floor and diminishing its height. The same is true, <b>all the more so,</b> if he placed <b>dirt</b> on the <i>sukka</i> floor <b>and nullified it.</b>
A case where one placed <b>straw</b> on the <i>sukka</i> floor <b>and he does not intend to evacuate it</b> from there, although he did not nullify it, <b>and</b> a case where one placed <b>undesignated dirt</b> that was not nullified, <b>are</b> the subject of <b>a dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi Yosei and the Rabbis</b> with regard to whether the actions alone are effective as nullification. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna: In <b>a house</b> in which there is a corpse or an olive-bulk of a corpse, the <i>halakha</i> is that if there is a handbreadth of space between the corpse and the roof, the roof serves as a barrier that prevents the ritual impurity from spreading beyond the roof. However, if there is less than a handbreadth of space between the corpse and the roof, the roof does not serve as a barrier, and the ritual impurity spreads upward. In a house of that sort <b>where one filled</b> the space between the corpse and the roof <b>with straw or pebbles</b> mixed with clods of dirt, <b>and</b> then <b>nullified</b> the straw or dirt, <b>it is</b> effectively <b>nullified,</b> and the ritual impurity spreads upward.
By inference, if <b>he</b> explicitly <b>nullified it, yes,</b> it is nullified; if <b>he did not nullify it, no,</b> it is not nullified. <b>And it is taught concerning</b> this mishna in the <i>Tosefta</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> If one placed <b>straw</b> on the <i>sukka</i> floor <b>and he does not intend to evacuate it, its</b> halakhic status <b>is like</b> that of <b>undesignated dirt and</b> it is <b>nullified.</b> If he placed <b>dirt</b> on the <i>sukka</i> floor <b>and he does intend to evacuate it, its</b> halakhic status <b>is like</b> that of <b>undesignated straw, and it is not nullified.</b> Apparently, the <i>tanna’im</i> already discussed this matter.
If a <i>sukka</i> <b>was more than twenty cubits high, but</b> the ends of the <b>palm leaves [<i>hutzin</i>] fall within twenty cubits,</b> then the following distinction applies: <b>If</b> the <b>shade</b> provided solely by the leaves within twenty cubits of the ground <b>is greater than the sunlight</b> in the <i>sukka</i>, <b>it is fit. If not, it is unfit.</b>
The Gemara applies the same principle to the opposite case. In a case where the <i>sukka</i> <b>was</b> only <b>ten handbreadths high,</b> the minimum height for a fit <i>sukka</i>, <b>but</b> the ends of <b>the palm leaves fall within ten</b> handbreadths, <b>Abaye thought to say</b> that the same calculation applies here: <b>If the sunlight</b> in the <i>sukka</i> <b>is greater than</b> the <b>shade</b> provided by the leaves within ten handbreadths of the ground, meaning that those leaves do not constitute a fit <i>sukka</i> on their own, the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b>
<b>Rava said to him:</b> That calculation does not apply in this particular case, as, if the branches fall within ten handbreadths of the ground, <b>that is</b> considered <b>a sagging [<i>seruḥa</i>] residence, and a person does not reside in a sagging residence.</b> Therefore, it cannot even be considered a temporary residence.
If a <i>sukka</i> <b>was more than twenty cubits high and one built a</b> raised <b>platform in it opposite the entire middle wall,</b> as typically a <i>sukka</i> has three walls and the fourth side is open as an entrance, <b>and</b> the platform <b>has</b> an area of at least a bit more than seven by seven handbreadths, the minimum area required for <b>fitness of a <i>sukka</i>,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit.</b> Since the seven-by-seven-handbreadth section from the platform to the roof has three walls and it is less than twenty cubits high, that section is a fit <i>sukka</i> in and of itself, and the rest of the <i>sukka</i> beyond the platform is fit as far as the roofing continues.
<b>And</b> if one built the platform <b>along the side</b> wall of the <i>sukka</i>, then the following distinction applies: <b>If there are four</b> or more <b>cubits from the edge of the platform to the</b> opposite <b>wall,</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit,</b> as the area of the platform has only two walls. However, if the distance to the opposite wall is <b>less than four cubits,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit,</b> as the halakhic status of the roofing that covers the distance to the wall is that of a curved extension of the opposite wall.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>teaching us?</b> Is it <b>that we say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a curved wall</b> applies to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>? <b>We</b> already <b>learned</b> this <i>halakha</i> in a mishna (17a): In the case of <b>a house that was breached</b> by a hole in the middle of the roof, <b>and one roofed over</b> the breach, <b>if from the wall to the roofing there are four</b> or more <b>cubits</b> of the remaining original roof <b>it is</b> an <b>unfit</b> <i>sukka</i>. By inference, if the distance is <b>less than that, it is</b> a <b>fit</b> <i>sukka</i>. That is due to the <i>halakha</i> of a curved wall. The intact portion of the roof is considered an extension of the wall. As this <i>halakha</i> was already taught with regard to <i>sukka</i>, what is novel in the <i>halakha</i> of the platform?
The Gemara explains that there is indeed a novel element to the <i>halakha</i> of the platform. <b>Lest you say</b> that one applies the <i>halakha</i> of a curved wall specifically <b>there,</b> in the case of a house that was breached, as the wall of the house <b>is suited to be the wall</b> of a <i>sukka</i> since it is less than twenty cubits high; <b>however, here,</b> in the case of the platform, <b>where</b> the opposite wall <b>is not suited to be the wall</b> of a <i>sukka</i> due to its excessive height, <b>say no,</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a curved wall does not apply. Therefore, <b>it teaches us</b> that in the case of the platform too, the roof is considered an extension of the wall.
If the <i>sukka</i> <b>was more than twenty cubits high and one built a platform in the center of</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>if there is from the edge of the platform to the wall in each and every direction</b> a distance of <b>four cubits, it is unfit,</b> as the platform has no walls. If the distance is <b>less than four cubits,</b> then it <b>is fit.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>teaching us?</b> Is it <b>that we say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of <b>curved wall</b> applies to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>? Then <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>is</b> identical to <b>that</b> <i>halakha</i>, as we already learned that the <i>halakha</i> of a curved wall applies.
The Gemara explains that there is indeed a novel element to the <i>halakha</i>. <b>Lest you say</b> that <b>we say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a curved wall</b> applies only <b>in one direction,</b> with regard to one wall of the <i>sukka</i>; <b>but in each and every direction</b> with regard to all the walls of the <i>sukka</i>, <b>no,</b> the <i>halakha</i> does not apply; therefore, <b>it teaches us</b> that this <i>halakha</i> may be applied to consider the roof as an extension of all four walls.
If the <i>sukka</i> <b>was less than ten handbreadths</b> high <b>and he dug</b> out an area <b>inside</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>in order to complete</b> the requisite height of the <i>sukka</i> <b>to ten</b> handbreadths, <b>if from the edge of the dug-</b>out <b>area to the wall there is</b> a distance of <b>three handbreadths, it is unfit,</b> as in that case the edge of the dug-out area is not joined to the wall of the <i>sukka</i>. Therefore, even though the interior space is ten handbreadths high, its walls are not the requisite height to be considered a fit <i>sukka</i>.

Daf 4b

If the distance from the edge of the dug-out area to the wall was <b>less than three handbreadths</b> then it is <b>fit,</b> as the edge of the dug-out area is joined to the wall of the <i>sukka</i> based on the principle of <i>lavud</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different there,</b> in the case of a <i>sukka</i> with a platform in its center, <b>that you said</b> that it is a fit <i>sukka</i> if the wall is at a distance of <b>less than four cubits</b> from the edge of the platform, <b>and what is different here that you said</b> the wall must be at a distance of <b>less than three handbreadths</b> for the <i>sukka</i> to be fit?
The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> in the case of the <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high, <b>where there</b> already <b>is a wall,</b> but it is removed from the platform, as long as the wall is at a distance of <b>less than four cubits,</b> it is <b>sufficient</b> to render the <i>sukka</i> fit. <b>Here,</b> where the <i>sukka</i> is less than ten handbreadths high, its wall is not a fit wall. In order <b>to render it a wall</b> by adding the height of the dug-out area, if the distance between them is <b>less than three handbreadths, yes,</b> the dug-out area is considered joined to the wall, as based on the principle of <i>lavud</i> two objects are considered joined if the gap between them is less than three handbreadths; and if <b>not, no,</b> they are not considered joined.
If a <i>sukka</i> <b>was more than twenty cubits high, and one built a pillar in</b> the <i>sukka</i>, far from the walls, <b>that is ten handbreadths high,</b> and the distance from the top of the column to the roofing was less than twenty cubits, <b>and on</b> the horizontal surface of the column <b>there is</b> a bit more than seven by seven handbreadths, the minimum area required for <b>fitness of a <i>sukka</i>, Abaye thought to say</b> that this is a fit <i>sukka</i> because of the principle: <b>Extend and raise the partitions</b> of this pillar. Given that the column is at least ten handbreadths high, its four sides are therefore considered partitions, and the <i>halakha</i> is that the legal status of a partition is as if it extends and continues upwards indefinitely. Based on that perspective, the surface of the column is supported by four partitions at least ten handbreadths high that extend upward indefinitely, and from the top of the pillar to the roof is less than twenty cubits; therefore, this squared column forms a fit <i>sukka</i>.
<b>Rava said to</b> Abaye: That is not so, since in order to have a fit <i>sukka</i> <b>we require conspicuous partitions, and there are none,</b> as the sides of the column do not actually project above the surface.
§ <b>The Sages taught:</b> If <b>one inserted four posts [<i>kundeisin</i>]</b> into the floor <b>and placed roofing over them</b> but no walls, <b>Rabbi Ya’akov deems it</b> a <b>fit</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>and the Rabbis deem it unfit.</b>
<b>Rav Huna said:</b> The <b>dispute</b> between the Rabbis and Rabbi Ya’akov is in a case where the four posts are aligned <b>on the edge of the roof,</b> directly above the exterior walls of a house, <b>as Rabbi Ya’akov holds</b> that <b>we say</b> the principle: <b>Extend and raise the partitions.</b> Since the exterior walls of the house are full-fledged partitions, they are considered as extending upward indefinitely, constituting the walls of the <i>sukka</i>. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>we do not say</b> the principle: <b>Extend and raise the partitions. However,</b> if the posts are placed <b>in the center of the roof,</b> then the walls of the house are irrelevant and <b>everyone agrees</b> that <b>it is</b> an <b>unfit</b> <i>sukka</i>. <b>And Rav Naḥman said: The dispute</b> is in the case of a <i>sukka</i> <b>in the center of the roof,</b> as according to Rabbi Ya’akov, if the posts themselves are one handbreadth wide, they serve as the partitions, while the Rabbis hold that it is not a fit <i>sukka</i> until it has two complete walls and a partial third wall.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Is Rav Naḥman saying that only if the <i>sukka</i> is <b>in the center of the roof</b> there is <b>a dispute</b> between Rabbi Ya’akov and the Rabbis, <b>but</b> if it is <b>at the edge of the roof everyone agrees</b> that <b>it is fit? Or perhaps</b> he is saying that there is a <b>dispute both in this</b> case <b>and in that</b> case? No resolution was found, so the dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from another <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one drove four posts into the ground and placed roofing over them, Rabbi Ya’akov deems it fit and the Rabbis deem it unfit.</b>
<b>But isn’t</b> the legal status of <b>the ground like</b> that of <b>the center of the roof,</b> as it is not surrounded by partitions that extend upward, <b>and</b> nevertheless <b>Rabbi Ya’akov deems it fit?</b> This is <b>a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Huna,</b> who said that everyone agrees that a <i>sukka</i> in the center of the roof is unfit. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, it is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of Rav Huna’s opinion.
<b>And furthermore,</b> there is an additional refutation of the opinion of Rav Huna. It is apparent from this <i>baraita</i> <b>that they disagree</b> with regard to the case of posts inserted <b>in the center</b> of the roof; <b>however,</b> in the case of the posts inserted <b>on the edge of the roof everyone agrees</b> that it is <b>fit. Let us say,</b> then, that <b>this is a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Huna on two</b> counts. First, with regard to his statement that everyone agrees in the case of a <i>sukka</i> in the center of the roof that it is unfit, while the <i>baraita</i> cites a dispute on the matter; second, with regard to his statement that there is a dispute in the case of a <i>sukka</i> on the edge of the roof, while the <i>baraita</i> indicates that everyone agrees that it is fit.
The Gemara rejects this: <b>Rav Huna</b> could have <b>said to you</b> that there is no proof from the <i>baraita</i> with regard to the second matter, as it is possible that <b>they disagree in</b> the case of a <i>sukka</i> in <b>the center of the roof and</b> that <b>the same is true in</b> the case of a <i>sukka</i> <b>on the edge of the roof. And</b> the fact <b>that they</b> specifically <b>dispute</b> the case of a <i>sukka</i> <b>in</b> the <b>center of the roof</b> is <b>to convey to you the far-reaching</b> nature of the opinion <b>of Rabbi Ya’akov, who deems</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>fit even in the center of the roof.</b>
<b>The Sages taught:</b> If <b>one inserted four posts into the ground and placed a roof over them, Rabbi Ya’akov says: One considers</b> whether the posts are wide enough that <b>if they were grooved and split,</b> forming a piece of wood with two segments at a right angle, <b>and they have a handbreadth to here,</b> in this direction, <b>and a handbreadth to there,</b> in that direction, then they <b>are considered a double post [<i>deyumad</i>].</b> With regard to certain <i>halakhot</i>, the status of a double post positioned at a corner is that of two full-fledged partitions. <b>And if not,</b> if after splitting them they are narrower than that, <b>they are not considered a double post, as Rabbi Ya’akov would say:</b> The minimum measure of <b>double posts of a <i>sukka</i></b> to be considered full-fledged partitions is one <b>handbreadth. And the Rabbis say:</b> The <i>sukka</i> is fit <b>only if it has two</b> full-fledged partitions <b>in the standard sense,</b> completely closing each of those two sides, <b>and a third</b> wall, which, based on a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai, measures <b>even a handbreadth.</b>
§ The mishna continues: A <i>sukka</i> <b>that is not</b> even <b>ten handbreadths high</b> is unfit. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>?
<b>It was stated</b> that <b>Rav, and Rabbi Ḥanina, and Rabbi Yoḥanan, and Rav Ḥaviva taught</b> the matter below.
As an aside, the Gemara notes: <b>Throughout the entire order of <i>Mo</i>’<i>ed</i>, wherever this</b> second <b>pair</b> of Sages is mentioned, there are some <i>amora’im</i> who <b>replace Rabbi Yoḥanan and</b> do so by <b>inserting Rabbi Yonatan</b> in his place.
And this is what they taught: The <b>Ark</b> of the Covenant was itself <b>nine</b> handbreadths high, as it is stated explicitly in the Torah that it was one and a half cubits high and the cubit used to measure Temple vessels consisted of six handbreadths. <b>And</b> the <b>Ark cover</b> was one <b>handbreadth</b> thick. <b>There</b> is a total height of <b>ten</b> handbreadths <b>here. And it is written: “I will meet with you there and I will speak with you from above the Ark cover”</b> (Exodus 25:22),

Daf 5a

<b>and it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says: The Divine Presence never</b> actually <b>descended below, and Moses and Elijah never</b> actually <b>ascended to</b> heaven <b>on high, as it is stated: “The heavens are the heavens of the Lord, and the earth He gave to the children of man”</b> (Psalms 115:16), indicating that these are two distinct domains. Apparently, from ten handbreadths upward is considered a separate domain. Consequently, any <i>sukka</i> that is not at least ten handbreadths high is not considered an independent domain and is unfit.
The Gemara asks: <b>And did the Divine Presence never descend below</b> ten handbreadths? <b>But isn’t it written: “And God descended onto Mount Sinai”</b> (Exodus 19:20)? <br>The Gemara answers: Although God descended below, He always remained <b>ten handbreadths above</b> the ground. Since from ten handbreadths and above it is a separate domain, in fact, the Divine Presence never descended to the domain of this world. <br>The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it written: “And on that day His feet will stand on the Mount of Olives”</b> (Zechariah 14:4)? The Gemara answers: Here, too, He will remain <b>ten handbreadths above</b> the ground.
The Gemara asks: <b>And did Moses and Elijah never ascend to</b> the heavens <b>on high? But isn’t it written: “And Moses went up to God”</b> (Exodus 19:3)? <br>The Gemara answers: Nevertheless, he remained <b>below ten</b> handbreadths adjacent to the ground. <br>The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it written: “And Elijah went up by a whirlwind heavenward”</b> (II Kings 2:11)? <br>The Gemara answers: Here, too, it was <b>below ten</b> handbreadths. <br>The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it written: “He grasps the face of the throne, and spreads His cloud upon him”</b> (Job 26:9)? <b>And Rabbi Tanḥum said:</b> This <b>teaches that the Almighty spread of the radiance of His Divine Presence and of His cloud upon him.</b> Apparently, Moses was in the cloud with God. <br>The Gemara answers: Here, too, it was <b>below ten</b> handbreadths.
The Gemara asks: <b>In any case: “He grasps the face of the throne,” is written,</b> indicating that Moses took hold of the Throne of Glory. The Gemara rejects this: <b>The throne was extended for him down to ten</b> handbreadths <b>and</b> Moses <b>grasped it;</b> however, he remained below ten handbreadths. And since the Divine Presence speaks to Moses from above the Ark cover ten handbreadths above the ground, clearly a height of ten handbreadths is a distinct domain.
The Gemara wonders about the proof offered: <b>Granted,</b> the height of the <b>Ark</b> was <b>nine</b> handbreadths, <b>as it is written: “And they shall make an Ark of acacia wood; two cubits and a half shall be its length, and a cubit and a half its breadth, and a cubit and a half its height”</b> (Exodus 25:10), and one and a half cubits equal nine handbreadths. <b>However, from where do we</b> derive the fact that the thickness of the <b>Ark cover</b> was one <b>handbreadth?</b> The Torah never states its dimensions explicitly, <b>as Rabbi Ḥanina taught:</b> For <b>all the vessels that Moses crafted</b> for the Tabernacle, <b>the Torah provided</b> in <b>their</b> regard <b>the dimension of their length, the dimension of their width, and the dimension of their height.</b> However, for the <b>Ark cover,</b> the Torah <b>provided the dimension of its length and the dimension of its width;</b> but the Torah <b>did not provide the dimension of its height.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Go out and learn from the smallest</b> dimension mentioned in connection with any <b>of the</b> Tabernacle <b>vessels, as it is stated</b> with regard to the shewbread table: <b>“And you shall make unto it a border of a handbreadth around”</b> (Exodus 25:25). <b>Just as there,</b> the frame measures one <b>handbreadth, so too, here,</b> the thickness of the Ark cover measures a single <b>handbreadth.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And let us derive</b> the thickness of the Ark cover <b>from</b> the <b>vessels themselves,</b> the smallest of which measures a cubit. The Gemara answers: <b>If you grasped many, you did not grasp anything; if you grasped few, you grasped something.</b> If there are two possible sources from which to derive the dimension of the Ark cover, then without conclusive proof one may not presume that the Torah intended to teach the larger dimension. Rather, the presumption is that the Torah is teaching the smaller dimension, which is included in the larger measure.
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>let us derive</b> the thickness of the Ark cover <b>from the frontplate,</b> which is even smaller than a handbreadth, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The <b>frontplate is a type of plate</b> made <b>of gold that is two fingerbreadths wide and stretches from ear to ear. And written upon it are two lines:</b> The letters <b><i>yod</i>, <i>heh</i>,</b> <i>vav</i>, <i>heh</i>, the name of God, <b>above;</b> and the word <b><i>kodesh</i>,</b> spelled <i>kuf</i>, <i>dalet</i>, <i>shin</i>, followed by the letter <b><i>lamed</i>, below.</b> Together it spelled <i>kodesh laHashem</i>, meaning: Sacred to the Lord, with <i>yod</i>, <i>heh</i>, <i>vav</i>, <i>heh</i> written on the upper line in deference to the name of God. <b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei, said: I saw</b> the frontplate in the emperor’s treasury <b>in Rome,</b> where it was taken together with the other Temple vessels when the Temple was destroyed, <b>and upon it was written: Sacred to the Lord, on one line.</b> Why not derive the thickness of the Ark cover from the frontplate and say that it was only two fingerbreadths?
The Gemara answers: <b>One derives</b> the dimension of <b>a vessel from</b> the dimension of <b>a vessel, and one does not derive</b> the dimension of <b>a vessel from</b> the dimension of <b>an ornament.</b> The frontplate is not one of the Tabernacle vessels but one of the ornaments of the High Priest.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us derive</b> the thickness of the Ark cover <b>from the crown</b> featured atop several of the Tabernacle vessels, <b>as the Master said:</b> This <b>crown,</b> with regard to which the Torah did not specify its dimensions, could be <b>any size.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>One derives</b> the dimension of <b>a vessel from</b> the dimension of <b>a vessel, and one does not derive</b> the dimension of <b>a vessel from</b> the dimension of the <b>finish of a vessel</b> that serves decorative purposes. The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> it is <b>so</b> that one does not derive the dimensions of a vessel from the dimensions of the finish of a vessel, then how can dimensions be derived from <b>the border</b> of the table, which <b>is also</b> the <b>finish of a vessel</b> and not an integral part of the table? The Gemara answers: The <b>border</b> of the table <b>was below,</b> between the legs of the table, and the tabletop rested upon it. As it supports the table, it is an integral part of the table and not merely decoration.
The Gemara asks: <b>This</b> works out <b>well according to the one who said</b> that <b>its border was below</b> the tabletop; <b>however, according to the one who said</b> that <b>its border was above</b> the tabletop, <b>what can be said?</b> According to that opinion, <b>this</b> border <b>is</b> indeed the <b>finish of a vessel.</b>
<b>Rather,</b> the thickness of the Ark cover must be derived from a different source. <b>One derives</b> the missing dimensions of <b>an object for which the Torah provided</b> part of its <b>dimension,</b> e.g., the Ark cover, for which the Torah provided the dimensions of length and width, <b>from an object for which the Torah provided</b> its <b>dimension,</b> e.g., the border of the table. <b>And the frontplate and the crown, for which the Torah did not provide any dimension at all,</b> and their dimensions were determined by the Sages, <b>will not prove</b> anything. It is certainly appropriate to derive the dimension of the thickness of the Ark cover from that which was stated clearly in the Torah.
<b>Rav Huna said</b> that the thickness of the Ark cover is derived <b>from here: “Upon the face of [<i>penei</i>] the Ark cover on the east”</b> (Leviticus 16:14), <b>and there is no face [<i>panim</i>]</b> of a person that measures <b>less than</b> one <b>handbreadth.</b>
The Gemara asks: And why say that the face in the verse is specifically the face of a person? <b>Say</b> that the Ark cover is <b>like the face</b>

Daf 5b

<b>of</b> a bird called <b><i>bar Yokhani</i>,</b> whose face is significantly larger than a handbreadth? The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>If you grasped many, you did not grasp anything; if you grasped few, you grasped something.</b> The Gemara asks: If so, <b>say that it is like the face of a bird, which is extremely small? Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said: Rav Huna derives</b> that the thickness of the Ark cover was one handbreadth not through an actual comparison to the real faces of different creatures but rather by means of a verbal analogy between the terms <b><i>penei</i></b> and <b><i>penei</i></b> written in different places in the Torah. <b>It is written here: “Before [<i>penei</i>] the Ark cover”</b> (Leviticus 16:2), <b>and it is written there: “From the presence of [<i>penei</i>] Isaac his father”</b> (Genesis 27:30). The dimension of the Ark cover is like that of the face of a person, a handbreadth.
The Gemara suggests: <b>And let us derive</b> a verbal analogy <b>from the face of God, as it is written:</b> “For I have seen your face <b>as one sees the face of [<i>penei</i>] God, and you were pleased with me”</b> (Genesis 33:10). The term <i>penei</i> is used with regard to the face of God as well. The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>If you grasped many, you did not grasp anything; if you grasped few, you grasped something.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>And let us derive</b> a verbal analogy <b>from</b> the face of the <b>cherub</b> in the Tabernacle and the Temple, <b>as it is written: “Toward the Ark cover shall be the faces of [<i>penei</i>] the cherubs”</b> (Exodus 25:20), and their faces were presumably smaller than one handbreadth.
<b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said: We have learned</b> through tradition that <b>the faces of the cherubs were not smaller than a handbreadth,</b> and indeed <b>Rav Huna derived</b> the thickness of the Ark cover <b>from here as well,</b> i.e., from the verbal analogy between the instances of the word <i>penei</i> in the verses: “Upon the face of [<i>penei</i>] the Ark cover on the east” and: “The faces [<i>penei</i>] of the cherubs,” indicating that both are the same size.
Apropos the cherubs, the Gemara asks: <b>And what is</b> the form of the face of <b>a cherub [<i>keruv</i>]? Rabbi Abbahu said: Like</b> that of <b>a child [<i>keravya</i>], as in Babylonia one calls a child <i>ravya</i>.</b>
<b>Abaye said to him: But if</b> what you say is <b>so,</b> what is the meaning of <b>that</b> which <b>is written</b> about the faces of the celestial beasts drawing the celestial chariot: <b>“The face of the first was the face of the cherub, and the face of the second was the face of a man”</b> (Ezekiel 10:14)? According to your explanation, <b>this</b> face of the <b>cherub is</b> the same as <b>that</b> face of a <b>man.</b> The Gemara answers: Although two of the celestial beasts drawing that chariot had the face of a man, the difference between them is that one was <b>a large face and</b> one was <b>a small face.</b> In other words, the face described as the face of a man was the face of an adult, and the face described as the face of a cherub was that of a child. This is the source that the Ark and the Ark cover were ten handbreadths high.
However, with regard to the application of this measure to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>, the Gemara asks: <b>And from where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> the <b>interior space</b> of the <i>sukka</i> must be <b>ten</b> handbreadths high <b>without the</b> thickness of the <b>roofing? Say</b> that the ten handbreadths of the <i>sukka</i> are <b>with the</b> thickness of the <b>roofing.</b> Just as the ten handbreadths of the Ark are measured from the bottom of the Ark to the top of the Ark cover, let the <i>sukka</i> be measured to the top of the roofing.
<b>Rather,</b> the dimension of the <i>sukka</i> is not derived from the Ark; one instead <b>derived it from</b> the dimensions of <b>the eternal Temple, as it is written: “And the house which King Solomon built for the Lord, its length was sixty cubits, and its breadth twenty cubits, and its height thirty cubits”</b> (I Kings 6:2). <b>And it is written: “The height of the first cherub was ten cubits, and likewise was the second cherub”</b> (I Kings 6:26). <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Just as we find in the eternal Temple</b> that the <b>cherubs stand</b> reaching <b>one-third</b> the height <b>of the Temple,</b> as each cherub was ten cubits high and the Temple was thirty cubits high, <b>in the Tabernacle as well,</b> the <b>cherubs stand</b> reaching <b>one-third</b> the height of the Tabernacle.
And to calculate: <b>How many</b> cubits high <b>was the Tabernacle?</b> It was <b>ten cubits, as it is written: “Ten cubits shall be the length of a beam”</b> (Exodus 26:16). <b>How many</b> handbreadths <b>do these</b> ten cubits contain? They contain <b>sixty handbreadths. And one third of that</b> total <b>is how many?</b> It is <b>twenty handbreadths. Subtract</b> from this figure <b>ten</b> handbreadths <b>of the Ark and the Ark cover</b> upon which the cherubs stood, and <b>ten</b> handbreadths <b>remain,</b> which was the height of each individual cherub. <b>And it is written: “And the cherubs shall spread out their wings upward, screening [<i>sokhekhim</i>] the Ark cover with their wings”</b> (Exodus 25:20). Here <b>the Merciful One is referring to</b> the wings using the terminology of <b>roofing [<i>sekhakha</i>]</b> specifically <b>when</b> they are <b>ten</b> handbreadths <b>above</b> the Ark cover. This is a source that the roofing of the <i>sukka</i> is placed at least ten handbreadths high.
The Gemara asks: <b>And from where</b> is it known <b>that their wings were</b> spread <b>above their heads,</b> from which it is derived that roofing is ten handbreadths high? <b>Perhaps they were</b> spread <b>level with their heads.</b> In that case, the ten handbreadths derived would include the roofing, leaving the interior space of the <i>sukka</i> less than ten handbreadths high. <b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said</b> that <b>it is written:</b> “Spread out their wings <b>upward,”</b> indicating that the wings were above their heads. The Gemara asks: If so, <b>say</b> that the wings <b>were extremely high</b> to an unspecified height. The Gemara answers: <b>Does the verse say: Upward, upward?</b> It says upward only once, meaning slightly over their heads. There is proof from the verses that the roofing was at least ten handbreadths off the ground.
The Gemara asks: <b>This</b> calculation works out <b>well according to Rabbi Meir, who said</b> that <b>all the cubits</b> in the Tabernacle and the Temple <b>were intermediate</b> cubits, consisting of six handbreadths; <b>however, according to Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that the <b>cubit</b> used in the dimensions <b>of a building</b> in the Temple <b>was</b> a cubit consisting of <b>six handbreadths,</b> but the cubit used in the dimensions <b>of vessels was</b> a cubit consisting of only <b>five</b> handbreadths, <b>what is there to say?</b>
Based on that calculation, <b>how many</b> handbreadths <b>was</b> the height of the <b>Ark and</b> the <b>Ark cover?</b> They totaled <b>eight and a half</b> handbreadths. The height of the Ark was one and a half cubits, which, based on a five-handbreadth cubit, equals seven and a half handbreadths. Including the additional handbreadth of the Ark cover, the total height is eight and a half handbreadths. If the cherubs were one third of the height of the Tabernacle, which is twenty handbreadths, <b>eleven and a half</b> handbreadths <b>remain</b> for the height of the cherubs, over which their wings were spread. Therefore, <b>say</b> that for <b>a <i>sukka</i></b> to be fit for use its interior space must be <b>eleven and a half</b> handbreadths high. However, there is no recorded opinion that requires a <i>sukka</i> with that dimension.
<b>Rather,</b> according <b>to Rabbi Yehuda,</b> the Sages <b>learned</b> the minimum height of a <i>sukka</i> as a <b><i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai. <b>As Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi said</b> that <b>Rav said: The measures</b> in various areas of <i>halakha</i>, e.g., olive-bulk, dried fig-bulk, egg-bulk, <b>and</b> the various <i>halakhot</i> of <b>interpositions</b> that serve as a barrier between one’s body and the water in a ritual bath and invalidate immersions, <b>and</b> the dimensions and nature of halakhic <b>partitions</b> are all <b><i>halakhot</i></b> transmitted <b>to Moses from Sinai.</b> They were not written in the Torah; rather, they were received in the framework of the Oral Law.
The Gemara questions this assertion: Are <b>measures</b> a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai? <b>They are</b> written <b>in the Torah, as it is written: “A land of wheat, and barley, and vines, and figs, and pomegranates, a land of olive oil and honey”</b> (Deuteronomy 8:8), <b>and Rav Ḥanin said: This entire verse is stated for</b> the purpose of teaching <b>measures</b> with regard to different <i>halakhot</i> in the Torah.
<b>Wheat</b> was mentioned as the basis <b>for</b> calculating the time required for one to become ritually impure when entering <b>a house afflicted with leprosy, as we learned</b> in a mishna: With regard to <b>one who enters a house afflicted with leprosy</b> of the house (see Leviticus, chapter 14), <b>and his clothes</b> are draped <b>over his shoulders, and his sandals and his rings are in his hands,</b> both <b>he and they,</b> the clothes, sandals, and rings, <b>immediately</b> become <b>ritually impure.</b>

Daf 6a

However, if <b>he was dressed in his clothes, and his sandals were on his feet, and his rings were on his fingers, he immediately</b> becomes <b>ritually impure, but they,</b> the clothes, sandals, and rings, <b>remain pure until he stays</b> in the house <b>long enough to eat half a loaf</b> of bread. This calculation is based on <b>wheat bread,</b> which takes less time to eat, <b>and not</b> on <b>barley bread,</b> and it relates to one who is <b>reclining and eating</b> it together <b>with relish</b> or a condiment, which hastens the eating. This is a Torah measurement connected specifically to wheat.
<b>Barley</b> is also used as a basis for measurements, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna: <b>A bone</b> from a corpse the size <b>of a</b> grain of <b>barley imparts</b> ritual <b>impurity through contact and by being carried,</b> but <b>it does not impart impurity</b> by means <b>of a tent,</b> i.e., if the bone was inside a house, it does not render all the articles in the house ritually impure.
The halakhic measure determined by <b>a vine is the quantity of a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of wine for a nazirite.</b> A nazirite, for whom it is prohibited to drink wine, is liable to be flogged if he drinks that measure.
<b>Fig</b> alludes to the measure of <b>a dried fig-bulk with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>carrying out</b> on <b>Shabbat.</b> One is liable for carrying food fit for human consumption on Shabbat, provided that he carries a dried fig-bulk of that food.
<b>Pomegranate</b> teaches the following measure, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna: <b>All</b> ritually impure wooden <b>vessels belonging to</b> ordinary <b>homeowners</b> become pure through being broken, as broken vessels cannot contract or maintain ritual impurity. They are considered broken if they have holes <b>the size of pomegranates.</b>
The Sages interpreted: <b>“A land of olive oil and honey,”</b> as: <b>A land, all of whose measures are olive-bulks.</b> The Gemara poses a question: <b>Does it enter your mind</b> that it is a land <b>all of whose measures</b> are olive-bulks? <b>But aren’t there those</b> measures <b>that we</b> just <b>mentioned</b> above, which are not olive-bulks? <b>Rather, say: A land, most of whose measures are olive-bulks,</b> as most measures relating to forbidden foods, e.g., fats, blood, <i>piggul</i>, leftover sacrificial flesh, ritually impure food, and the sciatic nerve, are olive-bulks, as are the measures for a corpse to transmit impurity in a tent and for an animal carcass to transmit impurity through contact.
<b>Honey,</b> i.e., dates from which date honey is extracted, also determines a measure, as with regard <b>to</b> eating on <b>Yom Kippur,</b> one is liable only if he eats <b>a large date-bulk</b> of food.
<b>Apparently,</b> all <b>these</b> halakhic measurements <b>are</b> derived from this verse <b>in the Torah</b> and are not <i>halakhot</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai. The Gemara refutes this argument: <b>And how can you understand</b> it in that manner that all these <b>measures are</b> explicitly <b>written</b> in the Torah with regard to each of the <i>halakhot</i> mentioned above? <b>Rather, they are <i>halakhot</i></b> that were transmitted to Moses from Sinai, <b>and the verse</b> cited <b>is mere support</b> for these <i>halakhot</i>, not a source.
Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi said earlier that Rav said that the <i>halakhot</i> governing <b>interpositions</b> that invalidate ritual immersion are <i>halakhot</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai. The Gemara challenges this assertion: <b>These,</b> too, <b>are</b> written <b>in the Torah, as it is written: “And he shall bathe his flesh in the water”</b> (Leviticus 14:9), and the Sages derived <b>that nothing should interpose between his flesh and the water.</b> Apparently, the <i>halakhot</i> of interposition are derived from a verse in the Torah and not through oral tradition.
The Gemara answers: <b>When the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses <b>comes</b> to teach, it is not with regard to an interposition on one’s skin, which is indeed derived from verses in the Torah. Rather, it comes to teach that an interposition <b>in one’s hair</b> invalidates the immersion, <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabba bar bar Ḥana, as Rabba bar bar Ḥana said: A single hair [<i>nima</i>] tied</b> in a knot <b>interposes</b> and invalidates the immersion. <b>Three</b> hairs tied together in a knot <b>do not interpose,</b> because three hairs cannot be tied so tightly that water cannot penetrate them. With regard to <b>two</b> hairs tied together in a knot, <b>I do not know</b> the <i>halakha</i>. This <i>halakha</i> with regard to hair is a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: The <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>one’s hair is also</b> written <b>in the Torah,</b> as it is taught in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to that <b>which is written: “And he shall bathe [<i>et besaro</i>] his flesh in the water.”</b> The superfluous word <b><i>et</i></b> comes to include <b>that which is subordinate to his flesh, and what is that?</b> That is <b>his hair.</b> The fact that, like the body, there can be no interposition between one’s hair and the water is also derived from a verse.
The Gemara answers: <b>When the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>comes</b> to teach, it is not with regard to an interposition in one’s hair, which is indeed derived from a verse in the Torah. Rather, it comes to teach <b>in accordance with</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Yitzḥak, as Rabbi Yitzḥak said:</b>

Daf 6b

<b>By Torah law,</b> if there is an interposition between a person and the water, and it covers <b>the majority</b> of <b>his</b> body, <b>and he is particular about it</b> and wants the interposing substance removed, only then <b>is it</b> considered <b>an interposition</b> that invalidates immersion in a ritual bath. <b>However, if he is not particular about</b> that substance, <b>it is not</b> considered <b>an interposition.</b> The Sages, however, <b>issued a decree</b> that it is prohibited to immerse with a substance covering <b>the majority</b> of <b>one’s</b> body with regard to <b>which he is not particular, due to</b> substances covering <b>the majority</b> of <b>one’s</b> body with regard to <b>which he is particular.
