And,</b> they issued a decree that it is prohibited to immerse with a substance covering <b>the minority</b> of <b>his</b> body with regard to <b>which one is particular, due to</b> substances covering <b>the majority</b> of <b>his</b> body with regard to <b>which one is particular.</b>
The Gemara raises a question: Then <b>let us also issue a decree</b> deeming substances covering <b>the minority</b> of <b>one’s</b> body with regard to <b>which he is not particular</b> an interposition, <b>due to</b> substances covering <b>the minority</b> of <b>his</b> body with regard to <b>which he is particular, or alternatively, due to</b> substances covering <b>the majority</b> of <b>his</b> body with regard to <b>which he is not particular.</b>
The Gemara answers: We do not issue that decree because the <i>halakha</i> that deems both an interposition covering the minority of his body about which one is particular and an interposition covering the majority of his body about which one is not particular an interposition <b>is itself a decree. Shall we</b> then <b>arise and issue</b> one <b>decree to</b> prevent violation of another <b>decree?</b> In any case, these details with regard to interpositions are neither written nor alluded to in the Torah; rather, they are <i>halakhot</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai.
The <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai that the minimum height for <b>partitions</b> is ten handbreadths <b>is as we stated</b> earlier. The Gemara asks: <b>This</b> works out <b>well</b> according <b>to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who holds that there is no verse in the Torah from which this <i>halakha</i> can be derived, as he therefore concludes that it is a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai. <b>However, according to Rabbi Meir,</b> who holds that all of the cubits in the Temple consist of six handbreadths and therefore the measure of ten handbreadths can be derived from verses in the Torah, <b>what is there to say?</b> What is the <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai with regard to partitions?
<b>When the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses <b>comes</b> to teach, it is with regard <b>to</b> other <i>halakhot</i> concerning partitions, e.g., the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>extending</b> [<b><i>gode</i>],</b> according to which an existing partition is extended upward or downward to complete the requisite measure; <b>and</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>joining [<i>lavud</i>],</b> according to which two solid surfaces are joined if they are separated by a gap of less than three handbreadths; <b>and</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>a curved wall</b> of a <i>sukka</i>. A <i>sukka</i> is fit even if there are up to four cubits of unfit roofing, provided that this roofing is adjacent to one of the walls of the <i>sukka</i>. In that case, the unfit roofing is considered a bent extension of the wall. These concepts are certainly not written in the Torah.
§ Among the factors listed in the mishna that render a <i>sukka</i> unfit is: <b>And</b> one <b>that does not have three walls.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i>: In order to construct a fit <i>sukka</i>, <b>two</b> of the walls must be walls <b>in the standard sense,</b> sealing the entire length and height of the <i>sukka</i>, <b>and the third</b> wall may be <b>even</b> one <b>handbreadth</b> long. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: Three</b> of the walls must be walls <b>in the standard sense, and the fourth</b> wall may be <b>even</b> one <b>handbreadth</b> long.
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what</b> principle do <b>they disagree? The Rabbis hold: The tradition</b> of the manner in which the verses in the Torah are written <b>is authoritative,</b> and one derives <i>halakhot</i> based on the spelling of the words. <b>And Rabbi Shimon holds: The vocalization</b> of the Torah <b>is authoritative,</b> meaning that one derives <i>halakhot</i> based on the pronunciation of the words, although it diverges from the spelling.
With regard to <i>sukka</i>, <b>the Rabbis hold: The tradition</b> of the verses <b>is authoritative,</b> as the word <i>basukkot</i> is written three times in the context of the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>. It is written twice in the verse: <b>“In <i>sukkot</i> [<i>basukkot</i>]</b> shall you reside seven days; all that are home-born in Israel shall reside <b>in <i>sukkot</i> [<i>basukkot</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 23:42). In both of these instances, the word in Hebrew is spelled without a <i>vav</i>, as are Hebrew words in the singular. And one time it is written with a <i>vav</i>, as are Hebrew words in the plural: “So that your future generations will know that I caused the children of Israel to reside <b>in <i>sukkot</i> [<i>basukkot</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 23:43). <b>There is</b> mention <b>here</b> of <i>sukka</i> <b>four</b> times, two singular plus one plural hinted at here in these verses.
<b>Subtract one to</b> teach the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> <b>itself, and three remain.</b> These three remaining <i>sukkot</i> teach that the <i>sukka</i> requires three walls; <b>two</b> of the three are walls <b>in the standard sense,</b> and <b>the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>comes and reduces the</b> dimension of the <b>third and establishes it as</b> one <b>handbreadth.</b> That tradition teaches that one wall need not be any longer than one handbreadth.
On the other hand, <b>Rabbi Shimon holds: The vocalization</b> of the Torah <b>is authoritative.</b> Therefore, although two of the instances are written without a <i>vav</i>, since they are all vocalized in the plural, <b><i>basukkot</i>, <i>basukkot</i>, <i>basukkot</i>, there is</b> mention <b>here</b> of <i>sukka</i> <b>six times</b> in these two verses. <b>Subtract one verse to</b> teach the mitzva of the <i>sukka</i> <b>itself, and</b> two mentions of <i>basukkot</i>, which equal <b>four</b> <i>sukkot</i>, <b>remain</b> and teach that the <i>sukka</i> requires four walls. <b>Three</b> of the walls are walls <b>in the standard sense, and the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>comes and reduces the</b> dimension of the <b>fourth and establishes it as</b> one <b>handbreadth.</b>
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead <b>that everyone agrees</b> that <b>the vocalization</b> of the Torah <b>is authoritative, and here,</b> it is <b>with regard to this that they disagree: One Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds</b> that to derive <b>its roofing requires a verse;</b> therefore, only three of the original six <i>sukkot</i> remain from which walls can be derived. The <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai reduces the dimension of one of the three walls to one handbreadth. <b>And one Sage,</b> Rabbi Shimon, <b>holds</b> that to derive <b>its roofing does not require a verse,</b> as the essence of <i>sukka</i> is its roofing. No additional source beyond the verse from which the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> is derived is required for the roofing. Therefore, walls are derived from four of the six <i>sukkot</i>: Three full-fledged walls and a fourth measuring one handbreadth.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead <b>that everyone agrees</b> that <b>the tradition</b> of the verses <b>is authoritative, and here,</b> it is <b>with regard to this that they disagree: One Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds: When the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses <b>comes</b> to teach, it is <b>to reduce</b> to one handbreadth the dimension of one of the three walls derived from the verses. <b>And one Sage,</b> Rabbi Shimon, <b>holds: When the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses <b>comes</b> to teach, it is <b>to add</b> another wall to the three walls derived from the verses; however, the dimension of that fourth wall may be one handbreadth.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead <b>that everyone agrees</b> that <b>when the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses <b>comes</b> to teach, it is <b>to reduce</b> the dimension of one of the three walls. And everyone agrees that <b>the tradition</b> of the verses <b>is authoritative,</b> and there are four mentions of <i>sukka</i> in the verse. <b>And here</b> it is <b>with regard to</b> whether <b>one derives</b> numbers for halakhic matters from the <b>first</b> mention of a term in the Torah that <b>they disagree.</b> When that total is derived from the number of instances a certain word appears in the Torah, there is a dispute whether the first instance is included in the tally, or whether the first instance is necessary to teach the mitzva itself and the number may be counted only from subsequent mentions. One <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Shimon, <b>holds</b> that <b>one derives</b> numbers from the <b>first</b> mention and therefore four walls derived from the verses. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds</b> that <b>one does not derive</b> numbers from the <b>first</b> mention, and therefore only three walls are derived from the verses.
<b>Rav Mattana said</b> that the <b>rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon</b> is derived <b>from here: “And there shall be a <i>sukka</i> for shade in the daytime from the heat, and for refuge and cover from storm and from rain”</b> (Isaiah 4:6). A <i>sukka</i> without three full-fledged walls does not provide shelter nor serve as refuge.
§ The Gemara asks: According to the opinion that a <i>sukka</i> can be built with two full-fledged walls and a third that is one handbreadth, <b>where does one position that</b> third wall that measures one <b>handbreadth? Rav said: He positions it</b> at the end of one of the standing walls <b>opposite</b> the wall <b>that emerges</b> from the other end of that wall.
<b>Rav Kahana and Rav Asi said to Rav:</b>

Daf 7a

<b>And let him position</b> the wall measuring one handbreadth <b>opposite</b> the wall that emerges like the diagonal <b>line</b> formed by <b>the end</b> of the furrows as the field gradually narrows. This third partition would represent the third side of a triangle and would make the <i>sukka</i> appear more like a full-fledged structure, as the diagonal would represent closure of both unwalled directions. <b>Rav was silent</b> and did not respond.
<b>It was also stated</b> that <b>Shmuel said in the name of Levi: He positions it</b> at the end of one of the standing walls <b>opposite</b> the wall <b>that emerges</b> from the other end of that wall. <b>And similarly, they rule in the study hall: He positions it</b> at the end of one of the standing walls <b>opposite</b> the wall <b>that emerges</b> from the other end of that wall.
<b>Rabbi Simon</b> said, <b>and some say</b> it was <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> who <b>said,</b> that the third wall is positioned differently. <b>He establishes for</b> the third side a wall that measures <b>an expansive handbreadth,</b> measured with the fingers spread apart, which is slightly larger than a standard handbreadth. <b>And he</b> then <b>positions it less than three handbreadths</b> from and <b>adjacent to the wall</b> opposite the second wall. <b>And</b> the legal status of <b>any</b> item positioned <b>less than three</b> handbreadths from and <b>adjacent to the wall is like</b> an item <b>joined</b> to that wall. In this way, the handbreadth-wide wall is joined to the adjacent wall, and it is as if it is a wall of four handbreadths, which is the majority of the minimum measure of the wall of a full-fledged <i>sukka</i>, seven handbreadths.
<b>Rav Yehuda said: A <i>sukka</i> constructed like an alleyway,</b> with two parallel full-fledged walls, is <b>fit, and</b> with regard to <b>that</b> third wall that measures one <b>handbreadth, he positions it</b> adjacent to one of the walls <b>in any direction that he chooses,</b> as it is merely a conspicuous marker.
<b>Rabbi Simon</b> said, <b>and some say</b> it was <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> who <b>said:</b> In the case of a <i>sukka</i> built like an alleyway open on two ends, a third wall measuring a single handbreadth is insufficient. Rather, <b>one establishes for</b> the third side <b>a board</b> with a width of <b>four</b> handbreadths <b>and a bit and positions it less than three</b> handbreadths from and <b>adjacent to</b> either <b>wall,</b> as a wall on either of the open ends. <b>And</b> the legal status of <b>any</b> item positioned <b>less than three</b> handbreadths from and <b>adjacent to the wall is like</b> an item <b>joined</b> to that wall. The result is a full-fledged seven-handbreadth <i>sukka</i> wall.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different there,</b> in the case of two attached walls, <b>where you say</b> that a wall with the dimension of <b>an expansive handbreadth suffices</b> to complete the third wall, <b>and what is different here where you say</b> that <b>it requires a board</b> that measures <b>four</b> handbreadths and a bit? The Gemara answers: <b>There, where there are two walls in the standard sense,</b> as they are attached forming a type of structure, <b>it is sufficient to have</b> the third wall measure <b>an expansive handbreadth</b> in order to render the <i>sukka</i> fit; however, <b>here, where there are not two walls</b> in the standard sense, as they are not attached, <b>if there is a board</b> that measures <b>four</b> handbreadths as the third wall, <b>yes,</b> it is fit, and <b>if not, no,</b> it is unfit.
<b>Rava said: And</b> the <i>sukka</i> consisting of two adjacent walls with a third wall measuring one handbreadth <b>is permitted</b> and fit <b>only</b> if the third wall is <b>in</b> the <b>form of a doorway.</b> One can render the sukka fit only by splitting the one-handbreadth wall and attaching one half to the standing wall and one half across from the other wall that emerges from the standing wall, and then placing a pole across the two halves. By creating the form of a doorway, that third wall becomes like an open gate, which is considered a halakhic partition.
<b>Some say</b> that <b>Rava said: And</b> a <i>sukka</i> consisting of two adjacent walls <b>is also permitted</b> and fit if the third wall is <b>in</b> the <b>form of a doorway.</b> In other words, Rava does not reject the remedy of the expansive-handbreadth wall suggested by Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi; rather, he suggests an alternative.
<b>Some say</b> a third version of that which <b>Rava said: And</b> a <i>sukka</i> consisting of two adjacent walls, even with a third that is an expansive handbreadth wide as suggested by Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, <b>also requires the form of a doorway</b> to be fit. In other words, in addition to Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi’s remedy, one must also create the form of a doorway to render the <i>sukka</i> fit.
The Gemara relates: <b>Rav Ashi found Rav Kahana</b> establishing his <i>sukka</i>, which had two adjacent walls, <b>and establishing</b> a third wall that was <b>an expansive handbreadth</b> wide <b>and establishing the form of a doorway</b> as well. Rav Ashi <b>said to</b> him: <b>And does the Master not hold</b> in accordance <b>with that</b> opinion <b>of Rava, as Rava said: And</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is also permitted</b> and fit if the third wall is <b>in</b> the <b>form of a doorway?</b> Why are you establishing a wall that is an expansive handbreadth wide as well? Rav Kahana <b>said to him: I hold</b> in accordance <b>with the other version</b> of the opinion of <b>Rava, as Rava said: And</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>also requires the form of a doorway,</b> in addition to the expansive handbreadth, to be fit.
§ It is taught in the <i>Tosefta</i> that if the <i>sukka</i> has <b>two walls in the standard sense</b> and a third wall that measures one handbreadth, it is fit. <b>Rava said: And likewise</b> with regard <b>to Shabbat</b> that occurs during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>, <b>since it is</b> considered <b>a wall with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>sukka</i> it is</b> considered <b>a partition with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>Shabbat.</b> Were one to construct a <i>sukka</i> in that manner in a public domain adjacent to the entrance to his house, its legal status would be that of a private domain and one would be permitted to move objects from it to his house and vice versa on Shabbat that occurs during the Festival. However, that structure is not considered a private domain on any other Shabbat.
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> Rava’s opinion from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>And do we say</b> that this principle: <b>Since</b> it is considered, etc., applies in this area of <i>halakha</i>? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The specifications of the <b>wall of a <i>sukka</i></b> are <b>like</b> those of a <b>partition</b> with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> <b>of Shabbat.</b> Just as with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, one forms a partition by establishing adjacent reeds, so too, one forms the wall of a <i>sukka</i> in the same manner, <b>provided that</b> the gap <b>from one reed to another will not be</b> as much as <b>three handbreadths.</b> If the gap is three handbreadths or greater, the legal status of the reeds is that they are not considered joined.
<b>But</b> the stringency of the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>Shabbat</b> goes <b>beyond</b> the stringency of the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> in terms of the criteria for effective partitions, <b>as</b> with regard to <b>Shabbat,</b> carrying <b>is permitted only in</b> a case where the total of <b>the standing</b> segments of the partition, the actual wall, <b>is greater than</b> the total of <b>the breached</b> segments of the partition, the gaps that are less than three handbreadths. <b>That is not so with regard to the <i>sukka</i>,</b> where, even if the breached segments total more than the standing segments, e.g., a <i>sukka</i> consisting of two walls in which there are gaps and a third wall measuring only a single handbreadth, it is still fit.
The Gemara analyzes the <i>baraita</i>. <b>What, isn’t</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaching that</b> the stringency with regard to <b>Shabbat that</b> occurs <b>during</b> the festival of <b><i>Sukkot</i></b> goes <b>beyond</b> the stringency with regard to the rest of the festival of <b><i>Sukkot</i>? And</b> apparently, <b>we do not say</b> the principle: <b>Since</b> it is considered a fit partition for <i>sukka</i> let it also be considered a fit partition for Shabbat. This is difficult according to Rava, in whose opinion that principle is applied in this case.
Rava rejects that interpretation of the <i>baraita</i>. <b>No,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is teaching <b>that</b> the stringency with regard to <b>Shabbat in general</b> goes <b>beyond</b> the stringency with regard to <b>Shabbat that</b> occurs <b>during</b> the festival of <b><i>Sukkot</i>.</b> On Shabbat during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>, a partition where the total of the breached segments of the partition is greater than the total of the standing segments is effective, as, since it is effective as a wall in a <i>sukka</i>, it is effective as a partition for Shabbat as well. That is not the case on Shabbat during the rest of the year, when a partition of that sort is ineffective on Shabbat.
Abaye asked: <b>If</b> it is <b>so</b> that the distinction in the <i>baraita</i> is not a fundamental distinction between the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i> and the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, but is instead a distinction between the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat in general and the specific case of Shabbat during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>, then <b>let</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>also teach</b> a novel distinction involving Shabbat that occurs during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>. The stringency with regard to <b><i>sukka</i> in general</b> during the rest of <i>Sukkot</i> goes <b>beyond</b> the stringency with regard to <b><i>sukka</i> on Shabbat</b> that occurs during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>, <b>as a <i>sukka</i> in general</b> consisting of two parallel walls, like an alleyway, <b>requires</b> that its third wall measure <b>an expansive handbreadth, while a <i>sukka</i> on Shabbat</b> does <b>not require an expansive handbreadth</b> for this purpose, <b>and</b> it is <b>sufficient</b> for the third wall to be established <b>with a side post</b> ten handbreadths high and any width.
Since the side post is effective as a partition with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, it should also be effective as a wall with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i> although it is less than one handbreadth wide, <b>as you are</b> the <b>one who said: If one placed roofing over an alleyway which has a side post</b> on one of the open ends to permit carrying in that alleyway on Shabbat, <b>it is fit</b> as a <i>sukka</i> for that same Shabbat, although it would not be fit during the rest of the week of the Festival.
Rava replied to him: That is indeed my statement; however, the fact is that the <i>baraita</i> does not cite that distinction, because <b>it is not necessary</b> to state that there are circumstances in which the general <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i> are stricter than its <i>halakhot</i> on Shabbat, as there is no novelty in the concept that the <i>halakhot</i> of partitions on Shabbat should apply to a <i>sukka</i>. <b>Now</b> that <b>we say</b> that <i>halakhot</i> may be derived <b>from a leniency to a stringency,</b> as a <i>halakha</i> that applies to <i>sukka</i>, which is a positive mitzva, is applied to the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, which is a stringent prohibition punishable by <i>karet</i>; then <b>from a stringency,</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, <b>to a leniency, all the more so</b> may <i>halakhot</i> be derived. Therefore, there is no reason for the <i>baraita</i> to mention that distinction explicitly.
§ Apropos roofing over an alleyway, the Gemara elaborates about <b>the</b> matter <b>itself. Rava said:</b>

Daf 7b

If <b>one placed roofing over an alleyway that has a side post, it is fit</b> for use as a <i>sukka</i>.
<b>And</b> similarly, <b>Rava said: If one placed roofing over</b> upright <b>boards</b> surrounding <b>wells, it is fit</b> for use as a <i>sukka</i>. A well is usually at least four handbreadths wide and ten handbreadths deep. Therefore, it is considered a private domain, and it is prohibited to draw water from it on Shabbat, as that would constitute a violation of the prohibition to carry from a private domain into a public one. In order to permit drawing water from the well, the surrounding area must be partitioned off and rendered a private domain. For the benefit of Festival pilgrims, the Sages instituted a special leniency that full-fledged partitions need not be constructed around the well for this purpose. Rather, it is sufficient if there are four double posts at the four corners of the area surrounding the well. Since these symbolic barriers are considered partitions for the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, they are considered partitions for the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i> on Shabbat as well.
The Gemara notes: <b>And</b> it is <b>necessary</b> for Rava to state the <i>halakha</i> in each of the two similar cases, <b>as if he</b> had <b>taught us</b> only that the <i>sukka</i> is fit in the case of the <b>alleyway,</b> one could say that it is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that there are two full-fledged walls; however,</b> in the case of upright <b>boards</b> surrounding <b>wells, where there are not two full-fledged walls</b> and most of the area is breached, <b>say no,</b> it is not considered a fit <i>sukka</i>.
<b>And if he</b> had <b>taught us</b> only the case of upright <b>boards</b> surrounding <b>wells,</b> one could say that it is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> in that case <b>it is</b> in the <b>category of</b> a <i>sukka</i> with <b>four,</b> albeit virtual, <b>walls; however,</b> in the case where <b>one placed roofing over an alleyway, where it is not</b> in the <b>category of</b> a <i>sukka</i> with <b>four walls, say no,</b> it is not considered a fit <i>sukka</i>.
<b>And if he</b> had <b>taught us</b> only <b>these two</b> cases, to teach that a partition with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat is a partition with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>, one could say that it is due to the fact that one can derive a <i>halakha</i> <b>from a stringency,</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, <b>to a leniency,</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>; <b>however,</b> to derive a <i>halakha</i> <b>from a leniency to a stringency, say no.</b> Therefore, it is <b>necessary</b> to teach the third <i>halakha</i> with regard to a <i>sukka</i> consisting of two walls in the standard sense and a third wall measuring a handbreadth: Since the third wall is considered a wall with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>, a leniency, it is considered a wall with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat, a stringency.
§ The mishna continues: <b>And</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>whose sunlight,</b> i.e., the sunlight that passes through the roofing, <b>is greater than its shade, is unfit.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that in the statement: <b>Whose sunlight</b> is greater than its shade, the reference is to sunlight that passes through <b>due to</b> sparse <b>roofing, and not</b> to the sunlight entering <b>due to</b> gaps in the <b>walls.</b> It is possible for a <i>sukka</i> to have more sunlight than shade due to sunlight passing through the sides and not the roofing, in which case the <i>sukka</i> is fit. <b>Rabbi Yoshiya says:</b> If the sunlight exceeds the shade the <i>sukka</i> is unfit, <b>even</b> if the sunlight is <b>due to</b> gaps in the <b>walls.</b>
<b>Rav Yeimar bar Shelemya said in the name of Abaye: What is the rationale for</b> the statement of <b>Rabbi Yoshiya?</b> It is <b>as it is written: “And you shall screen [<i>vesakkota</i>] the Ark with the curtain”</b> (Exodus 40:3). <b>The curtain is a partition</b> and not a covering over the Ark, <b>and</b> nevertheless, <b>the Merciful One calls it roofing [<i>sekhakha</i>]. Apparently, we require</b> the purpose of <b>a partition</b> to be <b>similar</b> to the purpose of <b>roofing;</b> just as the roofing must be mostly impermeable by sunlight, so must the partition.
<b>And</b> how do <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Yoshiya, interpret the term: And you shall screen [<i>vesakkota</i>]? <b>That</b> term teaches <b>that we</b> should <b>bend</b> the top of the curtain <b>a bit so that it appears as roofing</b> over the Ark.
<b>Abaye said: Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>and Rabbi Yoshiya, and Rabbi Yehuda, and Rabbi Shimon, and Rabban Gamliel, and Beit Shammai, and Rabbi Eliezer, and <i>Aḥerim</i> all hold</b> that <b>we require</b> the <b><i>sukka</i></b> to be sturdy and fit for dwelling like <b>a permanent residence.</b>
Abaye cites the relevant statements of the <i>tanna’im</i> listed above. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi states this opinion, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: Any <i>sukka</i> that does not have</b> an area of <b>four cubits by four cubits is unfit.</b> These are the dimensions of a permanent residence.
The fact that <b>Rabbi Yoshiya</b> holds that a <i>sukka</i> must be a permanent residence can be seen from <b>that which we stated,</b> that the walls must also be impermeable by sunlight like the walls of a permanent residence.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> also holds that a <i>sukka</i> must be a permanent residence, <b>as we learned</b> in the mishna: <b>A <i>sukka</i> that is more than twenty cubits high is unfit; Rabbi Yehuda deems it fit.</b> As explained above, in constructing a <i>sukka</i> more than twenty cubits high, one cannot render his residence a temporary residence; rather, he must construct a sturdy permanent residence.
<b>Rabbi Shimon</b> agrees, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The dimensions of a <i>sukka</i> are <b>two</b> walls <b>in the standard sense,</b> and <b>a third</b> wall that measures <b>even a handbreadth; Rabbi Shimon says: Three</b> of the walls must be walls <b>in the standard sense, and a fourth</b> wall is required that measures <b>even a handbreadth.</b> Apparently, a <i>sukka</i> must be surrounded on four sides like a permanent residence.
<b>Rabban Gamliel</b> holds that a <i>sukka</i> must be a permanent residence, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who establishes his <i>sukka</i> atop a wagon or atop a boat, Rabban Gamliel deems it unfit;</b> a mobile structure is not a permanent residence. <b>Rabbi Akiva deems it fit.</b> Apparently, Rabban Gamliel requires that a <i>sukka</i> be a permanent residence.
<b>Beit Shammai</b> agree, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna: In the case of <b>one whose head and most of his</b> body <b>were in the <i>sukka</i> and his table was in the house, Beit Shammai deem</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>unfit,</b> since a small <i>sukka</i> is unfit for use and one cannot fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> with it. <b>And Beit Hillel deem it fit.</b> Apparently, Beit Shammai require that the <i>sukka</i> be similar to a permanent structure.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> holds that a <i>sukka</i> must be a permanent residence, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna: In the case of <b>one who establishes his <i>sukka</i> like a type of circular hut</b> whose walls slope down from the center and has no roof, <b>or</b> one <b>who rested</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>against the wall,</b> taking long branches and placing one end on the ground and leaning the other end against the wall, establishing a structure with no roof, <b>Rabbi Eliezer deems it unfit because it does not have a roof, and the Rabbis deem it fit.</b> A permanent residence has a roof.
<b><i>Aḥerim</i></b> agree, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b><i>Aḥerim</i> say: A <i>sukka</i> built</b> in a circular shape <b>like a dovecote is unfit, because it does not have corners,</b> and a permanent residence is one with corners.
§ <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> With regard to <b>a <i>sukka</i></b> that is <b>shaped like a furnace</b> and is completely round, <b>if its circumference has sufficient space for twenty-four people to sit in it, it is fit, and if not, it is unfit.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did Rabbi Yoḥanan rule that the <i>sukka</i> must be so expansive? The Gemara answers: It is undoubtedly <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>who said: Any <i>sukka</i> that does not have</b> an area of <b>four cubits by four cubits is unfit.</b> Since he requires the <i>sukka</i> with the largest minimum dimensions, Rabbi Yoḥanan must hold in accordance with his opinion.
However, even if he holds in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, the question arises: <b>Now, since</b> when <b>a person sits,</b> he occupies one <b>cubit</b> of space, the circumference required by Rabbi Yoḥanan for the <i>sukka</i> is twenty-four cubits. However, mathematically, for <b>every three handbreadths circumference</b> in a circle, <b>there is a diameter of</b> approximately one <b>handbreadth.</b> Consequently, rather than requiring a <i>sukka</i> that holds twenty-four people, a <i>sukka</i> that holds merely <b>twelve people</b> should <b>suffice,</b> since a <i>sukka</i> with a circumference of twelve cubits has a diameter of approximately four. In that case, why does Rabbi Yoḥanan require the <i>sukka</i> to have double the necessary circumference?

Daf 8a

The Gemara answers: <b>This applies only in</b> the middle of <b>the circle</b> that has a circumference of twelve cubits, as the diameter of the circle is four cubits; <b>but in</b> order for a <b>square</b> inscribed within a circle to have a perimeter of sixteen cubits, the circle <b>requires</b> a circumference that is <b>more</b> than twelve cubits.
The Gemara asks further: <b>Now,</b> by <b>how much is</b> the perimeter of <b>a square</b> inscribing a circle <b>greater than the</b> circumference of that <b>circle?</b> It is greater by <b>one quarter</b> of the perimeter of the square. If that is the case, a circle <b>with</b> a circumference of <b>sixteen</b> cubits is <b>sufficient.</b> Why, then, does Rabbi Yoḥanan require a circumference of twenty-four cubits?
The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> statement with regard to the ratio of the perimeter of a square to the circumference of a circle <b>applies to a circle inscribed in a square, but</b> in the case of <b>a square circumscribed by a circle,</b> the circle <b>requires a greater</b> circumference <b>due to the projection of the corners</b> of the square. In order to ensure that a square whose sides are four cubits each fits neatly into a circle, the circumference of the circle must be greater than sixteen cubits.
The Gemara calculates precisely how much greater the circumference must be in order to circumscribe the four-by-four-cubit square. <b>Now,</b> in <b>every square</b> whose sides each measure one <b>cubit</b> its <b>diagonal measures one and two-fifths cubits,</b> and in a circle that circumscribes a square, the diagonal of the square is the diameter of the circle. In this case, the circumscribed square measures four by four cubits; therefore, the diagonal of the square, which is the diameter of the circle, measures five and three-fifths cubits. Since the Gemara calculates the circumference of the circle as three times its diameter, a circular <i>sukka</i> with a circumference of <b>seventeen</b> cubits <b>minus one-fifth</b> of a cubit should be <b>sufficient.</b> The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>was not precise</b> and rounded the dimensions of the circular <i>sukka</i> to a number higher than the absolute minimum.
The Gemara wonders: <b>Say that we say</b> that the Sage was <b>not precise</b> when the difference between the number cited and the precise number is <b>slight;</b> however, when the difference is <b>great, do we say</b> the Sage was <b>not precise?</b> After all, Rabbi Yoḥanan stated that the minimum measure is twenty-four cubits, a difference of more than seven cubits.
<b>Mar Keshisha,</b> i.e., the elder, <b>son of Rav Ḥisda, said to Rav Ashi: Do you hold</b> that when <b>a man sits, he sits</b> and occupies one <b>cubit,</b> and consequently a <i>sukka</i> that seats twenty-four people must have a circumference of twenty-four cubits? In fact, <b>three people sit</b> and occupy <b>two cubits.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>How many</b> cubits <b>are there</b> in the <i>sukka</i> required by Rabbi Yoḥanan? There are <b>sixteen</b> cubits. But <b>we require</b> a <i>sukka</i> with a circumference of <b>seventeen</b> cubits <b>minus one-fifth,</b> as calculated above. The Gemara answers: <b>He was not precise</b> and rounded the figure down to the lower whole number; actually, the required circumference is four-fifths of a cubit larger.
The Gemara rejects this explanation: <b>Say that we say</b> that the Sage was <b>not precise</b> when the result is a <b>stringency,</b> e.g., he required a <i>sukka</i> whose dimensions are greater than the minimum required dimensions; however, when the result is a <b>leniency, do we say</b> the Sage <b>was not precise?</b> In that case, the lack of precision will lead to establishing a <i>sukka</i> whose dimensions are smaller than the minimum requirement.
<b>Rav Asi said to Rav Ashi: Actually, a man sits</b> and occupies <b>one cubit, and Rabbi Yoḥanan is not factoring the space</b> that the <b>men</b> occupy in his calculation. In other words, to this point, the assumption has been that Rabbi Yoḥanan calculated the circumference of the <i>sukka</i> required to seat twenty-four people. Actually, he merely calculated the circumference of the inner circle formed by the twenty-four people seated.
The Gemara asks: <b>How many</b> cubits <b>are there</b> in the circumference of the inner circle formed by a circle of twenty-four people? There are <b>eighteen</b> cubits. Based on the principle that for every three cubits of circumference there is one cubit of diameter, the diameter of a circle whose outer circumference surrounds twenty-four people is eight cubits. To calculate the circumference of the inner circle, subtract from the diameter the space occupied by two people, each sitting at one end of the diameter. The result is a diameter of six cubits. Based on the above principle, a circle with a diameter of six cubits will have a circumference of eighteen cubits. However, a circumference of <b>seventeen</b> cubits <b>minus one-fifth</b> of a cubit should be <b>sufficient.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>This is</b> the case <b>where he was not precise, and</b> in this case <b>he is not precise</b> when the result is a <b>stringency,</b> as instead of sixteen and four-fifths, Rabbi Yoḥanan required eighteen cubits.
<b>The Sages of Caesarea, and some say</b> that it was <b>the judges of Caesarea, said</b> that Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement could be explained using a different calculation: The circumference of <b>a circle inscribed in a square</b> is <b>one</b>-<b>quarter</b> less than the perimeter of the square,

Daf 8b

while the perimeter of <b>a square circumscribed by a circle</b> is smaller than the circumference of that circle by <b>half,</b> i.e., if one adds half the perimeter of the square to the perimeter of the square, that is equal to the circumference of its circumscribing circle. Therefore, a circle with a circumference of twenty-four cubits would circumscribe a square with a perimeter of sixteen cubits, as prescribed by Rabbi Yoḥanan. The Gemara notes: <b>And</b> that <b>is not</b> the case, <b>as we see that</b> the circumference of the circumscribing circle <b>is not that much.</b> The actual circumference is closer to seventeen cubits.
§ <b>Rabbi Levi said in the name of Rabbi Meir:</b> With regard to <b>two craftsmen’s booths, one within the other,</b> as potters would build two booths, an inner one used as living quarters and an outer one for plying their craft and selling their wares, <b>the inner one is not</b> fit for fulfillment of the mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> since the potter resides there year-round and it is not evident during the Festival that he is residing there for the sake of the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>. <b>And</b> since it a permanent residence, <b>it is</b> also <b>obligated in</b> the mitzva of <b><i>mezuza</i>. And the outer</b> booth <b>is</b> fit for fulfillment of the mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> since he does not reside there year-round, and when he resides there during the Festival it is evident that he is doing so for the sake of the mitzva. Since it is not designated as a year-round residence, but rather serves as an entrance to his residence and a passage for merchants and merchandise, it is not considered a residence <b>and is not obligated in</b> the mitzva of <b><i>mezuza</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is the outer booth exempt from the mitzva of <i>mezuza</i>? <b>Let the outer</b> booth <b>be</b> considered <b>like a gatehouse of the inner</b> booth <b>and</b> therefore <b>be obligated in</b> the mitzva of <b><i>mezuza</i>.</b> The Gemara answers: It is exempt <b>because</b> even the inner booth <b>is not</b> a <b>permanent</b> residence. It requires a <i>mezuza</i> because the potter resides there year-round; however, that alone does not render it a full-fledged residence that would obligate one to affix a <i>mezuza</i> to the outer booth as its gatehouse.
<b>The Sages taught:</b> The booths represented by the mnemonic: <b><i>Gimmel</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>kaf</i>,</b> which stands for <b>a booth of gentiles [<i>goyim</i>], a booth of women [<i>nashim</i>], a booth of domesticated animals [<i>behema</i>], a booth of Samaritans [<i>Kutim</i>], a booth of any sort,</b> each is <b>fit</b> for use as a <i>sukka</i>, <b>provided it is roofed in the standard sense.</b> None of them is disqualified due to the one who constructed it or the purpose for which it was constructed.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the term: <b>In the standard sense? Rav Ḥisda said</b> that it means: <b>And</b> provided <b>that one established</b> the booth <b>to</b> provide <b>shade of a <i>sukka</i></b> from its roofing, it may be used to fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>What does</b> the phrase: A booth <b>of any sort,</b> come <b>to include?</b> What other booths are included in this generalization? The Gemara answers: It <b>comes to include</b> the <b>booths</b> listed in another <i>baraita</i> with the mnemonic: <b><i>Reish</i>, <i>kuf</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>shin</i>, as the Sages taught: The booth</b> known by the mnemonic <b><i>reish</i>, <i>kuf</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>shin</i>,</b> which stands for <b>the booth of shepherds [<i>ro’im</i>], the booth of fig driers [<i>kayyatzim</i>], the booth of guards of fields [<i>burganin</i>], the booth of the guards of produce [<i>shomerei peirot</i>], a booth of any sort,</b> each is <b>fit, provided it is roofed in the standard sense.</b>
The Gemara asks again: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the term: <b>In the standard sense? Rav Ḥisda said</b> that it means: <b>And</b> provided <b>that one established</b> the booth <b>to</b> provide <b>shade of a <i>sukka</i>,</b> it may be used to fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>What does</b> the phrase: A booth <b>of any sort, come to include?</b> The Gemara answers: It <b>comes to include</b> the <b>booths</b> listed in the first <i>baraita</i> cited above with the mnemonic <b><i>gimmel</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>kaf</i>.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>This <i>tanna</i> who</b> taught and detailed the <i>halakhot</i> of booths <b>of <i>gimmel</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>kaf</i></b> did so because the fitness of the booths of <b><i>gimmel</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>kaf</i></b> for use in fulfilling the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> is <b>powerful</b> and more obvious <b>to him because they are permanent</b> structures, even though their builders are not obligated in the mitzva. <b>And he taught:</b> Booths of <b>any sort, to include</b> the booths of <b><i>reish</i>, <i>kuf</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>shin</i>, which,</b> although they <b>are</b> seasonal and <b>not permanent</b> structures, may still be used to fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>.
<b>And that</b> other <b><i>tanna</i></b> who taught and detailed the <i>halakhot</i> of booths <b>of <i>reish</i>, <i>kuf</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>shin</i></b> did so because the fitness of the booths of <b><i>reish</i>, <i>kuf</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>shin</i></b> for use in fulfilling the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> is <b>powerful</b> and more obvious <b>to him</b> because those who constructed the booths are <b>obligated</b> in the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>. <b>And he taught:</b> Booths of <b>any sort, to include</b> the booths of <b><i>gimmel</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>kaf</i>, which,</b> although those who constructed them are <b>not obligated</b> in the mitzva, may still be used to fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>.

Daf 9a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>an old <i>sukka</i>, Beit Shammai deem it unfit</b> for the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> <b>and Beit Hillel deem it fit. And which is</b> considered <b>an old <i>sukka</i>?</b> It is <b>any</b> booth <b>that one established thirty days</b> or more <b>prior to the Festival</b> without expressly designating that it was for the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>. In that case, the assumption is that he constructed it for some other purpose. <b>However, if he established it</b> expressly <b>for the sake of the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i>, <b>even</b> if he constructed it <b>at the beginning of the</b> previous <b>year, it is fit</b> for use in the fulfillment of the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>, even according to Beit Shammai.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of Beit Shammai?</b> The Gemara explains that it is as <b>the verse states: “The festival of <i>Sukkot</i> is seven days unto the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 23:34), indicating that <b>we require a <i>sukka</i> established for the sake of the Festival.</b> A <i>sukka</i> not constructed expressly for the Festival is unfit.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> how do <b>Beit Hillel</b> interpret this verse? The Gemara answers: In Beit Hillel’s opinion, <b>that</b> verse <b>is necessary to</b> teach <b>in accordance with</b> the statement <b>of Rav Sheshet, as Rav Sheshet said in the name of Rabbi Akiva: From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> use of <b>the wood of the <i>sukka</i> is prohibited</b> for any purpose other than for the <i>sukka</i> <b>all seven</b> days of the Festival, and it is designated exclusively for the mitzva? It is derived as <b>the verse states: “The festival of <i>Sukkot</i> is seven days unto the Lord.”</b>
<b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> in explanation that <b>Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira says: Just as the name of Heaven takes effect upon the Festival peace-offering, so too, the name of Heaven takes effect upon the <i>sukka</i>, as it is stated: “The festival of <i>Sukkot</i> is seven days unto the Lord”; just as the Festival</b> offering <b>is</b> consecrated <b>to the Lord, so too, the <i>sukka</i> is</b> consecrated <b>to the Lord.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> don’t <b>Beit Shammai require</b> the verse <b>to</b> derive <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>as well?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, indeed it is so</b> that Beit Shammai derives the sanctity of the wood of the <i>sukka</i> from this verse. Therefore, the rationale for their opinion with regard to an old <i>sukka</i> must be based on a different verse.
<b>Rather, what is the rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of Beit Shammai</b> with regard to an old <i>sukka</i>? <b>Another verse is written: “You shall prepare for you the festival of <i>Sukkot</i> for seven days”</b> (Deuteronomy 16:13), from which it is derived that <b>we require a <i>sukka</i> established for the sake of the Festival.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> how do <b>Beit Hillel</b> interpret this verse? The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> verse <b>is necessary to</b> teach <b>that one</b> may <b>establish a <i>sukka</i></b> even <b>during the intermediate days of the Festival.</b> If one failed to construct a <i>sukka</i> prior to the onset of the Festival, or if it collapsed during the Festival, he may establish it during the intermediate days, as the mitzva to establish a <i>sukka</i> is in effect for all seven days of the Festival.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> from where do <b>Beit Shammai</b> derive this <i>halakha</i>? <b>They hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who said: One</b> may <b>not establish a <i>sukka</i> during the intermediate days of the Festival.</b> Therefore, the requirement to build the <i>sukka</i> for the sake of the mitzva may be derived from this verse.
The Gemara proceeds to clarify Beit Hillel’s opinion: <b>And</b> do <b>Beit Hillel not</b> agree with the statement <b>that Rav Yehuda</b> said that <b>Rav said? As Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> If <b>one fashioned</b> ritual fringes <b>from</b> hanging threads that remain protruding from the fabric like <b>thorns</b> after most of the superfluous threads were torn, and tied them into ritual fringes; <b>or</b> if he tied the fringes <b>from threads</b> that hang down after sewing; <b>or</b> if he tied them <b>from the fringes [<i>geradin</i>]</b> that hang from the bottom of a garment, the ritual fringes are <b>unfit</b> for fulfilling the mitzva. However, if the ritual fringes were tied <b>from balls of thread</b> that were not spun for the sake of the mitzva, they are <b>fit.</b>
And Rav Yehuda related: <b>When I stated this</b> <i>halakha</i> in the name of Rav <b>before Shmuel, he said to me: Even</b> ritual fringes tied <b>from balls of thread</b> are <b>unfit, as we require the spinning</b> of the thread to be <b>for the sake of</b> the mitzva. Just as the threads for the ritual fringes must be spun for the sake of the mitzva, <b>here too, let us require a <i>sukka</i> established for the sake of</b> the mitzva.
The Gemara answers: It is <b>different there, as the verse states: “You shall prepare for you fringes”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:12), from which it is derived: <b>“For you,” for the sake of your obligation.</b> The fringes, from the beginning of their production, must be produced for the sake of the mitzva. The Gemara asks: <b>Here, too,</b> with regard to <i>sukka</i>, the verse says: <b>“You shall prepare for you the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>”</b> (Deuteronomy 16:13). Shouldn’t it be derived: <b>“For you,” for the sake of your obligation?</b>
The Gemara answers that <b>this</b> term “for you” <b>is required to exclude</b> use of a <b>stolen</b> <i>sukka</i>; establish the <i>sukka</i> for you, and do not use a <i>sukka</i> belonging to another. The Gemara asks: <b>There, too,</b> with regard to ritual fringes, <b>isn’t</b> the term “for you” <b>required to exclude</b> use of <b>stolen</b> ritual fringes?
The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> with regard to ritual fringes, <b>another verse is written: “And they shall make for them</b> ritual fringes” (Numbers 15:38), from which it is derived: “For them,” <b>of their own,</b> to exclude the use of stolen ritual fringes. Therefore from the term “for you,” it may be derived that ritual fringes must be produced for the sake of the mitzva.

Daf 9b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>one who establishes his <i>sukka</i> beneath a tree,</b> it is <b>as though he established it inside the house</b> and it is unfit. If one established <b>a <i>sukka</i> atop</b> another <b><i>sukka</i>, the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit and the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit. Rabbi Yehuda says: If there are no residents in the upper</b> <i>sukka</i>, <b>the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rava said: They taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> that a <i>sukka</i> beneath a tree is unfit <b>only with regard to a tree whose shade is greater than its sunlight,</b> as the source of the shade in the <i>sukka</i> is the tree and not the roofing. <b>However, if its sunlight is greater than its shade,</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit,</b> as in that case the roofing provides the shade.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> does Rava reach this conclusion? The Gemara answers: He learns this <b>from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches:</b> It is <b>as though he established it inside the house. Why do I</b> need the mishna <b>to teach:</b> It is <b>as though he established it inside the house? Let</b> the mishna <b>teach</b> simply: It is <b>unfit. Rather, this is teaching us that</b> in the context of this <i>halakha</i>, <b>a tree is similar to a house; just as</b> with regard to <b>a house, its shade is greater than its sunlight, so too,</b> with regard to <b>a tree,</b> it invalidates the <i>sukka</i> only if <b>its shade is greater than its sunlight.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> even <b>if the sunlight is greater than the shade</b> of the tree, <b>what of it?</b> Why does Rava deem the <i>sukka</i> beneath the tree fit in that case? <b>Isn’t</b> there <b>unfit roofing,</b> the uncut branches of the tree, <b>joining together with</b> the <b>fit roofing</b> on the <i>sukka</i>, rendering even the fit roofing on the <i>sukka</i> unfit? <b>Rav Pappa said:</b> This is referring to a case <b>where one lowered</b> the uncut branches and combined them with the fit roofing so that the branches still attached to the tree are inconspicuous. Given that the majority of the roofing is fit, the roofing in its entirety is fit.
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> it is a case <b>where he lowered them, what is</b> the purpose of <b>stating</b> this <i>halakha</i>? Isn’t it self-evident? The Gemara answers that it is necessary <b>lest you say: Let us issue a decree</b> and deem the roofing unfit in a case <b>where one lowered them due to</b> a case <b>where one did not lower them.</b> Therefore, <b>it teaches us that we do not issue</b> such <b>a decree.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>That</b> <i>halakha</i>, <b>too, we</b> already <b>learned</b> in a mishna: <b>If one trellised the grapevine, the gourd, or the ivy,</b> climbing plants, over a <i>sukka</i> while they are still attached to the ground, <b>and</b> he then added <b>roofing atop them,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit,</b> as roofing attached to the ground is unfit. <b>If</b> the amount of fit <b>roofing was greater than</b> the plants attached to the ground, <b>or if he cut</b> the climbing plants so that they were no longer attached to the ground, <b>it is fit.</b>
The Gemara clarifies the details of the mishna: <b>What are the circumstances? If we say</b> that it is referring to a case where <b>he did not lower</b> the climbing plants and combine them with the fit roofing, <b>doesn’t</b> the <b>unfit roofing join together with</b> the <b>fit roofing</b> on the <i>sukka</i>, rendering even the fit roofing on the <i>sukka</i> unfit? <b>Rather, isn’t</b> the mishna referring to a case <b>where he lowered them, and conclude from this</b> mishna <b>that we do not issue a decree</b> in a case where he lowered the branches due to a case where he did not lower the branches. Rava’s statement is therefore unnecessary. The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say that this applies</b> only <b>after the fact,</b> i.e., that if one already lowered the uncut branches or plants it is not unfit, <b>but</b> one may <b>not</b> do so <b><i>ab initio</i>;</b> therefore, Rava <b>teaches us</b> that one may place roofing in this manner even <i>ab initio</i>.
§ The mishna continues: If one established <b>a <i>sukka</i> atop</b> another <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> the upper <i>sukka</i> is fit and the lower <i>sukka</i> is unfit. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states: <b>“In <i>sukkot</i> shall you reside”</b> (Leviticus 23:42), <b>and not in a <i>sukka</i> that is beneath</b> another <b><i>sukka</i>, and not in <i>sukka</i> that is beneath a tree, and not in a <i>sukka</i> that is inside a house.</b>
The Gemara questions that derivation. <b>On the contrary,</b> the term <b>“in <i>sukkot</i>,”</b> which is written in the plural, <b>indicates two.</b> The conclusion should be that one sitting inside a <i>sukka</i> beneath a <i>sukka</i> fulfills the mitzva. <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said:</b> Although the term is vocalized in the plural, <b><i>basukkot</i> is written</b> without the <i>vav</i>, indicating a single <i>sukka</i>.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> There are <b>times when both of</b> the <i>sukkot</i> one atop the other <b>are fit;</b> there are <b>times when both of</b> the <i>sukkot</i> <b>are unfit;</b> there are <b>times when the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit and the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit;</b> and there are <b>times when the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit and the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: There are <b>times when both of</b> the <i>sukkot</i> one atop the other <b>are fit. What are the circumstances?</b> It is in a case <b>where</b> in the <b>lower <i>sukka</i> its sunlight is greater than its shade,</b> rendering the <i>sukka</i> unfit, <b>and</b> in <b>the upper <i>sukka</i> its shade is greater than its sunlight,</b> rendering the <i>sukka</i> fit. <b>And</b> the roofing of <b>the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is within twenty</b> cubits of the ground. In that case, the roofing of the upper <i>sukka</i> is effective for both the upper <i>sukka</i> and the lower one.
There are <b>times when both of</b> the <i>sukkot</i> <b>are unfit. What are the circumstances?</b> It is in a case <b>where</b> in <b>both</b> <i>sukkot</i>, <b>their shade is greater than their sunlight, but the upper one is</b> more than <b>twenty cubits above</b> the roofing of the lower <i>sukka</i>, rendering it unfit. Since the roofing of the upper <i>sukka</i> is unfit, and it casts shade over the lower <i>sukka</i>, the lower <i>sukka</i> is also unfit.
There are <b>times when the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit and the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit.</b>

Daf 10a

<b>What are the circumstances?</b> It is in a case <b>where</b> in <b>the lower</b> <i>sukka</i>, <b>its shade is greater than its sunlight,</b> rendering the <i>sukka</i> fit, <b>and</b> in <b>the upper</b> <i>sukka</i>, <b>its sunlight is greater than its shade</b> and it is therefore insignificant, <b>and</b> the roofing of <b>both is within twenty</b> cubits of the ground.
<b>And</b> there are <b>times when the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit and</b> the <b>lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit. What are the circumstances?</b> It is in a case <b>where</b> in <b>both</b> <i>sukkot</i> <b>their shade is greater than their sunlight, and</b> the roofing of the <b>upper</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is within twenty</b> cubits of the roofing of the lower one. In this case the upper <i>sukka</i> is fit, while the lower <i>sukka</i> is a <i>sukka</i> beneath a <i>sukka</i> and is unfit.
The Gemara asks: This is <b>obvious.</b> There is nothing novel in any of these scenarios. The Gemara answers: <b>It was necessary for</b> the <i>tanna</i> to mention the case where <b>the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit and the upper</b> <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit,</b> as it contains a novel element. <b>Lest you say: Let us issue a decree</b> and deem the lower <i>sukka</i> unfit, as <b>perhaps</b> the <b>unfit roofing</b> of the upper <i>sukka</i> <b>joins together with the fit roofing</b> of the lower <i>sukka</i> and renders it unfit as well; therefore, the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches us</b> that the two roofings do not join together and the upper roofing does not render the lower <i>sukka</i> unfit.
The Gemara elucidates this <i>halakha</i>. <b>How much</b> space <b>shall there be between</b> the roofing of the upper <b><i>sukka</i> and</b> the roofing of the lower <b><i>sukka</i> for the lower <i>sukka</i></b> to <b>be</b> considered a discrete entity and therefore <b>disqualified</b> as a <i>sukka</i> beneath a <i>sukka</i>?
<b>Rav Huna said:</b> There must be <b>a handbreadth</b> of space, <b>as we likewise find in tents of ritual impurity</b> the measure of a <b>handbreadth.</b> With regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual impurity imparted by a corpse, the legal status of the space of one handbreadth beneath a roof is that of a tent, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna: A space measuring one <b>handbreadth by</b> one <b>handbreadth with a height of</b> one <b>handbreadth transmits ritual impurity.</b> If a source of ritual impurity imparted by a corpse is in that space, the impurity is transmitted to all people, vessels, and food in that space. <b>And</b> a space that size <b>serves as a barrier before the</b> spread of <b>ritual impurity</b> beyond that space. <b>However,</b> if the space measures <b>less than the height of</b> one <b>handbreadth, it does not transmit</b> impurity to the objects in that space, <b>and it does not serve as a barrier</b> before the spread of ritual impurity. The impurity breaches the confining walls and rises upward as if there were no covering over it.
<b>Rav Ḥisda and Rabba bar Rav Huna say:</b> For this to be considered a <i>sukka</i> beneath a <i>sukka</i>, the space between the roofing of the upper <i>sukka</i> and that of the lower one must measure at least <b>four</b> handbreadths, <b>as we do not find a significant area</b> that measures <b>less than four</b> handbreadths, e.g., with regard to the domains of Shabbat.
<b>And Shmuel said:</b> The space between the roofing of the upper <i>sukka</i> and that of the lower one must measure at least <b>ten</b> handbreadths. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of Shmuel?</b> The Gemara explains: <b>As</b> the criterion for <b>its fitness, so too</b> is the criterion for <b>its unfitness; just as its fitness</b> is only <b>in</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>ten</b> handbreadths high, <b>so too,</b> its <b>unfitness</b> as a <i>sukka</i> is engendered only <b>by</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>ten</b> handbreadths high.
The Gemara questions Shmuel’s statement: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: If there are no residents in the upper</b> <i>sukka</i>, <b>the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b>
The Gemara clarifies: <b>What is</b> the meaning of: <b>There are no residents? If we say</b> that it means that there are no <b>actual residents,</b> the question arises: <b>Is that to say that residents cause</b> it to be unfit? If the upper <i>sukka</i> is a fit <i>sukka</i>, is there any difference whether or not people reside there? <b>Rather, what is</b> the meaning of: <b>There are no residents? Is it not</b> referring to <b>any</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>that is not suitable to</b> serve as <b>a residence? And what are the circumstances</b> of that case? It is a case <b>where</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is not ten</b> handbreadths <b>high,</b> as anything less than ten handbreadths high is not considered a residence. From the fact that it is Rabbi Yehuda who distinguishes between whether or not the upper <i>sukka</i> is at least ten handbreadths high, conclude <b>by inference that the first <i>tanna</i></b> of the mishna <b>holds</b> that the lower <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit even if</b> the upper <i>sukka</i> <b>is</b> less than ten handbreadths high and therefore <b>not suitable to</b> serve as <b>a residence.</b> This is contrary to the opinion of Shmuel.
<b>When Rav Dimi came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, <b>he said</b> that the Sages <b>say in the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, in explanation of the mishna: <b>If</b> the roofing of <b>the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is not</b> sufficiently sturdy to be <b>able to support the cushions and blankets of the upper</b> <i>sukka</i>, then <b>the lower</b> <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit,</b> as the upper <i>sukka</i> is not suitable to serve as an independent residence. According to this explanation, the mishna does not discuss the height of the upper <i>sukka</i>; it discusses the quality of the roofing.
The Gemara notes: Is that to say <b>by inference that the first <i>tanna</i> holds</b> that <b>even though</b> the roofing of the lower <i>sukka</i> <b>is not</b> sufficiently sturdy to be <b>able to support</b> the cushions and blankets of the upper <i>sukka</i>, the lower <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit?</b> In that case, the upper <i>sukka</i> is not a suitable residence. Why should the lower <i>sukka</i> be unfit?
The Gemara answers: The first <i>tanna</i> agrees that if the roofing of the lower <i>sukka</i> is unable to support the cushions and the blankets at all, the upper <i>sukka</i> is not considered a <i>sukka</i> and the lower <i>sukka</i> is fit. However, <b>there is</b> a practical difference <b>between</b> the opinions of the first <i>tanna</i> and Rabbi Yehuda in a case where the roofing of the lower <i>sukka</i> <b>is able to support</b> the cushions and the blankets of the upper <i>sukka</i> <b>with difficulty</b> and there is a concern that the roofing might collapse. In that case, the first <i>tanna</i> holds that since the roofing is capable of supporting the cushions and blankets, the upper <i>sukka</i> is considered a separate <i>sukka</i> and renders the lower <i>sukka</i> unfit. According to Rabbi Yehuda, since the roofing is able to support the weight of the cushions and blankets only with difficulty, the upper <i>sukka</i> is not fit. Therefore, the lower <i>sukka</i> is fit.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>one spread a sheet over</b> the roofing as protection for those sitting in the <i>sukka</i> <b>due to the sun, or</b> if one spread a sheet <b>beneath</b> the roofing as protection <b>due to the falling leaves, or</b> if <b>one spread</b> a sheet as a canopy <b>over the</b> frame of <b>a four-post [<i>kinof</i>]</b> bed, the area in the <i>sukka</i> beneath the sheets is <b>unfit.</b> In the first two cases, because the sheet is susceptible to ritual impurity, it renders the otherwise fit roofing unfit. In the case of the canopy, one is not sitting under the roofing of the <i>sukka</i>; rather, he is sitting inside a tent. <b>However, one</b> may <b>spread</b> the sheet <b>over</b> the frame of <b>a two-post [<i>naklitei</i>] bed,</b> which has one post in the middle of each end of the bed. When spreading the sheet over the posts it forms an inclined rather than a flat roof, and a tent with an inclined roof is not considered a significant structure.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Ḥisda said:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> the ruling that the sheet renders the <i>sukka</i> unfit <b>only</b> when it is placed underneath the roofing <b>due to the falling leaves; however,</b> if his intent was to spread the sheet for decorative purposes <b>to beautify</b> the <i>sukka</i>, it is not in the category of roofing and the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit.</b> The Gemara asks: This is <b>obvious,</b> as: <b>Due to the falling leaves,</b> is what <b>we learned</b> in the mishna. The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say</b> that <b>the same is true,</b> i.e., the <i>sukka</i> is unfit, <b>even</b> when the sheet was spread <b>to beautify</b> the <i>sukka</i>, <b>and</b> the reason <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> specifically the case where one spread the sheet <b>due to the falling leaves</b> is that the mishna <b>teaches the matter,</b> spreading a sheet in the <i>sukka</i>, <b>in the manner in which it</b> typically occurs. Rav Ḥisda <b>teaches us</b> that the formulation of the mishna is precise and the <i>halakha</i> applies specifically to the case cited. If one spread the sheet for decorative purposes, it does not render the <i>sukka</i> unfit.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that the following <i>Tosefta</i> <b>supports</b> the opinion of Rav Ḥisda. If <b>one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>in accordance with its halakhic</b> requirements, <b>and decorated it with colorful curtains and sheets, and hung in it</b> ornamental <b>nuts, almonds, peaches, and pomegranates, grape branches [<i>parkilei</i>], and wreaths of stalks</b> of grain, <b>wines, oils, and</b> vessels full of <b>flour, it is prohibited to</b> derive benefit and <b>use them</b>

Daf 10b

<b>until the conclusion of the last day of the Festival. And if</b> before he hung the decorations <b>he stipulated with regard to them</b> that he will be permitted to use them even during the Festival, <b>everything is according to his stipulation,</b> and he is permitted to use them. Apparently, sheets may indeed be spread in the <i>sukka</i> for decorative purposes. The Gemara rejects this: There is no proof from the <i>Tosefta</i>, as <b>perhaps</b> the reference is to sheets spread <b>on the side</b> of the <i>sukka</i>. However, if they are spread beneath the roofing, it renders the <i>sukka</i> unfit.
§ Apropos decorations, <b>it was stated: <i>Sukka</i> decorations do not diminish the</b> height of the <b><i>sukka</i>.</b> Decorations hanging from the roofing are not considered part of the structure and therefore do not diminish the height of the <i>sukka</i>. If the roofing is more than twenty cubits above the ground, the decorations hanging within twenty cubits of the ground do not render the <i>sukka</i> fit. <b>Rav Ashi said:</b> However, if the decorations are spread <b>on the side</b> of the roof, they are considered part of the structure and <b>diminish</b> the area. If the decorations render the interior of the <i>sukka</i> less than seven by seven handbreadths, the <i>sukka</i> is unfit.
The Gemara relates with regard to <b>Minyamin, the servant of Rav Ashi,</b> that <b>his shirt became wet [<i>itamisha</i>], and he spread it over the <i>sukka</i></b> to dry it. <b>Rav Ashi said to him: Take it down so that</b> people <b>will not say</b> that <b>they are roofing</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with an item susceptible to ritual impurity.</b> The servant said to him: <b>But don’t they see that it is wet</b> and understand that I placed it there to dry? Rav Ashi replied: Take it down <b>once it is dry</b> is what <b>I am saying to you,</b> as then people are apt to think that it is part of the roofing.
<b>It was stated</b> with regard to <b><i>sukka</i> decorations,</b> e.g., sheets spread beneath the roofing to decorate the <i>sukka</i>, <b>that are removed from</b> the roofing <b>four</b> handbreadths, the <i>amora’im</i> disagreed whether they interpose between the roofing and the <i>sukka</i>. <b>Rav Naḥman said:</b> The <i>sukka</i> remains <b>fit. Rav Ḥisda and Rabba bar Rav Huna said:</b> It is <b>unfit.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Ḥisda and Rabba bar Rav Huna happened</b> to come <b>to the house of the Exilarch. Rav Naḥman,</b> who was the official in charge of the Exilarch’s household, <b>lodged them in a <i>sukka</i> whose decorations were removed from</b> the roofing <b>four handbreadths. They were silent and did not say anything to him,</b> even though in their opinion the <i>sukka</i> was unfit. Rav Naḥman <b>said to them: Did the Sages retract their halakhic</b> ruling? Does your silence indicate that you concede to my ruling? <b>They said to him: We are on the path to perform a mitzva and,</b> therefore, <b>we are exempt from the</b> mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i>.</b> Therefore, it is permitted for us to sleep in this <i>sukka</i>. In terms of the <i>halakha</i>, our ruling is unchanged.
<b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: It is permitted to sleep in</b> a bed with <b>netting inside the <i>sukka</i>, even though</b> the bed <b>has a roof, provided that</b> the netting <b>is not more than ten</b> handbreadths <b>higher</b> than the bed. In that case, the netting is not considered a tent in and of itself.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear: One who sleeps in</b> a bed with <b>netting inside the <i>sukka</i> did not fulfill his obligation,</b> contrary to the statement that Rav Yehuda cited in the name of Shmuel. The Gemara answers: <b>With what are we dealing here?</b> It is a case <b>where</b> the netting <b>is more than ten</b> handbreadths <b>higher</b> than the bed and is considered a tent in and of itself.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a mishna: <b>One who sleeps beneath the bed in the <i>sukka</i> did not fulfill his obligation.</b> As the height of a typical bed is less than ten handbreadths, apparently, even if the covering beneath which one is sleeping in less than ten handbreadths high, it is a tent in and of itself and he does not fulfill his obligation. The Gemara answers: <b>Didn’t Shmuel interpret</b> the mishna as referring <b>to</b> the case of <b>a bed ten</b> handbreadths <b>high?</b> Therefore, one who sleeps beneath the bed did not fulfill his obligation.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> that which is taught in the mishna: <b>Or</b> if <b>one spread</b> a sheet as a canopy <b>over the</b> frame of a <b>four-post</b> bed, the area in the <i>sukka</i> beneath the sheet is <b>unfit.</b> Apparently, a bed with certain types of netting is unfit. The Gemara answers: <b>There, too,</b> it is a case where the posts <b>are ten</b> handbreadths <b>high.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But that is not</b> the way <b>it is taught, as it is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: <b><i>Naklitin</i> are two</b> posts <b>and <i>kinofot</i> are four</b> posts. If <b>one spread</b> a sheet <b>over four posts,</b> the area in the <i>sukka</i> beneath the sheet is <b>unfit;</b> if one did so <b>over two posts</b> the entire <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit, provided the two posts are not ten</b> handbreadths <b>higher than the bed.</b> This proves <b>by inference that</b> a sheet spread over <b>four posts</b> renders the area in the <i>sukka</i> beneath the sheet unfit <b>even if it is not ten</b> handbreadths <b>high.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Four posts are different because they are fixed</b> in the bed and constitute a significant space even without the requisite height. The Gemara asks: <b>But a <i>sukka</i> atop</b> another <b><i>sukka</i> is fixed, and</b> yet <b>Shmuel said: As</b> the criterion for <b>its fitness, so too</b> is the criterion for <b>its unfitness.</b> The upper <i>sukka</i> renders the lower <i>sukka</i> unfit only if it is ten handbreadths high. The Sages <b>say</b> in distinguishing between the cases: <b>There,</b> in the case of a <i>sukka</i> atop another <i>sukka</i>, where the measurement is <b>in order to disqualify</b> the lower <b><i>sukka</i>, ten</b> handbreadths are required to render the upper <i>sukka</i> a separate entity. However, <b>here,</b> in the case of the four-post bed, <b>in order to consider</b> the covering <b>a tent, less than ten</b> handbreadths <b>is also</b> considered to be <b>a tent,</b> as it is fixed.
§ <b>Rav Taḥalifa bar Avimi said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: One who sleeps naked in</b> a bed with <b>netting</b> and is required to recite <i>Shema</i> <b>moves his head out from</b> beneath <b>the netting and recites <i>Shema</i>.</b> Although he is naked, the netting is considered like a garment; therefore, it is permitted to recite <i>Shema</i>.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who sleeps naked in a bed with netting</b> may <b>not move his head out from</b> beneath <b>the netting and recite <i>Shema</i>.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>With what are we dealing here?</b> It is a case <b>where</b> the netting <b>is ten</b> handbreadths <b>high.</b> In that case, it is considered a tent and not a garment.
The Gemara notes: <b>So too, it is reasonable</b> to understand the <i>baraita</i> in that manner <b>from</b> the fact <b>that it is taught</b> in <b>the latter clause</b> of that <i>baraita</i>: <b>To what is this comparable?</b> It is comparable <b>to one standing naked in</b> his <b>house, that he</b> may <b>not move his head out the window and recite <i>Shema</i>.</b> That is certainly ineffective. The fact that the <i>baraita</i> likens the bed with netting to a house indicates that it is netting at least ten handbreadths high. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from it</b> that this is the correct understanding.

Daf 11a

<b>And</b> this <i>halakha</i> that it is not sufficient to place his head out the window applies <b>also</b> to <b>a house even if it is not ten</b> handbreadths <b>high. Since it is a fixed</b> structure <b>it is</b> considered <b>a tent</b> in and of itself, <b>as it is no less</b> permanent <b>than</b> a bed with <b>four posts,</b> which is considered a tent even though the netting is less than ten handbreadths higher than the bed.
<b>Some say another version</b> of the previous discussion: <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: It is permitted to sleep inside</b> a netted <b>bridal canopy in the <i>sukka</i> since it</b> is inclined and <b>does not have a roof, even though it is ten</b> handbreadths <b>high.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who sleeps in</b> a bed with <b>netting inside the <i>sukka</i> did not fulfill his obligation.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>With what are we dealing here</b> in the <i>baraita</i>? It is <b>with</b> a bed with netting <b>in</b> a case where, unlike a bridal canopy, <b>it has a roof.</b>
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> another question from what we learned: <b><i>Naklitin</i> are two</b> posts <b>and <i>kinofot</i> are four</b> posts. If <b>one spread</b> a sheet <b>over four posts,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit;</b> if he spread a sheet <b>over two posts</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit, provided the two posts are not ten</b> handbreadths <b>higher than the bed.</b> It can be inferred from here: <b>But</b> if they <b>are higher than ten</b> handbreadths the <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit even though it has no roof,</b> contrary to the opinion of Shmuel.
The Gemara answers: <b>Two posts are different</b> from the bridal canopy <b>because they are fixed</b> in the bed, and therefore the sheet over them is considered a tent even with an inclined roof. The Gemara asks: <b>If they are fixed</b> then <b>let them be</b> considered <b>like four posts</b> and let them render the <i>sukka</i> unfit even when they are less than ten handbreadths high. The Gemara answers: <b>Vis-à-vis four posts,</b> two posts are <b>not</b> considered <b>fixed</b> and therefore, they render the <i>sukka</i> unfit only when they are ten handbreadths higher than the bed. However, <b>vis-à-vis</b> a bed with <b>netting,</b> two posts <b>are</b> considered <b>fixed</b> and consequently, they render the <i>sukka</i> unfit even though they lack a roof.
<b>Rabba bar Rav Huna taught: It is permitted to sleep in</b> a bed with <b>netting even though it has a roof</b> and <b>even though it is higher than ten</b> handbreadths. <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did Rabba bar Rav Huna teach this <i>halakha</i>? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda who said</b> that in principle, <b>a temporary tent does not come and negate a permanent tent, as we learned</b> in a mishna that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: We were accustomed to sleep beneath the bed before the Elders.</b> Since a bed is a temporary tent relative to the more permanent <i>sukka</i>, even one sleeping beneath a bed is considered to be sleeping in the <i>sukka</i> and he fulfills his obligation in that manner.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> if the statement of Rabba bar Rav Huna is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, <b>let him say</b> simply that the <b><i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>If he said the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, I would have said that this applies only to a bed, which is made for</b> use <b>atop it</b> and not beneath it. Perhaps the reason a bed is not considered a tent in and of itself is that its primary purpose is to lie on top of it, not in the space beneath it. <b>However,</b> with regard to a bed with <b>netting, which is made for</b> use of the space <b>within it, say</b> that <b>no,</b> it is indeed considered a tent in and of itself and one who sleeps in it does not fulfill his obligation. Therefore, Rabba bar Rav Huna <b>teaches us</b> that <b>the rationale for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda is that a temporary tent does not come and negate a permanent tent,</b> and <b>there is no difference</b> whether the temporary tent is <b>a bed</b> or whether it is the <b>netting</b> over a bed.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>one trellised</b> climbing plants such as <b>a grapevine, or gourd</b> plant, <b>or ivy [<i>kissos</i>], over</b> a <i>sukka</i> while they were still attached to the ground, <b>and</b> then added <b>roofing atop them,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit. If</b> the amount of fit <b>roofing was greater</b> than the plants attached to the ground, <b>or if he cut</b> the climbing plants so that they were no longer attached to the ground, <b>it is fit.</b>
<b>This is the principle</b> with regard to the roofing of a <i>sukka</i>: <b>Anything that is susceptible to ritual impurity,</b> e.g., vessels, <b>or its growth is not from the ground,</b> e.g., animal hides, <b>one may not roof</b> his <i>sukka</i> <b>with it. And anything that is not susceptible to ritual impurity and its growth is from the ground, one may roof</b> his <i>sukka</i> <b>with it.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Yosef sat before Rav Huna, and he sat and said,</b> citing the mishna: <b>Or if he cut them, it is fit.</b> He added: <b>And Rav said</b> that it is not enough merely to cut the climbing plants; one <b>is obligated</b> to <b>move</b> the branches, thereby performing an action with the branches in order to render the roofing fit. When he placed the climbing plants atop the <i>sukka</i>, they were attached and therefore unfit roofing. When he ultimately cut them, it was as if the <i>sukka</i> were roofed by itself.
