In that case, the <i>sukka</i> is unfit due to the principle: Prepare it, and not from that which has already been prepared, derived from the verse: “You shall prepare for you the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>” (Deuteronomy 16:13).
<b>Rav Huna said to</b> Rav Yosef: <b>Shmuel stated this</b> <i>halakha</i>. <b>Rav Yosef turned his face away</b> in anger <b>and said to him: Did I say to you that Shmuel did not say it? Rav said it, and Shmuel said it</b> as well. What is your point? <b>Rav Huna said to him: This is what I am saying to you, that Shmuel said it and not Rav, as Rav deems</b> the roofing <b>fit</b> merely by cutting them, without moving them, <b>as</b> in <b>that</b> incident <b>where Rav Amram the Pious cast the sky-blue dye,</b> i.e., ritual fringes, <b>upon the garment</b> [<b><i>pirzuma</i></b>] <b>of the people of his household.</b> However, <b>he attached them, but did not cut the ends of their strands</b> prior to tying them, i.e., he took a single string, folded it a number of times, and inserted it into the hole in the garment. Since the fringes were uncut when he tied them, he was uncertain whether they were fit for use in fulfilling the mitzva, due to the principle: Prepare it, and not from that which has already been prepared.
Rav Amram <b>came before Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi</b> and asked him about the halakhic status of the ritual fringes. <b>He said to him</b> that <b>this</b> is what <b>Rav said: One cuts them</b> into separate strands <b>and they are fit.</b> There is no need to remove them, cut them, and reattach them to the garment as separate strands. <b>Apparently,</b> according to Rav, <b>their cutting is their preparation.</b> Cutting them qualifies as active preparation of the fringes. <b>Here too,</b> in the case of the roofing of a <i>sukka</i>, Rav holds: <b>Their cutting is their preparation,</b> and no further action is required.
The Gemara asks: <b>And does Shmuel hold</b> that <b>we do not say: Their cutting is their preparation? But didn’t Shmuel teach in the name of Rabbi Ḥiyya: If one cast</b> fringes <b>upon two corners</b> of a garment <b>simultaneously</b> by repeatedly inserting one strand into holes in both corners <b>and afterward cut the ends of their strands</b> resulting in two full-fledged fringes, the fringes are <b>fit. What, is it not</b> referring to a case <b>where one ties</b> the fringes as required <b>and afterward cuts them?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> it is referring to a case where <b>he cuts</b> the strands <b>and afterward ties</b> them.
The Gemara asks: If the reference is to a case where <b>he cuts</b> the strands <b>and afterward ties</b> them, <b>what</b> need was there <b>to state</b> that the ritual fringes are fit? That is the prescribed manner of preparing ritual fringes. The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say</b> that in addition to tying the fringes separately

Daf 11b

<b>we require</b> that it must be a single <b>corner at the time of threading</b> the strand through the hole. <b>And there is not</b> a single corner in this case, as although he ties the fringes separately, he threads the two corners simultaneously. Therefore, Shmuel <b>teaches us</b> that with regard to threading it is not a concern.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one attached</b> the ritual fringes <b>and did not</b> first <b>cut the ends of their strands, they are unfit. What, is it not</b> saying that the ritual fringes are <b>unfit forever</b> with no way to remedy the situation, <b>and</b> this is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rav?</b> The Gemara answers that <b>Rav</b> could have <b>said to you: What</b> is the meaning of <b>unfit?</b> It means they are <b>unfit until they will be cut;</b> not that they are unfit forever. <b>And Shmuel said</b> that it means <b>they are unfit forever. And Levi also said: They are unfit forever. And likewise, Rav Mattana said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: They are unfit forever.</b>
<b>Some say</b> that <b>Rav Mattana said:</b> There <b>was an incident</b> that happened <b>to me</b> involving this uncertainty with regard to ritual fringes, <b>and I came before Master Shmuel and he said to me: They are unfit forever.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to Rav’s opinion from a different <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one attached</b> the ritual fringes <b>and</b> only <b>afterward cut the ends of their strands, they are unfit. And furthermore, it is taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i> <b>with regard to a <i>sukka</i>:</b> The verse states: “Prepare for you the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>” (Deuteronomy 16:13), and from the language of this verse the Sages derived the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared. From here</b> the Sages <b>said: If one trellised a grapevine, a gourd</b> plant, <b>or ivy</b> over a <i>sukka</i> while still attached to the ground, <b>and</b> then he added <b>roofing atop</b> the vines, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b>
<b>What are the circumstances? If we say</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where he did not</b> subsequently <b>cut</b> the vines, <b>why does</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>particularly</b> teach that it is unfit <b>due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared? Let him derive</b> that the climbing plants are unfit for roofing due to the fact <b>that they are attached</b> to the ground, unrelated to the manner in which they were placed. <b>Rather,</b> it must be referring to a case <b>where he cut them and</b> nevertheless, the <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching</b> that the vines are <b>unfit, and learn from it that we do not say: Their cutting is their preparation; and</b> this is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rav.</b>
<b>Rav</b> could have <b>said to you: With what are we dealing here?</b> It is a case <b>where he pulled</b> the branches until they broke off the tree. Since, in that case, <b>their</b> active <b>preparation is not conspicuous,</b> it does not render the climbing plants fit roofing. The Gemara asks: <b>In any case,</b> does that which was taught with regard to ritual fringes: If <b>one attached</b> the ritual fringes <b>and</b> only <b>afterward cut</b> their strands, etc., pose <b>a difficulty to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav?</b> The Gemara concludes: Indeed, it remains <b>difficult</b> according to Rav.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that this dispute is <b>parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>.</b> If black berries grew on a myrtle branch, one of the four species taken on <i>Sukkot</i>, and its berries were more numerous than its leaves, the myrtle branch is unfit for use in fulfilling the mitzva of taking the four species on <i>Sukkot</i>. However, if one picked enough berries so that the leaves were more numerous, it is fit, although one may not pick the berries on the Festival itself. If <b>he transgressed and picked them</b> on the Festival, <b>it is unfit;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Shimon bar Yehotzadak. And the Rabbis deem it fit</b> in that case.
The Gemara proceeds to explain the basis for the comparison between the dispute with regard to the roofing of the <i>sukka</i> and the dispute with regard to the myrtle branch. The Sages initially <b>thought that everyone,</b> Rabbi Shimon bar Yehotzadak and the Rabbis, agrees that in fulfilling the mitzva of the four species, the three species, i.e., the <b><i>lulav</i>,</b> the myrtle branch, and the willow branch, <b>require a binding</b> by Torah law. Therefore, it is relevant to discuss preparation with regard to this binding. <b>And</b> the Sages also initially thought that everyone agrees that <b>we derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>lulav</i> from</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>sukka</i>, as it is written with regard to <i>sukka</i>: Prepare,</b> from which is derived the principle: Prepare it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared, and</b> the same applies to the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>lulav</i> as well.
<b>What, is it not</b> that the <i>tanna’im</i> <b>disagree with regard to the following? That the one who deems</b> the myrtle branch whose berries were picked on the Festival <b>fit, holds</b> that <b>with regard to</b> the branches on <b>a <i>sukka</i> we say: Their cutting is their preparation, and</b> therefore, <b>with regard to</b> berries on the myrtle branch as one of the species bound with the <b><i>lulav</i> as well, we say: Their picking is their preparation,</b> and no further action is required. <b>And the one who deems it unfit holds</b> that <b>with regard to</b> the branches on <b>a <i>sukka</i> we do not say: Their cutting is their preparation, and</b> therefore, <b>with regard to <i>lulav</i> as well, we do not say: Their picking is their preparation.</b> Therefore, since the myrtle branch was not prepared for use prior to the Festival, and it was bound together with the other species, it is considered already prepared and picking the fruit off the branch is not active preparation sufficient to render it fit.
The Gemara rejects that explanation of the dispute. <b>No,</b> the fact is <b>that everyone agrees</b> that <b>we do not say with regard to <i>sukka</i>: Their cutting is their preparation, and here</b> in the case of the myrtle branch, it is <b>with regard to deriving</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>lulav</i> from</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>sukka</i></b> that <b>they disagree. The one who deems</b> the myrtle branch <b>fit holds</b> that <b>we do not derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>lulav</i> from</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>sukka</i>,</b> and therefore the principle: Prepare it, and not from that which has already been prepared, does not apply to <i>lulav</i>. <b>And the one who deems</b> the myrtle branch <b>unfit holds</b> that <b>we derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>lulav</i> from</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>sukka</i>.</b>
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead: <b>If we hold that <i>lulav</i> requires a binding, everyone agrees</b> that <b>we derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>lulav</i> from</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b><i>sukka</i></b> and the principle: Prepare it, and not from that which has already been prepared, applies to the <i>halakhot</i> of the four species as well. <b>And here</b> it is <b>with regard to the following</b> that <b>they disagree:</b> One <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Shimon bar Yehotzadak, <b>holds</b> that the <i>lulav</i> <b>requires a binding,</b> and therefore the myrtle branch is unfit; <b>and</b> the other <b>Sage,</b> i.e., the Rabbis, <b>holds</b> that the <i>lulav</i> <b>does not require a binding,</b> and therefore, preparation is not relevant with regard to <i>lulav</i> and it makes no difference whether the berries were picked before or after the myrtle branch was bound together with the <i>lulav</i> and the willow branch. <b>And</b> they disagree <b>with regard to</b> the same topic as <b>in the dispute between these <i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A <i>lulav</i>, whether</b> it is <b>bound</b> with the myrtle and willow <b>and whether</b> it is <b>not bound, is fit. Rabbi Yehuda says: If it is bound it is fit; if it is not bound it is unfit.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the rationale</b> for the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda?</b> From where does he derive this requirement by Torah law? The Gemara answers: By means of a verbal analogy, <b>he derives</b> the term <b>taking,</b> written with regard to the four species, <b>from</b> the term <b>taking</b> written with regard to <b>the bundle of hyssop. It is written there,</b> in the context of the sacrifice of the Paschal lamb in Egypt: <b>“Take a bundle of hyssop”</b> (Exodus 12:22), <b>and it is written here,</b> in the context of the four species: <b>“And you shall take for yourselves on the first day</b> the fruit of a beautiful tree, branches of a date palm, and boughs of a dense-leaved tree, and willows of the brook, and you shall rejoice before the Lord your God seven days” (Leviticus 23:40). <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to the Paschal lamb, the mitzva to take the hyssop is specifically <b>in a bundle, so too here,</b> the mitzva to take the four species is specifically <b>in a bundle. And the Rabbis</b> hold: <b>We do not derive</b> the term <b>taking from</b> the term <b>taking</b> by means of the verbal analogy.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which is taught</b> in this <i>baraita</i>: There is <b>a mitzva to bind the</b> myrtle and the willow with the <b><i>lulav</i>. And if he did not bind it, it is fit. If</b> the <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, when one did not bind it, why is it fit? If</b> it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, why</b> is there <b>a mitzva</b> to bind it at all? The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. And</b> the reason that there is a mitzva to bind it is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that it is stated: “This is my God and I will glorify Him [<i>ve’anvehu</i>]”</b> (Exodus 15:2), which they interpreted to mean: <b>Beautify yourself [<i>hitna’e</i>] before Him in</b> the performance of the <b>mitzvot.</b> The Rabbis agree that although failure to bind the three species does not render them unfit for the mitzva, the performance of the mitzva is more beautiful when the <i>lulav</i> is bound.
§ We learned in the mishna: <b>This is the principle</b> with regard to the roofing of a <i>sukka</i>: One may not roof the <i>sukka</i> with <b>anything that is susceptible to ritual impurity</b> or whose growth is not from the ground. The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> with regard to the roofing of a <i>sukka</i> derived? <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that <b>the verse states: “And there went up a mist from the earth,</b> and watered the entire face of the ground” (Genesis 2:6); <b>just as mist,</b> i.e., a cloud, <b>is a substance not capable of contracting ritual impurity, and its growth is from the ground,</b> i.e., arises from the ground, <b>so too,</b> the roofing of the <b><i>sukka</i></b> must consist of <b>a substance</b> that is <b>not susceptible to ritual impurity and its growth is from the ground.</b> Since the mitzva of <i>sukka</i> evokes the clouds of glory with which God enveloped the Israelites in the desert, the legal status of roofing should be like that of a cloud.
The Gemara asks: <b>This</b> works out <b>well according to the one who said</b> that the <i>sukkot</i> mentioned in the verse: “I made the children of Israel to reside in <i>sukkot</i>” (Leviticus 23:43), <b>were clouds of glory,</b> as it is reasonable that the roofing of the <i>sukka</i> is modeled after clouds. <b>However, according to the one who said</b> that the children of Israel <b>established for themselves actual <i>sukkot</i></b> in the desert, and the <i>sukkot</i> of today commemorate those, <b>what can be said?</b> According to that opinion, there is no connection between a <i>sukka</i> and a cloud. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states: <b>“I made the children of Israel to reside in <i>sukkot</i>”;</b> these booths <b>were clouds of glory,</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. Rabbi Akiva says: They established for themselves actual <i>sukkot</i>. This</b> works out <b>well according to Rabbi Eliezer; however, according to Rabbi Akiva what can be said?</b>
<b>When Rav Dimi came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, he said that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> that <b>the verse states: “You shall prepare for you the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>”</b> (Deuteronomy 16:13). The expression “festival of <i>Sukkot</i>” <b>likens <i>sukka</i> to the Festival peace-offering</b> [<b><i>ḥagiga</i>]. Just as the Festival peace-offering is an item not susceptible to ritual impurity, and its growth is from the ground,</b> as animals draw nourishment from vegetation, <b>so too,</b> the roofing of <b>the <i>sukka</i></b> must be <b>a substance that is not susceptible to ritual impurity and its growth is from the ground.</b>

Daf 12a

The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> that juxtaposition is the source of the <i>halakha</i>, say: <b>Just as the Festival peace-offering</b> is brought from <b>animals, so too</b> the <b><i>sukka</i></b> roofing should consist of <b>animals.</b> As that is clearly not the case, that verse cannot be the source for the roofing of the <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara cites a different source: <b>When Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia he said that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> that <b>the verse states:</b> “You shall prepare for you the festival of <i>Sukkot</i> for seven days <b>as you gather from your threshing floor and from your winepress”</b> (Deuteronomy 16:13), and the Sages interpreted that it is <b>with regard to the waste of the threshing floor and of the winepress</b> that <b>the verse is speaking.</b> One uses grain stalks and vines for roofing the <i>sukka</i>, materials that are not susceptible to ritual impurity and grow from the ground.
The Gemara asks: <b>And say</b> that the verse teaches that one uses the items placed on the <b>threshing floor itself,</b> i.e., stalks with the grain still attached to them, <b>and the</b> items placed in the <b>winepress itself,</b> i.e., vines with the grapes still attached, as roofing. Grain and grapes, like all foods, are susceptible to ritual impurity. If the verse is interpreted in this manner, the mishna’s criteria for roofing fit for a <i>sukka</i> could not be derived from it. <b>Rabbi Zeira said: “Winepress” is written</b> in the verse <b>here,</b> referring to the wine, <b>and it is impossible to roof with</b> wine. Apparently, the verse is referring to stalks and sheaves but not to produce.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya strongly objects to this:</b> Why can’t a <i>sukka</i> be roofed with wine? <b>Say</b> that it is referring to <b>congealed wine that comes from Senir,</b> from Mount Hermon, <b>which is similar to a cake of figs.</b> Since it is possible to interpret the verse as referring to the use of food for roofing, the mishna’s criteria for roofing fit for a <i>sukka</i> could not be derived from it. <b>Rabbi Zeira said: This matter was in our hands,</b> as we assumed that we found the source in the Torah for the materials fit for roofing, <b>and Rabbi Yirmeya came and took an axe to it.</b> He destroyed the proof by raising the matter of congealed wine.
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> One may nevertheless derive the ruling of the mishna from this verse: <b>“From your threshing floor,”</b> indicating an item that comes from the threshing floor, <b>but not</b> the items placed on the <b>threshing floor,</b> i.e., grain, <b>itself; “from your winepress,” but not</b> the items placed in the <b>winepress,</b> i.e., grapes, <b>itself.</b> The verse is referring to the waste products of the produce placed on the threshing floor and in the winepress.
<b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that proof can be cited <b>from here: “Go forth to the mount and fetch olive branches, and branches of wild olive, and myrtle branches, and palm branches, and the boughs of a dense-leaved tree</b> in order to make <i>sukkot</i> as written” (Nehemiah 8:15). From this verse, the materials for <i>sukka</i> roofing can be derived.
Apropos this verse, the Gemara asks: <b>These myrtle</b> branches <b>are</b> the same as <b>those</b> boughs of <b>a dense-leaved tree;</b> why does the verse mention both? <b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that this is how it is to be understood. The term “myrtle branches” is referring to <b>a wild myrtle,</b> unfit for use as one of the four species, to be used <b>for the</b> roofing of the <b><i>sukka</i>. And</b> the term “boughs of <b>a dense-leaved tree”</b> is referring to the myrtle, whose leaves overlie each other, to be used <b>for the <i>lulav</i>,</b> the mitzva of the four species.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One</b> may <b>not roof</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>with bundles of straw</b> tied with rope, <b>or bundles of wood, or bundles of twigs. And</b> with regard to <b>all of</b> the bundles, <b>if one untied them,</b> they are <b>fit</b> for use in roofing the <i>sukka</i>, as their lack of fitness is due to the fact that the bundles are tied. <b>And</b> even when tied, <b>all of</b> the bundles are <b>fit for</b> use in constructing the <b>walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i>.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Ya’akov said: I heard</b> explanations <b>from Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> for <b>two</b> similar <i>halakhot</i> of <i>sukka</i>: <b>One</b> with regard to the <i>halakha</i> in <b>this</b> mishna that bundles may not be used in roofing the <i>sukka</i>, <b>and the other</b> with regard to the mishna below, pertaining to <b>one who hollows out</b> space in <b>a stack of grain</b> by removing sheaves from the bottom of the stack <b>to establish a <i>sukka</i> for him.</b> In that case, the space is surrounded by grain on the sides and above, and therefore it is <b>not a <i>sukka</i>.</b>
The rationale for <b>one</b> of the <i>halakhot</i> is <b>due to the decree of the storehouse.</b> Although, fundamentally, the <i>sukka</i> is fit, the Sages issued a decree prohibiting its use, lest one come to use his storehouse as a <i>sukka</i> and fail to establish it properly. <b>And</b> the rationale for <b>one</b> of the <i>halakhot</i> is <b>due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared,</b> as no active preparation was performed. <b>And I do not know</b> at present <b>which of</b> the <i>halakhot</i> <b>is due to</b> the decree of the <b>storehouse and which of them is due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya said: Let us see</b> and determine which rationale Rabbi Yoḥanan applied to each <i>halakha</i>; <b>as Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: For what</b> reason did <b>they say</b> that <b>one</b> may <b>not roof</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>with bundles of straw, and bundles of wood, and bundles of twigs?</b> It is because <b>sometimes a person comes from the field in the evening, and</b> he has <b>his bundle</b> of wood or straw <b>on his shoulder, and he lifts it and places it atop his</b> storage <b>shed to dry it. And,</b> when the festival of <i>Sukkot</i> arrives, <b>he reconsiders</b> and decides to use the shed as a <i>sukka</i> and the bundle on top of it <b>for roofing. And</b> in that case the roofing would be unfit, as <b>the Torah said: Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared.</b> From Rabbi Yoḥanan’s formulation, apparently it is unfit due to the decree lest one come to use his storehouse as a <i>sukka</i> and fail to establish it properly, not due to some fundamental prohibition. <b>From</b> the fact <b>that this</b> case of the bundles is prohibited <b>due to the decree of the storehouse, that</b> case of the stack of grain must be prohibited <b>due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> why was <b>Rabbi Ya’akov</b> unable to arrive at Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion based on the <i>halakha</i> cited in his name? The Gemara explains: It is because <b>he did not hear this</b> statement <b>of Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba,</b> and there was no other proof.
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> The distinction of Rabbi Yoḥanan between these two cases is difficult. <b>Is that to say</b> that <b>bundles of straw and bundles of wood</b> are unfit roofing <b>due to the decree of the storehouse</b> and <b>not due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared?</b> Is that ultimately the principle underlying the decree of the storehouse? <b>And</b> on the other hand, in the case of <b>one who hollows a stack of grain,</b> is the <i>sukka</i> unfit <b>due</b> only <b>to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared,</b> but <b>not due to the decree of the storehouse?</b> Rather, there is no distinction between the <i>halakhot</i> and both reasons apply to both.
The Gemara notes: <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could have <b>said to you,</b> in response to Rav Ashi, that it is not so because the <i>halakhot</i> are formulated differently in the respective <i>mishnayot</i>. <b>Here,</b> in the mishna pertaining to bundles, <b>where it teaches: One</b> may <b>not roof with them, it is <i>ab initio</i></b>

Daf 12b

<b>that one</b> may <b>not roof, due to the decree of the storehouse</b> issued by the Sages; <b>but by Torah</b> law, it <b>seems well</b> to do so. <b>There,</b> in the mishna pertaining to the stack of grain, <b>where it teaches: It is not a <i>sukka</i>,</b> it means that not only by rabbinic decree, but <b>even after the fact, by Torah</b> law <b>as well, it is not a <i>sukka</i>.</b>
§ <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> If <b>one roofed</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>with convex arrow</b> shafts, which are made of wood with a protrusion on the end that is fitted into the socket of the metal arrowhead, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit.</b> These shafts are flat wooden utensils, which are not susceptible to ritual impurity. Therefore, they are fit roofing for a <i>sukka</i>. If, however, one roofed his <i>sukka</i> <b>with concave</b> arrow shafts, which have a socket into which a protrusion from the metal arrowhead is inserted, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b> Since these shafts are wooden utensils with a receptacle, they are susceptible to ritual impurity. Therefore, they are unfit roofing for a <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara asks: The fact that if one roofed a <i>sukka</i> with <b>convex</b> arrow shafts, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit</b> is <b>obvious.</b> It is no different from roofing with straight, smooth reeds. The Gemara answers: Stating this <i>halakha</i> is necessary. <b>Lest you say: Let us issue a decree</b> and prohibit roofing with <b>convex</b> shafts <b>due to</b> the prohibition against roofing with <b>concave</b> shafts, therefore Rav <b>teaches us</b> that no decree is issued, and convex shafts are fit roofing.
<b>The Master said:</b> If one roofed a <i>sukka</i> with <b>concave</b> arrows, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b> The Gemara asks: This is <b>obvious.</b> All wooden receptacles are susceptible to ritual impurity. The Gemara answers: Stating this <i>halakha</i> is necessary. <b>Lest you say: A receptacle that is designated to be</b> permanently <b>filled, its status</b> is <b>not</b> that of <b>a receptacle,</b> as a receptacle is typically filled and emptied; in this case, once the arrowhead fills the receptacle, it remains there, therefore Rav <b>teaches us</b> that it is deemed a receptacle and is not fit roofing.
§ <b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> If <b>one roofed</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>with bundles of combed flax,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit,</b> as flax at that stage of processing is the raw material from which threads are spun, and it is susceptible to ritual impurity. If one roofed a <i>sukka</i> <b>with stalks of flax,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit.</b> Since they remain in their natural state and have not been processed in any way, their legal status is that of any tree, and they are not susceptible to ritual impurity. <b>And</b> if one roofed with <b>the <i>hoshen</i> of flax,</b> stalks at an intermediate stage of processing, <b>I do not know what</b> their status is, i.e., whether or not they are fit for roofing.
Rabba bar bar Ḥana added: <b>And</b> when Rabbi Yoḥanan used the term <b><i>hoshen</i> of</b> flax <b>itself, I do not know</b> to what stage of processing the flax he was referring. <b>Which way</b> do <b>you</b> look at it? Is it that <b>if one crushed</b> the flax <b>and did not comb</b> it, Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>calls it <i>hoshen</i>, but if he soaked</b> it <b>and did not crush</b> it, Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>calls it <i>hotzen</i>,</b> because he has not actually begun processing the flax itself? <b>Or, perhaps if he soaked</b> it <b>and did not crush</b> it, Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>calls it <i>hoshen</i>,</b> while <i>hotzen</i> is reserved for flax that was not processed at all.
§ <b>Rav Yehuda said:</b> With regard to <b>these</b> wide <b>licorice and wormwood</b> leaves, <b>one</b> may <b>roof his <i>sukka</i> with them,</b> since these are not consumed by people. Their legal status is that of any other plant; they are not susceptible to ritual impurity. <b>Abaye said: With licorice</b> leaves, <b>one</b> may <b>roof</b> his <i>sukka</i>; <b>with wormwood</b> leaves, <b>one</b> may <b>not roof</b> his <i>sukka</i>. <b>What is the reason</b> for this distinction? <b>Since</b>

Daf 13a

<b>their odor grows offensive</b> over time, <b>one abandons</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>and exits.</b> It is inappropriate to establish a <i>sukka</i> in which it is impossible to remain.
Similarly, <b>Rav Ḥanan bar Rava said:</b> With regard to <b>these thorns and shrubs, one</b> may <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with them. Abaye said: With thorns, one</b> may <b>roof</b> his <i>sukka</i>; <b>with shrubs, one</b> may <b>not roof</b> his <i>sukka</i>. <b>What is the reason</b> for this distinction? <b>Since their leaves fall</b> over time and they are apt to fall into the food and disturb those in the <i>sukka</i>, <b>one abandons</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>and exits.</b>
<b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> With regard to <b>this offshoot of the</b> trunk of the <b>palm tree,</b> from which several branches emerge; <b>one</b> may <b>roof the <i>sukka</i> with it. Although</b> the branches <b>are</b> naturally <b>bound, a binding at the hand of Heaven is not considered a binding.</b> Furthermore, <b>although one then binds</b> the branches together at the end removed from the trunk, where they grow apart into separate branches, and roofs with them, the <i>sukka</i> is fit, since if <b>one binds</b> a bundle that is already bound <b>into one</b> unit <b>it is not considered a binding.</b>
Likewise, <b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that <b>Ravina bar Sheila said:</b> With regard to <b>these offshoots of reeds, one</b> may <b>roof the <i>sukka</i> with them. Although</b> the branches <b>are</b> naturally <b>bound, a binding at the hand of Heaven is not considered a binding.</b> Furthermore, <b>although one then binds</b> the reeds together at the other end, the <i>sukka</i> is fit, since if <b>one binds</b> a bundle that is already bound <b>into one</b> unit <b>it is not considered a binding.</b>
The Gemara notes that <b>this</b> opinion <b>is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>reeds and spades, one</b> may <b>roof</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>with them.</b> The Gemara asks: The fact that one may roof his <i>sukka</i> with <b>reeds</b> is <b>obvious.</b> After all, they meet all the criteria of fit roofing. Rather, <b>say:</b> With regard to <b>these offshoots of reeds, one</b> may <b>roof the <i>sukka</i> with them.</b>
§ Apropos the above <i>halakha</i>, the Gemara cites another statement that <b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that <b>Ravina bar Sheila said: With these bitter herbs of a marsh, a person fulfills his obligation on Passover.</b>
<b>The Gemara raises an objection to his</b> opinion. With regard to every mitzva that requires use of hyssop, one takes standard <b>hyssop and neither a hyssop</b> that grows in <b>Greece, nor stibium hyssop, nor desert hyssop, nor Roman hyssop, nor</b> any other kind of <b>hyssop whose</b> name is accompanied by <b>a modifier.</b> The same should hold true for the mitzva of bitter herbs; bitter herbs of the marsh, whose name is accompanied by a modifier, are not the bitter herbs mentioned in the Torah.
<b>Abaye said</b> in response: There is a distinction between the cases. <b>Every</b> species <b>whose name was differentiated prior to the giving of the Torah,</b> i.e., the distinction between its different subspecies predated the Revelation at Sinai, <b>and the Torah</b> then <b>came and was particular about</b> one specific subspecies, <b>it is known that</b> the species <b>has</b> other subspecies identified with <b>a modifier</b> that are unfit for use in fulfilling the mitzva. <b>And these</b> bitter herbs, <b>their names were not differentiated prior to the giving of the Torah at all;</b> all the subspecies were known simply as bitter herbs. Therefore, when the Torah requires bitter herbs, one may fulfill the mitzva with all subspecies of bitter herbs.
<b>Rava said</b> a different explanation. Actually, the <b>name</b> of this plant <b>is merely bitter herbs</b> without a modifier. <b>And</b> the fact <b>that one calls them bitter herbs of the marsh is because they are</b> typically <b>found in the marsh.</b> Therefore, there is no reason that they may not be used to fulfill the mitzva on Passover.
§ <b>Rav Ḥisda said: If one bound one</b> item, even if he did so with a knot, <b>it is not considered a binding.</b> If one bound <b>three</b> items together, everyone agrees that <b>it is considered a binding.</b> If one bound <b>two</b> items, it is the subject of <b>a dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi Yosei and the Rabbis, as we learned</b> in a mishna: With regard to all matters that involve <b>the mitzva of hyssop,</b> the requirement is to have <b>three stalks</b> with their roots, <b>and on them three stems,</b> one on each stalk. <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> The <b>mitzva of hyssop</b> fundamentally requires <b>three stems.</b> If the bundle of hyssop was rendered incomplete, <b>its remnants</b> are fit for use with <b>two</b> stems. If all the stems broke, the hyssop is fit for use, as long as the <b>stumps of its</b> central stem remain <b>any size.</b>
<b>It enters our minds</b> to say: <b>From</b> the fact <b>that</b> Rabbi Yosei said that for the bundle of hyssop to be fit for the mitzva after the fact <b>its remnants are two,</b> apparently <b>its origins</b> were <b>also two</b> stalks. <b>And</b> the fact <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> that the binding includes <b>three</b> plants, that is the requirement <b>for</b> the <b>mitzva</b> to be performed <i>ab initio</i>. <b>And from</b> the fact <b>that Rabbi Yosei</b> requires <b>three</b> plants only <b>for</b> the <b>mitzva</b> to be performed <i>ab initio</i>, conclude that <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with him, hold that failure to include <b>three</b> stalks in the bundle <b>renders it unfit</b> for the mitzva. Apparently, the Rabbis and Rabbi Yosei dispute whether it is two or three items that are necessary to be considered a binding.
The Gemara questions that understanding of the dispute. <b>But wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> With regard to the <b>hyssop</b> bundle, if <b>its origins</b> were <b>two</b> stalks <b>and its remnants are one,</b> it is <b>unfit. And it is fit only when its origins</b> were <b>three and its remnants are two.</b> Rather, <b>reverse</b> the opinions in the mishna: According <b>to Rabbi Yosei,</b> failure to include <b>three</b> stalks in the bundle <b>renders it unfit</b> for the mitzva; according <b>to the Rabbis, three</b> is the requirement <b>for</b> the <b>mitzva</b> to be performed <i>ab initio</i>.
The Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> supporting this understanding. <b>And this was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to the <b>hyssop</b> bundle, if <b>its origins</b> were <b>two</b> stalks <b>and its remnants</b> are <b>one,</b> it is <b>fit. And it is unfit only when its origins and its remnants</b> are <b>one.</b> Clearly, this is the opinion of the Rabbis.
The Gemara questions the end of the <i>baraita</i>: If <b>its remnants</b> are <b>one,</b> it is <b>unfit? Didn’t you say</b> in the first clause of the <i>baraita</i> that if <b>its remnants</b> are <b>one</b> it is <b>fit?</b>

Daf 13b

<b>Rather,</b> emend the <i>baraita</i> and <b>say:</b> It is unfit <b>only when its origins, like its remnants,</b> are <b>one.</b>
<b>Mareimar taught:</b> With regard to <b>these bundles</b> of reeds <b>from Sura</b> that are bound for sale, <b>one</b> may <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with them. Although</b> the seller <b>bound them, he bound them merely to</b> ascertain <b>the number</b> more readily, and they will not remain bound.
<b>Rabbi Abba said:</b> With regard to <b>these huts</b> made <b>of willow branches, once their upper ties</b> holding them together <b>are undone,</b> they are <b>fit</b> roofing. The Gemara asks: <b>But aren’t they</b> still <b>tied from below? Rav Pappa said:</b> Rabbi Abba is referring to a case <b>where he unties them</b> from below as well.
<b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that Rabbi Abba is referring to a case <b>where one does not untie them</b> from below, they are fit for <i>sukka</i> roofing, as <b>any binding</b> that is <b>not destined to be moved is not considered a binding.</b> Since these huts are untied from above, were one to attempt to move them, they would fall apart.
§ <b>Rabbi Abba said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> With regard to <b>vegetables</b> about <b>which the Sages said: One fulfills his obligation</b> to eat bitter herbs <b>on Passover,</b> if they are spread over a source of ritual impurity imparted by a corpse, they <b>transmit ritual impurity,</b> and the impurity spreads to objects beneath them. <b>And,</b> nevertheless, the Sages decreed that <b>they do not serve as a barrier before the spread of ritual impurity.</b> The impurity breaches roofing made of these vegetables and rises upward, as if there were no covering over it. If one roofs a <i>sukka</i> with these vegetables, it is as if they were not there at all, <b>and they render a <i>sukka</i> unfit due to</b> the unfitness of <b>airspace.</b> Just as three handbreadths of airspace in the roofing renders a <i>sukka</i> unfit, so too, three handbreadths of these vegetables in the roofing renders a <i>sukka</i> unfit. <b>What is the reason</b> for this <i>halakha</i>? <b>Since when they dry they crumble and fall,</b> even while fresh, they are <b>as one that is not there.</b>
Apropos the statements of Rabbi Abba, the Gemara cites another. <b>Rabbi Abba said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said:</b> In the case of <b>one who harvests</b> bunches of grapes <b>for the winepress,</b> these bunches <b>do not have handles.</b> The stems, which connect the grapes to the clusters, are not required for the production of wine. Therefore, their legal status is not that of a handle in terms of ritual impurity; they are merely waste. Consequently, if these stems come into contact with a source of ritual impurity, they do not become impure and they do not transmit impurity to the attached grapes.
<b>And Rav Menashya bar Gadda said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said:</b> In the case of <b>one who harvests</b> grain <b>for roofing</b> a <i>sukka</i>, the grain <b>has no handles.</b> The legal status of the straw is not that of a handle for the grain. Since his interest is roofing his <i>sukka</i>, he wants only the straw, which is fit roofing, and not the grain, which is unfit. Therefore, in this context, the straw does not facilitate moving the grain.
The Gemara notes: <b>The one who said</b> that in the case of one who <b>harvests</b> grain, the straw is not a handle, <b>all the more so</b> would he say so in the case of one who <b>harvests</b> grapes, <b>since</b> the stems <b>are not suitable for his</b> needs. Stems are not wanted in the winepress, <b>so that they will not absorb wine.</b> By contrast, <b>the one who said</b> in the case of one who <b>harvests</b> grapes <b>that it has no handles,</b> he said so only in that case; <b>however,</b> in the case of <b>one who harvests</b> grain, he would say that <b>it has handles, since</b> the grain attached to the straw <b>is suitable for his</b> needs. He can <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with them</b> and weigh down the straw, <b>so that it does not scatter</b> in the wind.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say that</b> the statement of <b>Rav Menashya bar Gadda is</b> subject to a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Fig branches, and</b> there are <b>figs on them; vines, and</b> there are <b>grapes on them; straw, and</b> there are <b>stalks of grain on them; palm branches, and</b> there are <b>dates on them,</b> with regard to <b>them all, if the</b> amount of <b>waste is greater than</b> the amount of <b>the food,</b> a <i>sukka</i> roofed with them is <b>fit. And if not,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit. <i>Aḥerim</i> say:</b> The <i>sukka</i> is unfit <b>until the</b> amount of <b>straw is greater than</b> the combined amount of the handbreadth of <b>the handles</b> attached to the food that is susceptible to ritual impurity <b>and the food.</b>
The Gemara continues: <b>What, is it not</b> that <b>they disagree with regard to this: That</b> one <b>Sage,</b> <i>Aḥerim</i>, who said that the straw must be greater than the handles as well, <b>holds</b> that the produce designated for roofing <b>have handles; and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the first <i>tanna</i>, who disagrees, <b>holds that they do not have handles?</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Abba,</b> who says that grape clusters harvested for the winepress do not have handles, but grain harvested for roofing does, <b>it is certainly</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>.</b> Clearly, he holds in accordance with the opinion of <i>Aḥerim</i>, who hold that grain harvested for roofing has handles. However, <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Menashya bar Gadda,</b> who says that grain harvested for roofing does not have handles, <b>shall we say</b> that <b>it is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>,</b> and that he holds in accordance with the first <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i>? <b>Rav Menashya</b> could have <b>said to you that everyone agrees:</b> With regard to <b>one who harvests</b> grain for <b>roofing,</b> the grain <b>does not have handles. And here</b> in the <i>baraita</i>, <b>with what are we dealing?</b> It is a case <b>where one</b> initially <b>cut</b> the stalks <b>for food, and reconsidered</b> his plan <b>for them,</b> and decided to use them <b>for roofing.</b> Since initially, as food, the grain had handles, its status does not change despite his change of intent.
The Gemara asks: <b>If he cut them for food, what is the rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of the Rabbis</b> that the grain has no handles? As a rule, grain has handles. <b>And if you say</b> that <b>the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>once he reconsidered</b> his plan <b>for them</b> and decided to use them <b>for roofing, his</b> initial <b>intent was negated</b> and their legal status is like any other inedible roofing, and they consequently have no handles, the Gemara asks: <b>And was his</b> initial <b>intent negated in that</b> manner? <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna: <b>All vessels</b>

Daf 14a

<b>descend into their</b> state of <b>ritual impurity by means of thought?</b> Although an unfinished vessel ordinarily cannot become ritually impure, if the craftsman decided to leave it in its unfinished state, it immediately assumes the legal status of a completed vessel and can become ritually impure. <b>However, they ascend from their</b> state of <b>ritual impurity only by means of a change</b> resulting from an <b>action.</b> Merely deciding to complete the unfinished vessel does not alter its status. It loses its status as a vessel only when he takes action to complete it. <b>Action negates</b> status created by <b>action and</b> status created by <b>thought;</b> however, <b>thought negates neither</b> status created by <b>action nor</b> status created by <b>thought.</b> Therefore, once the straw of the grain harvested for food is considered a handle and is susceptible to ritual impurity, its status cannot be negated by thought alone.
<b>And if you say:</b> There is a distinction between the cases, as <b>this</b> principle <b>applies only</b> to <b>vessels, which are significant, but</b> with regard to <b>handles that are</b> not independently significant but are merely <b>for the purpose of</b> handling <b>food,</b> perhaps <b>by means of thought they become</b> handles <b>and by means of thought they emerge</b> from that status; <b>but didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna to the contrary? <b>All handles of food that one <i>besasan</i> on the threshing floor are ritually pure,</b> as through one’s actions he indicated that has no use for them and does not consider them significant. <b>And Rabbi Yosei deems</b> them capable of becoming <b>ritually impure.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: <b>Granted, according to the one who said</b> that <b><i>besasan</i></b> means that <b>one untied their binding,</b> it works out <b>well.</b> Although no action was performed on the sheaves, nevertheless, since their only purpose is to facilitate binding the sheaves, he indicated by unbinding them that the handles no longer suit his needs. <b>However, according to the one who said: What is</b> the meaning of <b><i>besasan</i>?</b> It means <b>he actually trampled them, what can be said?</b> According to that opinion, only an action can negate the status of the handles. What, then, is the rationale for the opinion of the Rabbis, who hold that thought alone can negate their status?
The Gemara answers: <b>Here too,</b> the dispute between the Rabbis and <i>Aḥerim</i> with regard to using grain for roofing the <i>sukka</i> is in a case <b>where one actually trampled them,</b> and that is the reason that they are no longer susceptible to ritual impurity. The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> and a change was made to the grain itself, <b>what is the rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of <i>Aḥerim</i>,</b> who nevertheless prohibit their use as roofing? The Gemara answers: It is <b>because</b> <i>Aḥerim</i> <b>state</b> their opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, as we learned</b> in the previously cited mishna: <b>Rabbi Yosei deems</b> them capable of becoming <b>ritually impure</b> even after trampling.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the basis of <b>this</b> comparison between the cases? <b>Granted, there,</b> in the dispute concerning the ritual impurity of the grain on the threshing floor, <b>the rationale</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei,</b> i.e., that the handles remain susceptible to ritual impurity, is that <b>they are suitable</b> for use. This is <b>in accordance with</b> the statement of <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish, as Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said:</b> Even after the grain is trampled the straw suits his needs, <b>since</b> the straw <b>is suited to</b> facilitate <b>turning over</b> the grain <b>with a pitchfork,</b> as the straw prevents the grain from falling between the prongs of the pitchfork.
<b>However, here,</b> where one needs the straw only for roofing the <i>sukka</i>, for <b>what are</b> the handles <b>suited</b> after they have been trampled? They serve no purpose in terms of handling the grain. The Gemara answers: <b>They are suited when one dismantles</b> the roofing, in order <b>to hold</b> the grain <b>by the straw,</b> so that it will scatter. Therefore, <i>Aḥerim</i> hold that the straw remains capable of contracting ritual impurity.
Apropos the dispute between the Rabbis and Rabbi Yosei, the Gemara discusses <b>the</b> matter <b>itself: All handles of food that one <i>besasan</i> on the threshing floor are ritually pure, and Rabbi Yosei deems</b> them capable of becoming <b>ritually impure.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of <b><i>besasan</i>? Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It means that <b>one actually trampled</b> them under foot. <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> It means <b>he untied their binding.</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>Granted, according to Rabbi Elazar, who said that <i>besasan</i></b> means that <b>he untied their binding, this is</b> the reason <b>that Rabbi Yosei deems</b> the handles <b>capable of contracting ritual impurity. However, according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said</b> that <b><i>besasan</i></b> means that one <b>actually trampled them, why does Rabbi Yosei deem</b> the handles <b>capable of contracting ritual impurity?</b> Didn’t one thereby render them insignificant? <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said:</b> Even after the grain is trampled, the straw suits his needs, <b>since</b> the straw <b>is suited to</b> facilitate <b>turning over</b> the grain <b>with a pitchfork.</b>
Apropos a pitchfork, the Gemara cites a related aggadic teaching: <b>Rabbi Elazar said: Why are the prayers of the righteous likened to a pitchfork [<i>eter</i>]?</b> It is written: “And Isaac entreated [<i>vayetar</i>] the Lord for his wife, because she was barren” (Genesis 25:21), <b>to say to you: Just as this pitchfork overturns the grain on the threshing floor from place to place, so too, the prayers of the righteous overturn the mind of the Holy One, Blessed be He, from the attribute of cruelty to the attribute of mercy,</b> and He accepts their prayers.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One</b> may <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with boards</b> like those used in the ceiling of a house; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Meir prohibits</b> their use. <b>If one placed a board that is four handbreadths wide atop</b> the <i>sukka</i>, the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b> He fulfills his obligation, <b>provided he does not sleep beneath</b> the board.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav said: The dispute is with regard to boards that have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, the standard size for boards used in house ceilings, as <b>Rabbi Meir is of</b> the opinion that the Sages issued <b>the decree of the roof.</b> In that case, the roofing of the <i>sukka</i> with boards that wide could be confused with a ceiling. If it were permitted to roof the <i>sukka</i> with a board that size, one might come to sleep beneath the ceiling of his own home during the Festival. <b>And Rabbi Yehuda is not of</b> the opinion that the Sages issued <b>the decree of the roof. However, with regard to boards that do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit. And Shmuel said: The dispute is with regard to boards that do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width; <b>however,</b> if <b>they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that it is unfit.</b>
The Gemara asks: According to Shmuel, the dispute is with regard to boards that <b>do not have four</b> handbreadths in their width, <b>and</b> apparently the same would hold true <b>even</b> if their width were <b>less than three</b> handbreadths. In that case, <b>aren’t they merely reeds;</b> why would Rabbi Meir prohibit their use?
<b>Rav Pappa said</b> that <b>this is what</b> Shmuel <b>is saying:</b> If <b>they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b> If their width is <b>less than three</b> handbreadths, <b>everyone agrees that</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit. What is the reason?</b> It is because <b>they are merely reeds. When they disagree</b> in the mishna, their disagreement pertains to a case where the boards are <b>from three to four</b> handbreadths wide. In that case, one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>holds</b> that <b>since they are not the measure of</b> a significant <b>place, we do not issue a decree</b> prohibiting their use. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>holds</b> that <b>since they have departed from the</b> halakhic <b>status of</b> being <b>joined [<i>lavud</i>],</b> which applies only to gaps of less than three handbreadths, <b>we issue a decree</b> prohibiting their use as roofing.
The Gemara cites proof with regard to the dispute between Rav and Shmuel. <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: <b>If one placed a board that is four handbreadths wide atop</b> the <i>sukka</i>, the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b> He fulfills his obligation, <b>provided he does not sleep beneath</b> the board. <b>Granted, according to Shmuel, who said</b> that <b>the dispute is with regard to boards that do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>however,</b> if <b>they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that it is unfit</b> roofing, it is <b>due to that</b> reason that <b>one should not sleep beneath</b> the board. <b>However, according to Rav,</b> who <b>said</b> that <b>the dispute is with regard to boards that have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>however,</b> if <b>they do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that it is fit, according to Rabbi Yehuda, why</b> may <b>one not sleep beneath it?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Do you hold</b> that this last <i>halakha</i> in the mishna, about not sleeping beneath the board, <b>is</b> a ruling with which <b>everyone,</b> including Rabbi Yehuda <b>agrees?</b> Rather, in <b>the latter clause</b> of the mishna <b>we have come to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir.</b> He alone, not Rabbi Yehuda, holds that one may not sleep beneath the board. Therefore, no proof can be cited from the mishna.
The Gemara cites an additional proof. <b>Come</b> and <b>hear: Two sheets</b> placed over the roofing of the <i>sukka</i> <b>join together</b> to constitute four handbreadths, the measure of unfit roofing that renders a <i>sukka</i> unfit.

Daf 14b

However, <b>two boards</b> placed on the <i>sukka</i> <b>do not combine. Rabbi Meir says: Even boards are like sheets,</b> in that they join together to constitute the measure of unfitness.
The Gemara elaborates: <b>Granted, according to Shmuel, who said</b> that <b>the dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Meir <b>is with regard to boards that do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>but</b> if <b>they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width <b>everyone agrees that it is unfit; what</b> is the meaning of that which Rabbi Meir said: Boards <b>join together?</b> It means that boards less than four handbreadths wide <b>combine to</b> measure <b>four</b> handbreadths, which renders the <i>sukka</i> unfit.
<b>However, according to Rav, who said</b> that <b>the dispute is with regard to boards that have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>but</b> if <b>they do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width <b>everyone agrees that it is fit, what are the circumstances? If</b> each of the boards <b>has four</b> handbreadths in its width, <b>why must they join together</b> to render the <i>sukka</i> unfit? If each board is four handbreadths wide, each is capable of rendering the <i>sukka</i> unfit on its own. And if each of the boards <b>does not have four</b> handbreadths in its width, <b>why</b> would Rabbi Meir prohibit their use? <b>But aren’t they merely reeds</b> according to Rav? Just as one may roof the <i>sukka</i> with reeds, one should be permitted to roof the <i>sukka</i> with these narrow boards.
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> explain <b>that there are four</b> handbreadths <b>in</b> the width of each board and each renders the <i>sukka</i> unfit on its own. However, <b>what</b> is the meaning of: Boards <b>join together?</b> It is with regard to a completely different matter. <b>They join together to</b> constitute <b>four cubits from the side.</b> If one placed these unfit boards adjacent to one of the walls of the <i>sukka</i>, they do not render the <i>sukka</i> unfit, due to the halakhic principle of curved wall, which views that roofing as an extension of that wall. However, that principle applies only up to four cubits of unfit roofing. If these boards join together to measure four cubits, the <i>sukka</i> is unfit according to Rabbi Meir. According to this explanation, the mishna can be explained in accordance with the opinion of Rav as well.
There is <b>another version</b> of the above exchange. <b>Granted, according to Shmuel, who said</b> that <b>the dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Meir <b>is with regard to boards that do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>but</b> if <b>they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that it is unfit, what</b> is the meaning of that which Rabbi Meir said: Boards <b>join together?</b> It means that <b>they join together to</b> constitute <b>four cubits from the side,</b> which renders the <i>sukka</i> unfit.
<b>However,</b> according <b>to Rav, granted,</b> according <b>to Rabbi Meir, what</b> is the meaning of: Boards <b>join together?</b> It means that <b>they join together to</b> constitute <b>four cubits from the side. However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who said that even</b> if <b>they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit, what</b> is the meaning of: Boards <b>do not join together? They are merely reeds,</b> which is fit roofing and fit roofing that joins together remains fit roofing. The Gemara answers: <b>Since Rabbi Meir</b> used the phrase: <b>Join together, Rabbi Yehuda,</b> although it is irrelevant according to his opinion, also <b>said: Do not join together.</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav,</b> and <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Shmuel.</b>
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav:</b> If <b>one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with cedar boards that do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees</b> that it is <b>fit.</b> If <b>there are four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>Rabbi Meir deems it unfit and Rabbi Yehuda deems it fit.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehuda said:</b> There was <b>an incident during a time of danger,</b> when the gentiles decreed that it is prohibited for Jews to construct a <i>sukka</i>, at <b>which</b> point <b>we brought boards that had four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>and we roofed the porch</b> with them so that it would not appear to be a <i>sukka</i>, <b>and we sat beneath them.</b> Evidently, boards four handbreadths wide are fit roofing for a <i>sukka</i>. <b>They said to him:</b> Is there <b>proof</b> to be cited <b>from there?</b> There is <b>no proof</b> from actions performed during <b>a time of danger.</b> It is possible that the <i>sukka</i> that they built on the porch was unfit, and they built it merely to commemorate the mitzva that they were unable to fulfill. From this <i>baraita</i>, it is apparent that the dispute between Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda is in a case of boards that are four handbreadths wide, in accordance with the opinion of Rav.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Shmuel:</b> If <b>one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with cedar boards that have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees</b> that the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b> If <b>there are not four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>Rabbi Meir deems it unfit and Rabbi Yehuda deems it fit. And Rabbi Meir concedes that, if</b> there is <b>between</b> one <b>board</b> and another <b>board</b> a gap the <b>complete</b> width <b>of a board,</b> then <b>one places</b> fit roofing from the <b>waste</b> of the threshing floor and the winepress, <b>and</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit. And Rabbi Yehuda concedes that if one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> with <b>a board that is four handbreadths wide</b> adjacent to one of the walls, the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit</b> based on the principle of curved wall; <b>and,</b> nevertheless, <b>one</b> may <b>not sleep beneath</b> that board, <b>and one who sleeps beneath it does not fulfill his obligation.</b> In any event, there are two <i>baraitot</i>, each in accordance with one of the two views presented.
§ <b>It is stated</b> that there is an amoraic dispute: If <b>one turned</b> the unfit boards <b>on their sides,</b> and the width of the side is less than the measure that renders them unfit, do the boards remain unfit, or are they fit because in their current placement their width is narrower? <b>Rav Huna said:</b> The <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit, and Rav Ḥisda and Rabba bar Rav Huna said:</b> It is <b>fit.</b>
The Gemara relates: <b>Rav Naḥman happened</b> to come <b>to Sura. Rav Ḥisda and Rabba bar Rav Huna entered before him. They said to him:</b> If <b>one turned</b> these boards <b>on their sides</b> and roofed the <i>sukka</i>, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? They sought to ascertain whether his ruling is in accordance with their opinion or in accordance with the opinion of Rav Huna. <b>He said to them:</b> The <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit;</b> since the boards are unfit roofing when placed flat, <b>their</b> legal status <b>became like</b> that of <b>skewers [<i>shapudin</i>] of metal,</b> which are unfit under all circumstances.
When they related this encounter to Rav Huna, <b>Rav Huna said to them: Didn’t I tell you that you should say</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with my</b> opinion? Even Rav Naḥman agrees with me. <b>They said to him: And did the Master actually say a reason</b> for this ruling <b>to us, and we did not accept it from him?</b> Rav Naḥman not only issued a ruling, he also explained his ruling to us. <b>He said to them: And did you ask me for the reason and I did not say</b> it to <b>you?</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>Let us say that</b> this <i>baraita</i> <b>supports</b> the opinion of Rav Huna: With regard to a <i>sukka</i> that <b>does not hold one’s head, most of his</b> body, <b>and his table;</b> a <i>sukka</i> whose wall <b>was breached with a breach large enough for a goat to jump through headlong,</b> i.e., three handbreadths; a <i>sukka</i> <b>that one placed atop</b> it <b>a board that is four handbreadths wide, even if he only introduced three handbreadths</b> of the board <b>into</b> the <i>sukka</i>, in all these cases, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b>
<b>What are the circumstances</b> of the case where one introduces only three handbreadths of a board that is four handbreadths wide? <b>What, is it not that he turned</b> the board <b>on its side,</b> thereby diminishing its width from four to three handbreadths, in accordance with the opinion of Rav Huna? The Gemara rejects this: <b>No, with what are we dealing here?</b> It is a case <b>where one placed</b> the board <b>over the entrance of the <i>sukka</i>,</b> where there is no wall. <b>He introduced three</b> handbreadths <b>into</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>and took one</b> handbreadth <b>out</b> of the <i>sukka</i>, <b>so that</b> the legal status of that part of the board would <b>be like</b> that of <b>roofing that protrudes from the <i>sukka</i>, and</b> the <i>halakha</i> is that the legal status of <b>any roofing that protrudes from the <i>sukka</i> is considered like that of the <i>sukka</i>.</b> However, since this board is not adjacent to the wall of a <i>sukka</i>, the principle of curved wall does not apply. Therefore, it is four handbreadths of unfit roofing; it is prohibited to sleep beneath that board, and the entire <i>sukka</i> is rendered unfit. Consequently, there is no support for or against the opinion of Rav Huna from this <i>baraita</i>.

Daf 15a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>a roof</b> made of boards that are four handbreadths wide <b>upon which there is no</b> coat of <b>plaster, Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel disagree with regard to the manner in which to render it fit. <b>Beit Shammai say: One moves</b> each board, and then it is considered as though he placed the board there for the sake of the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>, <b>and one</b> then <b>removes one</b> board <b>from among</b> the boards and replaces it with fit roofing. <b>Beit Hillel say:</b> One need not perform both actions; rather, <b>one</b> must either <b>move</b> the boards <b>or remove one from among</b> them. <b>Rabbi Meir says: One</b> only <b>removes one from among</b> them <b>and does not move</b> the others.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> according to <b>Beit Hillel, their reason</b> for initially prohibiting this roof is <b>due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared. If one moves</b> the boards, <b>he performs an action.</b> Likewise, <b>if he removes one</b> of the boards <b>from among</b> them, <b>he</b> also <b>performs an action.</b> Therefore, in both cases, he prepared the roofing and the <i>sukka</i> is fit. <b>However,</b> with regard to the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai, what is the rationale</b> for <b>their</b> prohibition against using the original ceiling for a <i>sukka</i>? <b>If</b> the rationale is also <b>due to</b> the principle: <b>Prepare</b> it, <b>and not from that which has already been prepared, one</b> action <b>should suffice.</b> Or <b>if</b> the rationale is <b>due to the decree of the roof,</b> lest one come to reside beneath a regular plastered ceiling inside a house, <b>removing one</b> board <b>from among</b> them should <b>suffice.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> the rationale is <b>due to the decree of the roof, and this is</b> what <b>they are saying: Although one moves</b> the boards, <b>if he removes one</b> board <b>from among</b> them, <b>yes,</b> it is fit; <b>if not, no,</b> it is unfit. Moving the boards is inconsequential. Removing one board from among them is all that is necessary.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: <b>Rabbi Meir says: One removes one from among</b> them <b>but does not move</b> the others. This indicates that the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir is</b> identical to the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai,</b> as according to the above explanation, Beit Shammai also hold that removing one of the boards and replacing it with fit roofing can render the <i>sukka</i> fit. It is unreasonable to say that Rabbi Meir would hold in accordance with the opinion of Beit Shammai, which is rejected.
The Gemara answers: <b>This is what</b> Rabbi Meir <b>is saying: Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel do not disagree on this matter.</b> They agree that the boards are prohibited due to the decree of the roof and that only by removing one of the boards is the <i>sukka</i> rendered fit. Rabbi Meir disagrees with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda that there is a dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna <b>teaching us?</b> Is it <b>that Rabbi Meir is of</b> the opinion that the Sages issued <b>the decree of the roof and Rabbi Yehuda is of</b> the opinion that the Sages did not issue <b>the decree of the roof? But didn’t they disagree about this once, as we learned</b> in the mishna above: <b>One</b> may <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with boards;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Meir prohibits</b> their use. The Gemara explained that the dispute is whether or not the Sages issued the decree of the roof.
<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> That is not the dispute, as <b>in the first clause,</b> i.e., in the earlier mishna, <b>we are dealing with</b> the case of <b>planed boards.</b> The rationale for their disagreement is not due to the decree of the roof; <b>but it is due to the decree of the vessels</b> that they <b>touched upon it.</b> The dispute is whether or not the Sages issued a decree prohibiting the use of planed beams in roofing the <i>sukka</i>, although as flat wooden vessels they are not susceptible to ritual impurity, lest one come to roof the <i>sukka</i> with vessels that are susceptible to ritual impurity.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according <b>to Rav Yehuda, who said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> If <b>one roofed</b> a <i>sukka</i> <b>with convex arrow</b> shafts, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit,</b> but if he roofed his <i>sukka</i> <b>with concave</b> arrow shafts, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit; and he did not issue a decree</b> and prohibit roofing with <b>convex</b> shafts <b>due to</b> the prohibition against roofing with <b>concave</b> shafts, <b>here too, let us not issue a decree</b> and prohibit roofing with <b>planed boards, due to</b> the prohibition against roofing with actual <b>vessels.</b>
<b>Rather,</b> according to Rav, <b>perforce you</b> must <b>say</b> that in <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna <b>they disagree with regard to the decree of the roof, and in the latter clause,</b> i.e., this mishna as well, <b>they disagree with regard to the decree of the roof.</b> Once again, the question arises: <b>Why do I</b> need them to <b>disagree</b> about the same issue <b>twice?</b>
The Gemara answers: Rather, <b>the latter clause</b> is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who is saying to Rabbi Meir: Why do you prohibit</b> roofing <b>with boards?</b> Is it <b>due to the decree of the roof? That</b> is the <b>reason according to Beit Shammai, who are of</b> the opinion that the Sages issued the decree. <b>But,</b> contrary to your opinion, <b>Beit Hillel do not issue the decree. And Rabbi Meir said</b> to Rabbi Yehuda: <b>Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel did not disagree with regard to this matter</b> at all. These are not two separate disputes; rather, it is one extended dispute.
The Gemara continues to ask: <b>This</b> works out <b>well according to Rav, who said</b> that <b>the dispute is</b> specifically <b>in</b> a case where the boards <b>have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width. He says <b>that Rabbi Meir is of</b> the opinion that the Sages issued <b>the decree of the roof, and Rabbi Yehuda is not of</b> the opinion that the Sages issued <b>the decree of the roof. However, according to Shmuel, who said</b> that <b>the dispute is</b> specifically <b>in</b> a case where the boards <b>do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>but where they have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees that</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is unfit,</b> and both Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda agree that the Sages issued the decree of the roof; if so, <b>in the latter clause</b> of the mishna, <b>with regard to what</b> matter <b>do they disagree?</b>
The Gemara answers: One may not use boards of this sort for roofing his <i>sukka</i>. Even according to Rabbi Yehuda, a <i>sukka</i> roofed in that manner is unfit, due to the decree of the roof. However, here, in the latter clause, it is <b>with regard to negating</b> an existing <b>roof</b> that consists of boards of this sort, in order to render the <i>sukka</i> fit that <b>they disagree.</b> One <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>holds:</b> The ceiling <b>is thereby negated,</b> by moving the boards, <b>and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>holds</b> that the ceiling <b>is not thereby negated</b> unless he also removes one beam from among them.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>one who roofs his <i>sukka</i> with</b> metal <b>skewers or with the long boards of the bed,</b> which compose its frame, <b>if there is space between</b> each one of <b>them equal to</b> the width of the skewers or the boards, and if he places fit roofing in those spaces, the <i>sukka</i> is <b>fit.</b> In the case of <b>one who hollows out</b> and creates a space inside <b>a stack of grain to establish a sukka for him,</b> it is <b>not a <i>sukka</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Let us say,</b> based on the mishna, <b>that this will be a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, as it is stated</b> that the <i>amora’im</i> disagreed concerning the following matter: With regard to the domains of Shabbat, if <b>the breached</b> segment <b>is equal to the standing</b> segment, is it deemed a partition or not? <b>Rav Pappa said: It is permitted</b> to carry within the partition; as long as the breached segment is not greater, it is considered a solid partition. <b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, said: It is prohibited</b> to carry within the partition, unless the standing portion is greater. Apparently, from the mishna, even if the fit roofing is equal to the unfit skewers and boards, the <i>sukka</i> is fit, contrary to the opinion of Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua.
The Gemara answers that <b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua,</b> could have <b>said to you: What</b> is the meaning of: <b>Like</b> the skewers and the boards? It does not mean that the space between the skewers and boards equals the width of the skewers and boards themselves. It is referring to a case <b>where</b> the space is large enough so that the fit roofing can <b>enter and emerge</b> easily, i.e., it is wider than the unfit roofing. According to this interpretation, the mishna can be explained according to Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, as well. The mishna agrees that even if the standing and breached areas are equal, the <i>sukka</i> is unfit.

Daf 15b

The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it possible to be precise?</b> The mishna need not be understood in that manner, since it is possible to calibrate the width of the spaces to equal the width of the unfit roofing, as the mishna required no more than that. <b>Rabbi Ami said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where one extends</b> the width of the spaces beyond the width of the unfit roofing. The mishna deems the <i>sukka</i> fit only if the width of the spaces is greater than the width of the unfit roofing.
<b>Rava said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the mishna is referring to a case <b>where one does not extend</b> the width of the spaces, and nevertheless, the fit roofing is greater than the unfit roofing, <b>if</b> the skewers <b>were placed lengthwise</b> across the <i>sukka</i>, <b>one places</b> the fit roofing <b>widthwise,</b> and if the skewers were placed <b>widthwise, one places the</b> fit roofing <b>lengthwise.</b> By doing so, the fit roofing overlaps the skewers at least somewhat; otherwise it would fall between the unfit roofing. Consequently, even if the space equals the unfit roofing, the fit roofing is greater than the unfit roofing.
§ The mishna continues: <b>Or with the long boards of the bed,</b> which compose its frame, the <i>sukka</i> is unfit. The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that the mishna <b>supports</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ami bar Tavyomei, as Rabbi Ami bar Tavyomei said:</b> If <b>one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with worn,</b> incomplete, <b>vessels,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b> Although these incomplete vessels are no longer susceptible to ritual impurity, they remain unfit because they were initially unfit for roofing. Proof can be adduced from the mishna: The long boards of the bed are no longer vessels but rather pieces from broken vessels; still, they may not be used for roofing the <i>sukka</i>.
The Gemara rejects this: The mishna is referring to a case <b>similar to</b> that <b>which Rabbi Ḥanan said</b> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said</b> in another context: It is not referring to the long boards alone. Rather, it is referring to a case <b>with the long board</b> of the bed <b>and two</b> of the <b>legs</b> attached to it <b>or</b> to a case <b>with the short board</b> of the bed <b>with two legs</b> attached to it. In this case, the structure could be propped up against a wall and used as a bed. <b>Here too,</b> the mishna is referring to roofing <b>with the long board and two legs or with the short board and two legs,</b> which are still considered complete vessels.
The Gemara asks: <b>Where is it stated</b> that <b>which Rabbi Ḥanan</b> said that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna:

Daf 16a

<b>A bed becomes ritually impure</b> as a complete <b>entity</b> if it comes into contact with a source of impurity. <b>And it becomes ritually pure as a</b> single <b>entity</b> through immersion, and in the case of impurity imparted by a corpse, through sprinkling and immersion. However, it may be neither impurified nor purified when dismantled. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. The Rabbis say: It becomes ritually impure</b> even when it is dismantled into its component <b>parts, and,</b> so too, <b>it becomes ritually pure</b> even when it is dismantled into its component <b>parts.</b> The Gemara asks: If the bed breaks into parts that serve no purpose, it is pure; <b>what are</b> these component parts mentioned by the Rabbis? <b>Rabbi Ḥanan said</b> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said:</b> The component parts are <b>a long board and two legs</b> attached to it <b>and a short board and two legs</b> attached to it.
The Gemara asks: <b>And for what</b> purpose <b>are</b> these parts <b>suited;</b> what function qualifies their status as vessels? The Gemara answers: It is possible for one <b>to lean them against the wall and to sit on them,</b> after placing boards across the top <b>and placing ropes</b> across their length and width. The boards of the bed can thereby be used for the purpose of sitting or lying upon them; consequently, they are considered vessels.
§ The Gemara returns to discuss <b>the</b> matter <b>itself</b> cited above. <b>Rabbi Ami bar Tavyomei said: If one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with worn,</b> incomplete, <b>vessels,</b> the <i>sukka</i> is <b>unfit.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What are</b> these <b>worn vessels? Abaye said:</b> They are small <b>cloths that do not have</b> an area of <b>three by three</b> fingerbreadths, <b>which,</b> due to their size, <b>are not suited</b> for use <b>either by the poor or by the wealthy.</b>
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ami bar Tavyomei:</b> In the case of <b>a mat</b> made <b>of</b> different types of vegetation, e.g., <b>papyrus and reed grass, even though its remnants were reduced from</b> the requisite <b>measure</b> for contracting ritual impurity, <b>one</b> may <b>not roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with them.</b> This precisely corresponds to the opinion of Rabbi Ami.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If <b>a mat of reeds</b> is <b>large</b> and not designated for sleeping, but is suited only for roofing, <b>one</b> may <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with it.</b> However, the status of <b>a small</b> mat, which can be utilized for sleeping, is that of a vessel, and <b>one</b> may <b>not roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with it. Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> The status of <b>even</b> a large mat is that of a vessel. It <b>is capable of contracting ritual impurity, and</b> therefore <b>one</b> may <b>not roof</b> his <i>sukka</i> <b>with it.</b>
The mishna states: In the case of <b>one who hollows out</b> and creates a space inside <b>a stack of grain,</b> it is not a <i>sukka</i>. <b>Rav Huna said:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> that it is not a <i>sukka</i> <b>only</b> in a case <b>where there is not a space one handbreadth</b> high <b>along seven</b> handbreadths upon which the grain was piled. <b>However, if there is a space</b> measuring <b>one handbreadth</b> high <b>along seven</b> handbreadths upon which the grain was piled, and now, by hollowing out the stack, one is raising the existing walls and not forming a new space, <b>it is</b> a fit <b><i>sukka</i>.</b>
<b>That is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who hollows out a stack of grain to make himself a <i>sukka</i>, it is a <i>sukka</i>.</b> The Gemara wonders: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna that <b>it is not a <i>sukka</i>? Rather, is it not</b> correct to <b>conclude from it, in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Huna,</b> that in certain circumstances it is possible to hollow out a stack of grain and establish a fit <i>sukka</i>? The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from it</b> that this is the case.
