Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i> is practiced only with the four species of the <i>lulav</i></b> as roofing. <b>And,</b> he claims, <b>logic dictates</b> that it is so, as it is derived by means of an <i>a fortiori</i> inference: <b>Just as</b> the mitzva of <b><i>lulav</i>, which is not practiced at night as it is during the day, is practiced only with the four species,</b> with regard to the mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i>, which is practiced at night as it is during the day, is it not right that its</b> roofing <b>should be only from the four species?</b>
The Rabbis <b>said to him:</b> That is not an <i>a fortiori</i> inference, as <b>any</b> <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference that you infer initially to be stringent, but ultimately</b> it is <b>to be lenient, is not a</b> legitimate <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b> If ultimately the stringency leads to a leniency, the entire basis of the inference is undermined.

Daf 37a

According to your reasoning, if <b>one did not find</b> any of the <b>four species</b> to roof his <i>sukka</i>, <b>he will sit idly</b> and fail to fulfill the mitzva of <i>sukka</i>; <b>and the Torah states: “You shall reside in <i>sukkot</i></b> for seven days” (Leviticus 23:42), meaning <b>a <i>sukka</i> of any material. Likewise, in</b> the book of <b>Ezra,</b> which can refer also to the book of Nehemiah, <b>it says: “Go forth unto the mount, and fetch olive branches, and pine branches, and myrtle branches, and palm branches, and branches of a dense-leaved tree, to make <i>sukkot</i>, as it is written”</b> (Nehemiah 8:15). Apparently, a <i>sukka</i> may be constructed even with materials other than the four species.
<b>And Rabbi Yehuda holds: These</b> olive branches and pine branches mentioned in the verse were <b>for the walls</b> of the <i>sukka</i>, which need not be built from the four species. <b>Myrtle branches, palm branches, and branches of a dense-leaved tree,</b> i.e., again myrtle, all of which are among the four species, were <b>for the roofing.</b> Rabbi Yehuda holds that one may roof the <i>sukka</i> only with the four species. <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna: <b>One</b> may <b>roof</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with boards;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b> As boards can be produced from one of the four species only if the trunk of the date palm is considered a <i>lulav</i>, <b>apparently, fibers and the trunk of the date palm are the species of the <i>lulav</i>.</b> The Gemara determines: Indeed, <b>conclude from it</b> that this is so.
The Gemara wonders: <b>And did Rabbi Yehuda say</b> with regard to the materials fit for roofing a <i>sukka</i> that <b>the four species, yes,</b> they are fit, but <b>other materials, no,</b> they are not fit? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one roofed</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>with cedar [<i>erez</i>] boards that have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>everyone agrees</b> that it is <b>unfit.</b> If <b>they do not have four</b> handbreadths <b>in their</b> width, <b>Rabbi Meir deems it unfit and Rabbi Yehuda deems it fit. And Rabbi Meir concedes that if there is between</b> one <b>board</b> and another <b>board</b> a gap the <b>complete</b> width <b>of a board,</b> then <b>one places</b> fit roofing from the <b>waste</b> of the threshing floor and the winepress <b>between</b> the boards <b>and</b> the <i>sukka</i> <b>is fit.</b> Apparently, Rabbi Yehuda permits one to roof the <i>sukka</i> with cedar wood, which is not one of the four species.
The Gemara responds: <b>What is</b> the <b><i>erez</i></b> to which the mishna refers? It is in fact a <b>myrtle</b> tree, <b>in accordance with</b> that <b>which Rabba bar Rav Huna</b> said, <b>as Rabba bar Rav Huna said</b> that <b>they say in the school of Rav: There are ten types of <i>erez</i>, as it is stated: “I will place in the wilderness the cedar [<i>erez</i>], the acacia-tree, the myrtle,</b> and pine tree; I will set in the plain the juniper, the box-tree, and the cypress all together” (Isaiah 41:19). All the trees listed in this verse are types of cedar, and the myrtle is one of them.
§ The mishna continues: <b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> One may tie the <i>lulav</i> <b>even with a cord. It is taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> that <b>Rabbi Meir said:</b> There was <b>an incident involving the prominent</b> residents <b>of Jerusalem who would bind their <i>lulavim</i> with gold rings.</b> The Sages <b>said to him:</b> Is there <b>proof from there? They would bind it with its own species beneath</b> the rings, which serve a merely decorative purpose and not a halakhic one.
<b>Rabba said to those who</b> would <b>bind the four species [<i>hoshana</i>] of the house of the Exilarch: When you bind the four species of the house of the Exilarch, leave</b> room for <b>a handgrip on it</b> where there is neither binding nor decoration <b>so that there will not be an interposition</b> between the <i>lulav</i> and the hand of the person taking it.
<b>Rava said:</b> That is unnecessary, as <b>any</b> addition whose purpose is <b>to beautify does not interpose. And Rabba said: Let a person not take the four species with a cloth [<i>sudara</i>]</b> around his hand, <b>since I require a complete taking, and there is none</b> in this case due to the interposition between his hand and the <i>lulav</i>. <b>And Rava said:</b> That is not a problem, as <b>taking by means of another object is considered taking.</b>
<b>Rava said: From where do I say that taking by means of another object is considered taking?</b> It is <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna: One undergoing purification from impurity imparted by a corpse must be sprinkled with purification water with the ashes of the red heifer. If the <b>hyssop</b> used to sprinkle the water was <b>short</b> and did not reach the water in the receptacle, <b>one renders it sufficiently</b> long <b>by</b> attaching <b>a string or a spindle, and</b> then he <b>dips</b> the hyssop into the water, <b>removes</b> it, <b>grasps the hyssop, and sprinkles</b> the water on the one undergoing purification. And <b>why</b> may he do so? Doesn’t <b>the Merciful One say</b> in the Torah: <b>“And</b> a ritually pure person shall <b>take</b> hyssop, <b>and dip</b> it in the water, and sprinkle it” (Numbers 19:18), indicating that one must take the hyssop while dipping it? <b>Rather,</b> may one <b>not conclude from</b> this that <b>taking by means of another object is considered taking?</b>
This proof is rejected: <b>From where</b> can that be proven? <b>Perhaps it is different there; since he attached</b> the string to the hyssop, its legal status <b>is like</b> that of the hyssop <b>itself.</b> However, the legal status of the cloth is not like that of the <i>lulav</i>, since it is not attached to the <i>lulav</i>. <b>Rather,</b> the fact that taking by means of another object is considered taking can be learned <b>from here:</b> If the ashes of the red heifer <b>fell from the tube</b> in which they were held <b>into the trough</b> in which the spring water was located, the water is <b>unfit,</b> since taking the ashes and placing them in the water must be performed intentionally.

Daf 37b

By inference, if <b>he spilled</b> the ashes intentionally from the tube into the water, <b>it is fit. Why?</b> Doesn’t <b>the Merciful One say</b> in the Torah: <b>“And</b> for the impure <b>they shall take</b> of the ashes of the burning of the purification from sin, <b>and he places</b> running water upon them in a vessel” (Numbers 19:17). Apparently, one must mix the water and the ashes intentionally. <b>Rather,</b> may one <b>not conclude from it</b> that <b>taking by means of another object is considered taking?</b>
<b>And Rabba said</b> with regard to the <i>lulav</i>: After binding the myrtle branches and willow branches, <b>let a person not insert the <i>lulav</i> into</b> the binding of <b>the four species, as perhaps</b> as a result <b>the leaves</b> will <b>fall</b> from the branches <b>and</b> the leaves <b>will</b> constitute <b>an interposition</b> between the various species. <b>And Rava said:</b> An object of one <b>species does not interpose before</b> an object of the same <b>species.</b>
<b>And Rabba said: Let a person not cut the <i>lulav</i></b> in order to shorten it while it is <b>in</b> the binding of <b>the four species, as</b> perhaps as a result <b>leaves will become detached and will</b> constitute <b>an interposition</b> between the various species. <b>And Rava said:</b> An object of one <b>species does not interpose before</b> an object of the same <b>species.</b>
§ <b>And Rabba said: It is prohibited to smell the myrtle branch</b> used in fulfillment <b>of the mitzva.</b> However, <b>it is permitted to smell the <i>etrog</i></b> used in fulfillment <b>of the mitzva.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for the distinction between them? The Gemara answers: With regard to <b>a myrtle branch, which exists</b> primarily <b>for</b> its <b>fragrance, when he sets it aside</b> exclusively for the mitzva, <b>he sets it aside from</b> enjoying its <b>fragrance.</b> With regard to <b>an <i>etrog</i>,</b> on the other hand, <b>which exists</b> primarily <b>for eating, when he sets it aside</b> exclusively for the mitzva, <b>he sets it aside from eating.</b> However, he never intended to prohibit this ancillary pleasure.
<b>And Rabba said:</b> With regard to <b>a myrtle branch, while it is attached</b> to the tree, it is <b>permitted to smell it</b> on Shabbat. With regard to <b>an <i>etrog</i>, while it is attached</b> to the tree, <b>it is prohibited to smell it.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for the difference between them? With regard to <b>a myrtle branch, which exists</b> primarily <b>to smell</b> it, <b>if you permit him</b> to smell it, <b>he will not come to cut it.</b> Once he has smelled it, he has no further use for it. With regard to <b>an <i>etrog</i>, which exists</b> primarily <b>for eating,</b> one may not smell it because <b>if you permit him</b> to do so, the concern is that <b>he will come to cut it</b> from the tree to eat it.
§ <b>And Rabba said:</b> One takes the <b><i>lulav</i></b> bound with the other two species <b>in the right</b> hand <b>and the <i>etrog</i> in the left.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for that arrangement? <b>These</b> species constitute <b>three mitzvot, and this</b> <i>etrog</i> <b>is</b> only <b>one mitzva.</b> One accords deference to the greater number of mitzvot by taking the three species in the right hand. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said to Rabbi Zerika: What is the reason</b> that <b>we recite the blessing only</b> with the formula: <b>About taking the <i>lulav</i>,</b> with no mention of the other species? Rabbi Zerika said to him: <b>Since it</b> is <b>highest of them all</b> and the most conspicuous, the other species are subsumed under it. Rabbi Yirmeya asks: <b>And</b> if that is the only reason, <b>let him lift the <i>etrog</i></b> higher than the <i>lulav</i> <b>and recite the blessing</b> mentioning it. Rabbi Zerika <b>said to him</b> that he meant: <b>Since</b> the tree <b>of its species is the tallest of them all,</b> it is the most prominent, and therefore it is appropriate for the formula of the blessing to emphasize the <i>lulav</i>.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>And where</b> in the recitation of <i>hallel</i> <b>would they wave</b> the <i>lulav</i>? They would do so <b>at</b> the verse: <b>“Thank the Lord,</b> for He is good” (Psalms 118:1, 29) that appears <b>at</b> both <b>the beginning and the end</b> of the psalm, <b>and at</b> the verse: <b>“Lord, please save us”</b> (Psalms 118:25); this is <b>the statement of Beit Hillel. And Beit Shammai say:</b> They would wave the <i>lulav</i> <b>even at</b> the verse: <b>“Lord, please grant us success”</b> (Psalms 118:25). <b>Rabbi Akiva said: I was observing Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua</b> and saw <b>that all the people were waving their <i>lulavim</i>, and</b> the two of <b>them waved</b> their <i>lulav</i> <b>only at: “Lord, please save us,”</b> indicating that this is the <i>halakha</i>.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks about the premise of the mishna. With regard to <b>waving, who mentioned it?</b> As no previous mention was made of waving the <i>lulav</i>, it is a non sequitur when the <i>tanna</i> begins discussion of the details of the custom. The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> <b>is basing</b> himself on the mishna <b>there</b> (29b), which states: <b>Any <i>lulav</i> that has three handbreadths</b> in length, <b>sufficient</b> to enable one <b>to wave with it, is fit</b> for use in fulfilling the mitzva. As the custom of waving the <i>lulav</i> was already established there, here the <i>tanna</i> <b>is saying: Where would they wave</b> the <i>lulav</i>?
<b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Menaḥot</i> 61a): With regard to <b>the two loaves and the two lambs</b> offered on the festival of <b><i>Shavuot</i>, how does he perform</b> their waving before the altar? <b>He places the two loaves atop the two lambs, and places his hand beneath them, and waves to and fro</b> to each side, <b>and he raises and lowers</b> them, <b>as it is stated: “Which is waved and which is lifted”</b> (Exodus 29:27), indicating that there is waving to the sides as well as raising and lowering.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: He moves</b> them <b>to and fro</b> to dedicate them <b>to He Whom the four directions are His. He raises and lowers</b> them <b>to He Whom the heavens and earth are His. In the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, <b>they taught</b> it <b>as follows. Rabbi Ḥama bar Ukva said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said: He moves</b> them <b>to and fro in order to</b> request <b>a halt</b> to <b>harmful winds,</b> storms and tempests that come from all directions; <b>he raises and lowers</b> them <b>in order to halt harmful dews</b> and rains that come from above. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Avin said, and some say</b> that it was <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Zevila</b> who said: <b>That is to say,</b>

Daf 38a

<b>non-essential aspects of a mitzva avert calamity, as waving is a non-essential aspect of the mitzva,</b> since even if one failed to wave the loaves he fulfilled his obligation, <b>and</b> nevertheless <b>it halts harmful winds and dews. And Rava said: And likewise</b> one should conduct himself the same way <b>with a <i>lulav</i>,</b> i.e., one should wave it to and fro and raise and lower it for the same reasons. When <b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov</b> would <b>move</b> the <i>lulav</i> <b>to and fro, he would say: This is an arrow in the eye of Satan,</b> as despite his best efforts, the Jewish people continue to joyously fulfill mitzvot. The Gemara notes: <b>That is not</b> a proper <b>manner</b> of conduct, <b>as</b> it will induce Satan to <b>come to incite him</b> to sin. Gloating due to his victory over the evil inclination will lead Satan to redouble his efforts to corrupt him.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>one who was coming</b> along <b>the way and did not have a <i>lulav</i> in his hand to take</b> and fulfill the mitzva while traveling, <b>when he enters his house</b> to eat, <b>he</b> should <b>take</b> the <i>lulav</i> <b>at his table.</b> He interrupts his meal to fulfill the mitzva of <i>lulav</i>. <b>If he did not take</b> the <i>lulav</i> in the <b>morning, he</b> should <b>take it in the afternoon, as the entire day is suited for</b> fulfilling the mitzva of <b><i>lulav</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara analyzes the mishna. On one hand, <b>you said</b> that if he did not take the <i>lulav</i> before the meal then <b>he takes it at his table. That is to say that</b> if remembers that he did not yet take the <i>lulav</i>, <b>he interrupts</b> his meal, takes the <i>lulav</i>, and then continues his meal. <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 9b): One may not begin to eat before he recites the afternoon prayer; however, <b>if they started</b> a meal, <b>they need not interrupt</b> the meal in order to pray. <b>Rav Safra said:</b> This is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>that</b> mishna, where one need not interrupt his meal, is referring to a case <b>where there is opportunity</b> to pray later <b>in the day; this</b> mishna, where one must interrupt his meal, is referring to a case <b>where there is no opportunity</b> to take the <i>lulav</i> later <b>in the day.</b> In that case, one must fulfill the mitzva immediately.
<b>Rava said: What is the difficulty?</b> The two cases are different, and there is no contradiction at all. <b>Perhaps this</b> mitzva of <i>lulav</i> <b>is</b> a mitzva <b>by Torah law,</b> and therefore one must interrupt his meal to take the <i>lulav</i>, while <b>that</b> mitzva to recite the afternoon prayer <b>is</b> a mitzva <b>by rabbinic law,</b> and therefore one need not interrupt his meal to pray. <b>Rather, Rava said: If</b> there is <b>a difficulty,</b> i.e., a contradiction, this is the <b>difficulty:</b> In the first clause in the mishna it says that <b>when he enters his house</b> to eat, <b>he</b> should <b>take</b> the <i>lulav</i> <b>at his table. Apparently, one</b> must <b>interrupt</b> his meal. <b>And then</b> in the latter clause of the mishna it <b>is taught: If he did not take</b> the <i>lulav</i> in the <b>morning, he</b> should <b>take it in the afternoon. Apparently, he</b> need <b>not interrupt</b> his meal.
Resolving the contradiction, <b>Rav Safra said:</b> This is <b>not difficult. This</b> clause, where one need not interrupt his meal, is referring to a case <b>where there is opportunity</b> to take the <i>lulav</i> later <b>in the day; that</b> clause, where one must interrupt his meal, is referring to a case <b>where there is no opportunity</b> to take the <i>lulav</i> later <b>in the day.</b>
<b>Rabbi Zeira said: What is the difficulty?</b> There is no contradiction, as <b>perhaps</b> the mishna is teaching that there is <b>a mitzva to interrupt</b> one’s meal and take the <i>lulav</i>; <b>but if he did not interrupt</b> his meal <b>he</b> should <b>take it in the afternoon, as the entire day is suited for</b> fulfilling the mitzva of <b><i>lulav</i>. Rather, Rabbi Zeira said: Actually,</b> the contradiction is <b>as we said initially,</b> between the ruling with regard to <i>lulav</i> and the ruling with regard to the afternoon prayer. <b>And</b> as to <b>that which you found difficult,</b> i.e., there is no contradiction at all, as <b>this</b> mitzva of <i>lulav</i> <b>is</b> a mitzva <b>by Torah law</b> and <b>that</b> mitzva to recite the afternoon prayer <b>is</b> a mitzva <b>by rabbinic law,</b> that is not difficult; as <b>here,</b> in the case of <i>lulav</i>, <b>we are dealing with the second day of the Festival</b> and beyond, during the intermediate days, when the mitzva of <i>lulav</i> is <b>by rabbinic law.</b> The contradiction is therefore between the rulings pertaining to two mitzvot by rabbinic law.
The language of the mishna <b>is also precise</b> and indicates that it is dealing with the intermediate days of the Festival <b>from</b> the fact <b>that it teaches: One who was coming along the way and does not have a <i>lulav</i> in his hand. As, if it enters your mind</b> to say that the mishna is referring to <b>the first day of the Festival, is it permitted</b> to travel a long distance on that day? Rather, it is referring to the intermediate days.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>one for whom a Canaanite slave, a woman, or a minor was reciting</b> <i>hallel</i>, <b>he repeats after them what they are saying</b> word for word. The mishna notes: <b>And may a curse come to him</b> for being so ignorant that he needs them to recite it for him. <b>If an adult male was reciting</b> <i>hallel</i> <b>on his</b> behalf, he need not repeat each word, as the adult male can fulfill the obligation to recite <i>hallel</i> on his behalf. Rather, <b>he</b> simply <b>answers: <i>Halleluya</i>,</b> to each phrase that is recited. In <b>a place where they were accustomed to repeat</b> certain verses, <b>he,</b> too, should <b>repeat</b> them. If the custom is <b>to</b> recite them <b>plainly,</b> without repetition, <b>he</b> should recite them <b>plainly. In a place where the custom is to recite a blessing</b> when reciting <i>hallel</i>, <b>he should recite a blessing. Everything is in accordance with the local custom</b> in these matters.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught: Actually, they said</b> that <b>a son may recite a blessing</b> on behalf of <b>his father, and a slave may recite a blessing</b> on behalf of <b>his master, and a woman may recite a blessing</b> on behalf of <b>her husband, but the Sages said: May a curse come to a man</b> who, due to his ignorance, requires <b>his wife and children to recite a blessing on his behalf.</b>
<b>Rava said:</b>

Daf 38b

<b>Many significant <i>halakhot</i> can be learned from the custom of <i>hallel</i></b> based on the manner in which it was recited. In reciting <i>hallel</i> there are allusions to several halakhic matters and customs that the Sages instituted due to circumstances extant at the time. Although due to increased literacy and familiarity with the <i>hallel</i> liturgy the reasons no longer apply, these customs remain in practice. The prayer leader <b>recites: “<i>Halleluya</i>”</b> (Psalms 113:1), <b>and</b> the congregation <b>recites: <i>Halleluya</i>,</b> in response. <b>From here</b> is the source <b>that</b> there is <b>a mitzva to respond: <i>Halleluya</i>.</b>
Likewise, the prayer leader <b>recites: “Give praise, servants of the Lord”</b> (Psalms 113:1), <b>and</b> the congregation <b>recites: <i>Halleluya</i>,</b> in response. <b>From here</b> is the source of the <i>halakha</i> cited in the mishna <b>that if an adult male was reciting</b> <i>hallel</i> <b>on his</b> behalf, <b>he answers: <i>Halleluya</i>. He recites: “Thank the Lord,</b> for He is good” (Psalms 118:1), <b>and they respond: “Thank the Lord,</b> for He is good.” <b>From here</b> is the source <b>that</b> there is <b>a mitzva to respond</b> by reciting <b>the beginnings of chapters. It was also stated</b> that <b>Rav Ḥanan bar Rava said:</b> There is <b>a mitzva to respond</b> by reciting <b>the beginnings of chapters.</b>
Rava continued to cite the significant <i>halakhot</i> learned from <i>hallel</i>. The prayer leader <b>recites: “Lord, please save us”</b> (Psalms 118:25), <b>and</b> the congregation <b>recites: “Lord, please save us,”</b> in response. <b>From here</b> is the source of the <i>halakha</i> cited in the mishna <b>that if a minor was reciting</b> a portion that is not from the beginning of a chapter <b>on one’s</b> behalf, <b>he recites after him</b> precisely <b>what he says.</b>
The prayer leader <b>recites: “Lord, please grant us success,” and</b> the congregation <b>recites</b> in response: “<b>Lord, please grant us success”</b> (Psalms 118:25). <b>From here</b> is the source of the <i>halakha</i> <b>that if</b> one <b>comes to repeat</b> a particular verse in <i>hallel</i> twice, <b>he</b> may <b>repeat</b> it. The prayer leader <b>recites: “Blessed is one who comes”</b> (Psalms 118:26), <b>and</b> the congregation <b>recites</b> the rest of the verse: <b>“In the name of the Lord”</b> (Psalms 118:26), in response. <b>From here</b> is the source of the <i>halakha</i> that the halakhic status of <b>one who hears</b> a passage recited <b>is equivalent to</b> that of <b>one who recites</b> it, as the congregation fulfills its obligation even though it does not repeat the entire verse.
Apropos this <i>halakha</i>, the Gemara relates that the Sages <b>raised a dilemma before Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba:</b> If <b>one heard</b> a passage recited <b>and did not recite</b> it himself, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Did he fulfill his obligation or not? <b>He said to them</b> that <b>the Sages, and the schoolteachers, and the heads of the nation, and the</b> homiletic <b>interpreters said: One who heard</b> a passage recited <b>and did not recite</b> it himself <b>fulfilled</b> his obligation.
<b>It was also stated</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Pazi said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said in the name of Bar Kappara: From where</b> is it derived that the halakhic status of <b>one who hears</b> a passage recited <b>is equivalent to</b> that of <b>one who recites</b> it? It is <b>as it is written: “All the words of the book which</b> the king of Judea <b>has read”</b> (II Kings 22:16). <b>And did</b> King <b>Josiah read them? Didn’t Shaphan read them, as it is written: “And Shaphan read it before the king”</b> (II Kings 22:10)? <b>Rather, from here</b> it is derived <b>that</b> the halakhic status of <b>one who hears</b> a passage recited <b>is equivalent to</b> that of <b>one who recites</b> it, and it is as though Josiah read the words himself.
The Gemara asks: <b>And perhaps after Shaphan read them Josiah read</b> them again? <b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said:</b> It should <b>not enter your mind</b> to say so, <b>as it is written: “Because your heart was tender and you humbled yourself before the Lord when you heard</b> what I spoke in this place” (II Kings 22:19). The Gemara infers: <b>“When you heard”</b> is written in the verse, <b>and not: When you read.</b> In other words, immediately upon hearing Shaphan read the text, King Josiah sent for Huldah the prophetess, which shows that he humbled his heart. Clearly, the halakhic status of one who hears a passage recited is equivalent to that of one who recites it.
Apropos <i>hallel</i>, the Gemara cites additional <i>halakhot</i>. <b>Rava said: Let a person not recite: “Blessed is one who comes,” and then,</b> after pausing, recite: <b>“In the name of the Lord.” Rather,</b> let him recite without pause: <b>“Blessed is one who comes in the name of the Lord.” Rav Safra said to</b> Rava:

Daf 39a

You, who are as great in this generation as <b>Moses, did you speak well?</b> It is not so; <b>rather,</b> both <b>there and here,</b> whether he recites it with or without pause, the latter part of the verse <b>is the conclusion of the matter, and we have no</b> problem <b>with it,</b> as it is clear that his intention is to recite the entire verse: “Blessed is one who comes in the name of the Lord.” <b>Rava said: Let a person not recite</b> in the <i>kaddish</i> prayer: <b>May His great name, and then,</b> after pausing, recite: <b>Be blessed. Rather,</b> let him recite without pause: <b>May His great name be blessed. Rav Safra said to</b> Rava: You, who are as great in this generation as <b>Moses, did you speak well?</b> It is not so, <b>rather,</b> both <b>there and here,</b> whether he recites it with or without pause, the latter part of the verse <b>is the conclusion of the matter, and we have no</b> problem <b>with it.</b>
§ The mishna continues: <b>In a place where they were accustomed to repeat</b> certain verses he too should repeat them. It was <b>taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i>: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>repeats</b> certain <b>matters in</b> <i>hallel</i>. <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Perata adds</b> certain <b>matters in</b> <i>hallel</i>. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> does <b>he add? Abaye said: He continues repeating</b> additional verses in <i>hallel</i>, those <b>from: I will thank You, and onward</b> until the end of the psalm, as is the custom even today.
§ The mishna continues: In a place where the custom is <b>to recite a blessing</b> when reciting hallel, <b>he should recite a blessing. Abaye said:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> that the obligation to recite a blessing is dependent on custom <b>only</b> with regard to the blessing recited <b>after</b> <i>hallel</i>. <b>However, before</b> <i>hallel</i>, there is <b>a mitzva to recite a blessing, as Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> With regard to <b>all</b> the mitzvot, <b>one recites a blessing over them prior to [<i>over</i>] their performance.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where may it be inferred that</b> the word <b><i>over</i> is the language of priority?</b> It is <b>as Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said that it is written: “And Ahimaaz ran by the way of the plain and overtook [<i>vaya’avor</i>] the Cushite”</b> (II Samuel 18:23). <b>Abaye said:</b> It is derived <b>from here: “And he passed [<i>avar</i>] before them” (</b>Genesis 33:3). <b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that the proof is <b>from here: “And their king passed [<i>vaya’avor</i>] before them and the Lord at their head”</b> (Micah 2:13).
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>one who purchases a <i>lulav</i> from another</b> who is an <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>during the Sabbatical Year,</b> the seller <b>gives him an <i>etrog</i></b> along with it <b>as a gift, as he is not permitted to purchase</b> the <i>etrog</i> <b>during the Sabbatical Year</b> because it is prohibited to engage in commerce with Sabbatical-Year produce.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: If, the seller <b>did not want to give him</b> the <i>etrog</i> <b>as a gift, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? How should the buyer purchase the <i>etrog</i>? <b>Rav Huna said: He incorporates the cost of the <i>etrog</i> into</b> the price of the <b><i>lulav</i>.</b> He should purchase the <i>lulav</i> at an inflated price to cover the cost of the <i>etrog</i> as well. The Gemara asks: <b>And let</b> the buyer <b>give</b> the seller the money for the <i>etrog</i> <b>directly;</b> why employ artifice in the transaction?
The Gemara answers: That is necessary <b>because one may not transfer money</b> used to purchase <b>Sabbatical-Year produce to an <i>am ha’aretz</i>,</b> lest he make improper use of money that has sanctity of the Sabbatical Year. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One</b> may <b>not transfer to an <i>am ha’aretz</i> money</b> used to purchase <b>Sabbatical-Year produce</b> that is worth <b>more than</b> the value of <b>food</b> sufficient for <b>three meals.</b> One may use money that has sanctity of the Sabbatical Year to purchase food for his personal use. If the money is sufficient for three meals, presumably the seller will use it in a permitted manner. <b>And if</b> the buyer <b>transferred</b> more money than that, <b>he</b> should <b>say: This money is deconsecrated</b> by my redeeming it in exchange for non-Sabbatical-Year <b>produce that I have in my house.</b>

Daf 39b

<b>And</b> then <b>he comes</b> home <b>and eats</b> the produce <b>in</b> the appropriate manner and at the appropriate time, due to <b>the sanctity of Sabbatical-Year</b> produce.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said</b> that it is permitted to transfer money used to purchase Sabbatical-Year produce to an <i>am ha’aretz</i> as long as it does not exceed the value of three meals? It is specifically in a case <b>where one purchases</b> produce that came <b>from</b> a field that was declared <b>ownerless</b> as required during the Sabbatical Year. In that case, the <i>am ha’aretz</i> who gathered the produce is paid only for the act of harvesting and not for the produce. <b>However,</b> if <b>he buys</b> produce that came <b>from</b> a field <b>that was safeguarded</b> for its owner in the manner that it is during the other years of the Sabbatical-Year cycle and was not declared ownerless, then <b>even</b> if one purchased produce <b>worth half an <i>issar</i>, it is prohibited</b> to transfer the money to him, as it is prohibited to utilize fruits that were safeguarded during the Sabbatical Year.
<b>Rav Sheshet raised an objection: And</b> is it permitted to purchase produce <b>from</b> an <b>ownerless</b> field worth only the value of <b>three meals and no more? He raised a contradiction</b> from a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 9:1): <b>Rue and sorrel,</b> two types of herbs, and vegetables such as <b>asparagus, purslane, coriander that is</b> found <b>in the mountains, water parsley of the rivers, and garden-eruca</b> are all <b>exempt from</b> the requirement of <b>tithes</b> in all years, <b>and they may be purchased from any person during the Sabbatical Year because</b> there is <b>no</b> plant <b>of their</b> species <b>that is safeguarded.</b> These plants are not cultivated but grow wild, rendering them ownerless. Apparently, these plants that grow wild may be purchased in any quantity, even from an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, with no three-meal limit.
The Gemara continues. Rav Sheshet <b>raised the objection, and he</b> also <b>resolved it:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> in the mishna with regard to food in the amount <b>sufficient</b> for his <b>sustenance [<i>man</i>].</b> These plants that the mishna excludes from the prohibition against purchase from an <i>am ha’aretz</i> are still subject to the three-meal limit. <b>And likewise, Rabba bar bar Ḥana said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> in the amount <b>sufficient</b> for his <b>sustenance [<i>man</i>]. From where may it be inferred that <i>man</i> is a term</b> meaning <b>sustenance?</b> It is <b>as it is written: “And the king appointed [<i>vayman</i>] for them</b> a daily portion of the king’s food” (Daniel 1:5).
§ The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> if one may not purchase produce from an <i>am ha’aretz</i> lest he misuse the money, it should <b>also</b> be prohibited to give him money and purchase <b>a <i>lulav</i></b> from him during the Sabbatical Year. The Gemara answers: The mishna is dealing with a case where the <b><i>lulav</i> is of the sixth</b> year <b>that is entering the seventh</b> year. As it grew during the sixth year, it is permitted, even though it was removed from the tree during the seventh year. The fact that it remained on the tree between Rosh HaShana and <i>Sukkot</i> does not render it Sabbatical-Year produce. The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> the <b><i>etrog</i>, too, is</b> an object <b>of the sixth</b> year <b>that is entering the seventh</b> year and should have the same status. The Gemara answers: With regard to <b>an <i>etrog</i>,</b> as opposed to a <i>lulav</i>, in determining its status <b>we go</b> according to its <b>picking</b> and not when it grew. Therefore, in that case, the <i>etrog</i> is considered to be Sabbatical-Year produce.
The Gemara objects: <b>But both Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Eliezer,</b> who disagree about the status of an <i>etrog</i> that grew in one year and was picked in the following year in terms of determining its year for the <i>halakhot</i> of tithing, agree with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> <b>of</b> the <b>Sabbatical Year</b> that with regard to <b>an <i>etrog</i> we go</b> according to its <b>ripening, as we learned in a mishna</b> (<i>Bikkurim</i> 2:6): The halakhic status of the fruit of <b>an <i>etrog</i></b> tree <b>is like</b> that <b>of</b> a typical fruit <b>tree in three manners and</b> like that <b>of a vegetable in one manner.</b>
The mishna elaborates: Its halakhic status is like that <b>of a tree in three manners:</b> With regard <b>to <i>orla</i>,</b> i.e., it is prohibited to eat of its fruit during the first three years after its planting; with regard <b>to fourth-</b>year produce, i.e., fruits that grow during the fourth year after the tree’s planting, which may not be used outside of Jerusalem unless they are deconsecrated by means of redemption; <b>and</b> with regard <b>to the Sabbatical Year.</b> With regard to all those <i>halakhot</i>, the year to which the fruit is ascribed is determined by when it ripens. And its halakhic status is like that <b>of a vegetable in one manner:</b>

Daf 40a

It is like a vegetable in <b>that at the time of its picking it is tithed;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabban Gamliel.</b> If it was picked in the third year of the Sabbatical cycle, poor man’s tithe is separated although it ripened in the second year, when the obligation is to separate second tithe and not poor man’s tithe. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> The halakhic status of the fruit of <b>an <i>etrog</i></b> tree <b>is like</b> that <b>of</b> a typical fruit <b>tree in every matter.</b> In any case, with regard to ascribing the status of Sabbatical-Year produce to the fruits, it is apparent from the mishna that the status of an <i>etrog</i> of the sixth year that was picked in the seventh year is that of sixth-year produce.
The Gemara answers: <b>It was</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna that distinguishes between the <i>lulav</i> and the <i>etrog</i> <b>who stated</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the statement of that <b><i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei said</b> that <b>Avtolemos,</b> one of the Sages, <b>testified in the name of five Elders:</b> The status of an <b><i>etrog</i></b> is determined <b>by</b> the time of its <b>picking</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>tithes. And our Sages were counted in Usha,</b> reached a decision, <b>and said:</b> The status of an <i>etrog</i> is determined by the time of its picking <b>both</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>tithes and</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the Sabbatical Year.</b>
The Gemara questions the formulation of the <i>baraita</i>: With regard to the <b>Sabbatical Year, who mentioned it?</b> As no previous mention was made of the Sabbatical Year, the discussion of the status of an <i>etrog</i> during the Sabbatical Year is a non sequitur. The Gemara answers: The <i>baraita</i> <b>is incomplete, and this</b> is what <b>it is teaching:</b> The status of <b>an <i>etrog</i></b> is determined <b>by</b> the time of its <b>picking</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>tithes and</b> determined <b>by</b> the time of its <b>ripening</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <b>Sabbatical Year. And our Sages were counted in Usha and said:</b> The status of <b>an <i>etrog</i></b> is determined <b>by</b> the time of its picking <b>both</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>tithes and</b> with regard <b>to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the Sabbatical Year.</b>
§ The Gemara resumes its discussion of the mishna: <b>The reason</b> that a <i>lulav</i> may be purchased from an <i>am ha’aretz</i> during the Sabbatical Year <b>is</b> specifically <b>that it is a <i>lulav</i> of the sixth year that is entering the seventh.</b> This indicates by inference that a <i>lulav</i> <b>of the seventh</b> year is <b>sacred</b> with the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year. The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is it sacred? <b>It is merely wood, and wood is not subject</b> to <b>the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year, as it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>reed leaves and vine leaves that one piled for storage upon the field,</b> if <b>he gathered them for eating, they are subject</b> to <b>the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year; if he gathered them for</b> use as <b>wood,</b> e.g., for kindling, <b>they are not subject</b> to <b>the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year.</b> Apparently, wood or any other non-food product is not subject to the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year.
The Gemara answers: It is <b>different there,</b> in the case of the reed and vine leaves, <b>as the verse states:</b> “And the Sabbatical produce of the land shall be <b>for you for food”</b> (Leviticus 25:6). From the juxtaposition of the term: For you, and the term: For food, it is derived: <b>For you</b> is <b>similar to for food;</b> the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year takes effect on <b>those</b> items <b>whose benefit and whose consumption coincide. Wood is excluded, as its benefit is subsequent to its consumption.</b> The primary purpose of kindling wood is not accomplished with the burning of the wood; rather, it is with the charcoal that heats the oven. Therefore, it is not subject to the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year.
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t there wood</b> used to provide <b>heat</b> (Rabbeinu Ḥananel), whose <b>benefit coincides with its consumption? Rava said: Undesignated wood exists for fuel,</b> i.e., charcoal, so its benefit is subsequent to its consumption.
§ The Gemara notes: The matter of whether <b>kindling wood,</b> whose benefit is subsequent to its consumption, is subject to the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may neither transfer Sabbatical-Year produce,</b> e.g., wine, <b>for soaking</b> flax to prepare it for spinning, as the benefit derived from the flax is subsequent to its soaking, when the soaked and spun thread is woven into a garment; <b>nor for laundering</b> with it, as the benefit derived is subsequent to the laundering when one wears the clean clothes. Soaking the flax or laundering the garment in wine is consumption of the wine, as it is no longer potable. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: One may transfer</b> Sabbatical-Year produce for those purposes.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the rationale</b> for the statement <b>of</b> the <b>first <i>tanna</i>?</b> It is <b>as the verse states</b> with regard to Sabbatical-Year produce: <b>“For food,”</b> from which it is inferred: <b>And not for soaking and not for laundering. What is the rationale</b> for the statement <b>of Rabbi Yosei</b> permitting one to do so? It is <b>as the verse states: “For you,”</b> from which it is inferred: <b>For you, for all your needs, and even for soaking and for laundering.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to <b>the first <i>tanna</i>, isn’t it written: “For you”?</b> How does he explain that term? The Gemara answers: From <b>that</b> term <b>“for you”</b> it is derived: <b>For you, similar to for food;</b> the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year takes effect on <b>those</b> items <b>whose benefit and whose consumption coincide,</b> which <b>excludes soaking and laundering,</b> where the items’ <b>benefit is subsequent to their consumption.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to <b>Rabbi Yosei, isn’t it written: “For food,”</b> indicating that it may not be used for any other purpose? The Gemara answers: <b>He needs that</b> phrase to teach: <b>For food, and not for a remedy [<i>melugma</i>], as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>For food and not for a remedy.</b> The <i>baraita</i> continues: Do <b>you say: For food and not for a remedy, or</b> perhaps <b>it is only:</b> For food <b>and not for laundering? When</b> the verse <b>says: “For you,” for laundering is</b> already <b>stated</b> as permitted since it includes all one’s bodily needs. <b>How,</b> then, <b>do I uphold</b> that which the verse states: <b>“For food”?</b> It is: <b>For food, and not for a remedy.</b> And should one ask: <b>What did you see</b> that led you <b>to include the</b> use of Sabbatical-Year produce for <b>laundering and to exclude the</b> use of Sabbatical-Year produce as <b>a remedy?</b>

Daf 40b

Rabbi Yosei could respond: <b>I include laundering, which</b> applies <b>equally to every person,</b> as everyone needs clean clothes, <b>and I exclude a remedy, which</b> does <b>not</b> apply <b>equally to every person;</b> it is only for the ill.
The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught that which the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to Sabbatical-Year produce: <b>For food, and not for a remedy; for food, and not for sprinkling</b> wine in one’s house to provide a pleasant fragrance; <b>for food, and not to make it an emetic [<i>apiktoizin</i>]</b> to induce vomiting? <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this <i>baraita</i>? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, as, if</b> it were in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, isn’t there also soaking and laundering</b> that should have been excluded in the <i>baraita</i>, as in their opinion, use of Sabbatical-Year produce for those purposes is prohibited?
§ <b>Rabbi Elazar said: Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>is deconsecrated only by means of purchase;</b> however, it cannot be deconsecrated through redemption. Merely declaring that the sanctity of that produce is transferred to money or other produce is ineffective. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It is deconsecrated <b>both by means of purchase and by means of redemption.</b>
<b>What is the rationale for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar?</b> It is <b>as it is written: “In this year of Jubilee</b> you shall return every man unto his possession” (Leviticus 25:13), <b>and juxtaposed to it</b> it is written: <b>“And if you sell an item</b> to your neighbor” (Leviticus 25:14); this indicates that in the Jubilee Year, during which the <i>halakhot</i> of the Sabbatical Year are in effect, one deconsecrates the produce <b>by means of purchase and not by means of redemption.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan, what is the rationale for</b> his opinion? It is <b>as it is written: “For it is a Jubilee; it shall be consecrated</b> unto you” (Leviticus 25:12); this indicates that <b>just as</b> one redeems <b>consecrated</b> items <b>both by means of purchase and by means of redemption, so too, Sabbatical-Year</b> produce can be redeemed <b>both by means of purchase and by means of redemption.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan, what does he do with this</b> juxtaposition of the Jubilee Year to the verse: <b>“If you sell an item”?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>He needs it</b> to derive a <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> that statement <b>of Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina says: Come and see how severe</b> even the <b>hint of</b> violation of the prohibition of <b>the Sabbatical Year is;</b> as the prohibition against commerce with Sabbatical-Year produce is not one of the primary prohibitions of the Sabbatical Year, and its punishment is harsh. <b>A person</b> who <b>engages in commerce with Sabbatical-Year produce</b> is <b>ultimately</b> punished with the loss of his wealth to the point that he is forced to <b>sell his movable property and his vessels, as it is stated: “In this year of Jubilee</b> you shall return every man unto his possession” (Leviticus 25:13), <b>and juxtaposed to it,</b> it is written: <b>“And if you sell an item</b> to your neighbor” (Leviticus 25:14).
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Elazar, what does he do with this verse</b> from <b>which Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> derived his opinion? The Gemara answers: <b>He needs it</b> to derive <b>in accordance with</b> that <b>which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“For it is a Jubilee; it shall be consecrated</b> unto you” (Leviticus 25:12); <b>just as</b> the sanctity of <b>consecrated</b> items <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which <b>they</b> are redeemed, <b>so too,</b> the sanctity of <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which <b>it</b> is redeemed.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Elazar, and it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan.</b> The Gemara elaborates that <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Elazar: Sabbatical-Year</b> sanctity <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which the produce is redeemed, <b>as it is stated: “For it is a Jubilee; it shall be consecrated</b> unto you”; <b>just as</b> the sanctity of <b>consecrated</b> items <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which <b>they</b> are redeemed <b>and</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> to use the money for non-sacred purposes, <b>so too,</b> the sanctity of <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which <b>it</b> is redeemed, <b>and</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> to use this money for purposes for which Sabbatical-Year produce may not be used.
<b>Or</b> perhaps extend the analogy and derive that <b>just as</b> the sanctity of <b>consecrated</b> items <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which <b>they</b> are redeemed, and the consecrated item <b>assumes non-sacred</b> status, <b>so too,</b> the sanctity of <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>takes effect on money</b> or objects in exchange for which <b>it</b> is redeemed, and the Sabbatical-Year produce <b>assumes non-sacred</b> status. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “It shall be</b> consecrated unto you,” meaning: <b>It shall be as it is.</b> Although the sanctity of Sabbatical-Year produce takes effect on the money, the produce remains consecrated as well.
The Gemara explains: <b>How so?</b> If <b>one purchased meat with Sabbatical-Year produce, both this,</b> the produce, <b>and that,</b> the meat, <b>must be removed during the Sabbatical Year.</b> The meat may be eaten only as long as the produce in exchange for which it was purchased may be eaten, i.e., as long as produce of that kind remains in the field. However, if he <b>purchased fish</b> in exchange <b>for the meat, the meat emerges</b> from its consecrated status, <b>and the fish assumes</b> consecrated status. If he then <b>purchased wine</b> in exchange <b>for the fish, the fish emerges</b> from its consecrated status, <b>and the wine assumes</b> consecrated status. If <b>he purchased oil</b> in exchange <b>for the wine, the wine emerges</b> from its consecrated status, <b>and the oil assumes</b> consecrated status.
<b>How so? The last</b> item purchased <b>assumes</b> the consecrated status of produce <b>of the Sabbatical Year, and the produce itself</b> remains consecrated and <b>forbidden</b> and never loses its consecrated status. The Gemara notes: <b>From</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> each case using the term: <b>Purchased, purchased, apparently</b> it means that <b>by means of transaction, yes,</b> the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year takes effect; however, <b>by means of redemption, no,</b> the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year does not take effect.
The Gemara continues: <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan. Both Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>and second-tithe</b> produce <b>are deconsecrated upon domesticated animals, undomesticated animals, and fowl, whether</b> they are <b>alive or whether</b> they are <b>slaughtered;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say: Upon slaughtered</b> animals, <b>they are deconsecrated; upon</b> animals that are <b>alive, they are not deconsecrated.</b> The reason is that a rabbinic <b>decree</b> was issued <b>lest one raise flocks from them.</b> If one breeds a herd from that consecrated animal, the entire herd would be sacred and the potential for misuse of second-tithe property would be great.
<b>Rava said:</b> This <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Meir and the Rabbis

Daf 41a

is specifically <b>with regard to male</b> animals, which do not bear offspring. <b>However, with regard to female</b> animals, <b>everyone agrees</b> that <b>upon slaughtered</b> animals, produce <b>is deconsecrated,</b> but <b>upon</b> animals that are <b>alive,</b> produce <b>is not deconsecrated.</b> The reason is that <b>a decree</b> was issued <b>lest one raise flocks from</b> the females, as typically they bear offspring. The Sages extended the decree to include males as well. From the fact that the <i>baraita</i> uses the term deconsecrated, and not the term purchased, apparently the sanctity of Sabbatical-Year produce takes effect by means of redemption as well.
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> This <b>dispute</b> whether the sanctity of Sabbatical-Year produce takes effect by means of redemption or only by means of purchase <b>is with regard to the original</b> Sabbatical-Year <b>produce</b> itself. <b>However, with regard to secondary produce</b> purchased in exchange for Sabbatical-Year produce, <b>everyone agrees</b> that its sanctity takes effect <b>both by means of purchase and by means of redemption. And</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>baraita</i> cited in support of the opinion of Rabbi Elazar <b>teaches: Purchased, purchased,</b> employing that term even with regard to secondary produce, and not the terms deconsecrated or redeemed, does not prove that sanctity takes effect only by means of purchase. Rather, <b>since</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>taught the first clause</b> of the <i>halakha</i> employing the term <b>purchased, he taught the latter clause</b> employing the term <b>purchased,</b> even though sanctity takes effect even by means of redemption.
<b>Ravina raised an objection to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Ashi:</b> With regard to <b>one who has a <i>sela</i></b> coin that has the sanctity <b>of the Sabbatical Year and seeks to purchase a garment with it, how should he do</b> so? <b>He should go to the storekeeper whose</b> store <b>he typically</b> patronizes <b>and say to him: Give me fruits in</b> exchange for this <b><i>sela</i>, and</b> the storekeeper <b>gives him</b> fruits. <b>And then he says to</b> the storekeeper: <b>These fruits</b> that you sold me and that assumed the sanctity of the Sabbatical Year <b>are given to you as a gift.</b> The storekeeper may then eat them as one eats Sabbatical-Year produce. <b>And</b> the storekeeper <b>says to him: Here is a <i>sela</i> for you as a gift, and that</b> person <b>purchases with it whatever he wants,</b> as the <i>sela</i> was deconsecrated. Ravina asks: <b>But here, isn’t it secondary produce,</b> as the <i>sela</i> had previously been exchanged for the original Sabbatical-Year produce, <b>and</b> nevertheless the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: By means of purchase, yes,</b> it is effective; <b>by means of redemption, no,</b> it is not?
<b>Rather, Rav Ashi said,</b> contrary to the suggestion above, that <b>the dispute is</b> specifically <b>with regard to secondary produce; however, with regard to original produce, everyone agrees: By means of purchase, yes,</b> it is deconsecrated; <b>by means of redemption, no,</b> it is not deconsecrated. <b>And</b> with regard to <b>that which is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> cited in support of the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>Both Sabbatical-Year</b> produce <b>and second-tithe</b> produce are deconsecrated upon cattle, undomesticated animals, and fowl, indicating that the sanctity of Sabbatical-Year produce takes effect through both purchase and redemption. <b>What</b> is the meaning of <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce? It is referring to <b>money</b> exchanged for <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce but not to the produce itself.
And the same must be said with regard to the second tithe mentioned in this <i>baraita</i>, <b>as, if you do not say so</b> but say instead that the <b>second tithe</b> referred to in the <i>baraita</i> is <b>actual second-tithe</b> produce, <b>isn’t it written</b> with regard to the second tithe: “Then shall you turn it into money <b>and bind up the money in your hand…</b>and you shall bestow the money for whatsoever your soul desires” (Deuteronomy 14:25–26), indicating that second-tithe produce can be redeemed only with money, with which other food items may be purchased? <b>Rather,</b> the <i>baraita</i> must be referring to <b>money</b> exchanged for <b>second-tithe</b> produce and not to the produce itself. <b>Here, too,</b> with regard to the Sabbatical Year, the <i>baraita</i> is referring to <b>money</b> exchanged for <b>Sabbatical-Year</b> produce and not to the produce itself.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Originally,</b> during the Temple era, the <b><i>lulav</i> was taken in the Temple</b> for <b>seven</b> days, <b>and in the</b> rest of the <b>country</b> outside the Temple it was taken for <b>one day. Once the Temple was destroyed, Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai instituted</b> an ordinance <b>that the <i>lulav</i></b> should <b>be taken</b> even <b>in the</b> rest of the <b>country</b> for <b>seven</b> days, in <b>commemoration of the Temple.</b>
<b>And</b> for similar reasons, he instituted an ordinance <b>that</b> for <b>the entire day of waving</b> the <i>omer</i> offering, <b>it</b> should <b>be prohibited</b> to eat the grain of the new crop. It is prohibited to eat the grain of the new crop until the <i>omer</i> offering is brought and waved in the Temple on the sixteenth of Nisan. The offering was sacrificed in the morning; however, after taking potential delays into consideration, the new crop remained prohibited until it was clear that the offering had been sacrificed. Practically speaking, it was prohibited to eat the new grain until the sixteenth of Nisan was over; it was permitted only on the seventeenth. Once the Temple was destroyed and there was no longer an <i>omer</i> offering sacrificed, it was permitted to eat the new crop on the sixteenth. However, Rabban Yoḥanan instituted an ordinance that eating the new grain would remain prohibited until the seventeenth to commemorate the Temple.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive that <b>we institute</b> ordinances in <b>commemoration of the Temple? Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> that it is <b>as the verse states: “For I will restore health unto you and I will heal you of your wounds, says the Lord; because they have called you an outcast, she is Zion, there is none that seeks her”</b> (Jeremiah 30:17). From the fact that the verse states: “There is none that seeks her,” it can be learned <b>by inference that it requires seeking,</b> i.e., people should think of and remember the Temple. That is the reason for Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai’s ordinance.
§ The mishna continues: Rabban Yoḥanan instituted <b>that</b> for <b>the entire day of waving</b> the <i>Omer</i> offering, it is prohibited to eat the grain of the new crop. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for this ordinance? It is that <b>soon the Temple will be rebuilt, and</b> people <b>will say: Last year,</b> when the Temple was in ruins, <b>didn’t we eat</b> of the new crop <b>as soon as the eastern</b> horizon <b>was illuminated,</b> as the new crop was permitted immediately with the advent of the morning of the sixteenth of Nisan? <b>Now, too, let us eat</b> the new grain at that time. <b>And they do not know that</b> although <b>last year, when there was no Temple, the illuminating of the eastern</b> sky <b>permitted</b> one to eat the new grain immediately, <b>now that there is a Temple, the <i>omer</i></b> offering <b>permits</b> one to eat the new grain. Until the <i>omer</i> offering is sacrificed, the new grain is not permitted.
The Gemara asks: <b>When</b> is it <b>that</b> the Temple <b>will be rebuilt</b> in this scenario? <b>If we say that it will be rebuilt on the sixteenth</b> of Nisan, since in the morning the Temple was not yet built, the <b>illuminating of the eastern sky permitted</b> one to eat the new grain, as the <i>omer</i> offering could not yet be brought. <b>Rather,</b> say <b>that it will be rebuilt on the fifteenth</b> of Nisan or on some earlier date, in which case the new grain would not become permitted by the <b>illuminating of the eastern sky.</b> In that case, <b>from midday and onward let it be permitted</b> to eat the new grain, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna in tractate <i>Menaḥot</i>: <b>The</b> people <b>distant</b> from Jerusalem, who are unaware of the precise time when the <i>omer</i> was brought, <b>are permitted</b> to eat the new grain <b>from midday and onward because</b> the members of the <b>court are not indolent with regard to</b> the <i>omer</i> and would not postpone bringing the offering after midday.
The Gemara says: <b>No,</b> it is <b>necessary</b> to institute the ordinance only in the case where the Temple <b>will be rebuilt at night,</b> on the evening of the sixteenth, and there was no opportunity to cut the <i>omer</i> that night. <b>Alternatively,</b> it was necessary to institute the ordinance in the case where the Temple was built <b>adjacent to sunset</b> on the fifteenth because there would not be sufficient time to complete all the preparations and sacrifice the offering by noon the next day. Therefore, Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai instituted that the new grain is prohibited for the entire day of the sixteenth. <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said:</b> That is not the reason; rather, <b>Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai stated</b> his ordinance <b>in</b> accordance with <b>the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, who said: It is prohibited by Torah law</b> to eat the new grain until the seventeenth of Nisan, <b>as it is written:</b>

Daf 41b

“And you shall eat neither bread, nor roasted grain, nor fresh grain, <b>until this selfsame [<i>etzem</i>] day,</b> until you have brought the offering of your God” (Leviticus 23:14), indicating <b>until the essence [<i>itzumo</i>] of the day,</b> and not the night before. <b>And he holds that</b> when the verse states: <b>“Until,”</b> the word <b>until is inclusive,</b> meaning that the grain is permitted only after the conclusion of the sixteenth.
The Gemara asks: <b>And does</b> Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai <b>hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda? <b>But doesn’t he disagree with him, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Once the Temple was destroyed, Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai instituted that</b> for <b>the entire day of waving</b> the <i>omer</i> offering, <b>it</b> should <b>be prohibited</b> to eat the grain of the new crop. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said to him: Isn’t it prohibited by Torah law, as it is written: “Until this selfsame day,”</b> which means: <b>Until the essence of the day?</b> Apparently, they have two divergent opinions.
The Gemara answers: <b>It is Rabbi Yehuda who is mistaken. He thought</b> that Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai <b>is saying</b> it is prohibited <b>by rabbinic law. And that is not</b> so; <b>he is saying</b> it is prohibited <b>by Torah law.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t</b> the mishna <b>say:</b> Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai <b>instituted,</b> indicating that it is a rabbinic ordinance? The Gemara answers: <b>What</b> is the meaning of <b>instituted?</b> It means that <b>he interpreted</b> the verses in the Torah <b>and instituted</b> public notice for the multitudes to conduct themselves accordingly.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>the first</b> day <b>of</b> the <b>festival of <i>Sukkot</i> occurs on Shabbat, all of the people bring their <i>lulavim</i> to the synagogue</b> on Shabbat eve, as it is prohibited to carry in a public domain on Shabbat. <b>The next day,</b> on Shabbat, everyone <b>rises early and comes</b> to the synagogue. <b>Each and every one recognizes his</b> <i>lulav</i> <b>and takes it.</b> This emphasis that each and every one recognizes his own <i>lulav</i> and takes it is <b>because the Sages said: A person does not fulfill his obligation</b> to take the <i>lulav</i> <b>on the first day of the Festival with the <i>lulav</i> of another, and</b> on <b>the rest of the days of the Festival a person fulfills his obligation</b> even <b>with the <i>lulav</i> of another. Rabbi Yosei says:</b> If <b>the first day of</b> the <b>Festival occurs on Shabbat, and he forgot and carried the <i>lulav</i> out into the public domain, he is exempt</b> from liability to bring a sin-offering for this unwitting transgression <b>because he carried it out with permission,</b> i.e., he was preoccupied with the performance of the mitzva and carried it out.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>From where are these matters</b> derived, that one does not fulfill his obligation with the <i>lulav</i> of another on the first day of the Festival? It is <b>as the Sages taught</b> that it is written: <b>“And you shall take</b> for yourselves on the first day the fruit of a beautiful tree, branches of a date palm, and boughs of a dense-leaved tree, and willows of the brook” (Leviticus 23:40). The use of second person plural in the phrase: “And you shall take,” indicates <b>that there should be taking in the hand of each and every</b> person. The word <b>yourselves</b> in the phrase “take for yourselves” means: <b>From your own, to exclude a borrowed or stolen</b> <i>lulav</i>. <b>From here the Sages stated: A person does not fulfill his obligation on the first day of</b> the <b>Festival with the <i>lulav</i> of another unless</b> the other <b>gave it to him as</b> a full-fledged <b>gift,</b> as in that case it belongs to him.
There was <b>an incident involving Rabban Gamliel, and Rabbi Yehoshua, and Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya, and Rabbi Akiva, who were</b> all <b>traveling on a ship</b> during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i> <b>and only Rabban Gamliel had a <i>lulav</i>, which he</b> had <b>bought for one thousand <i>zuz</i>. Rabban Gamliel took it and fulfilled</b> his obligation <b>with it and then gave it to Rabbi Yehoshua as a gift. Rabbi Yehoshua took it and fulfilled</b> his obligation <b>with it and gave it to Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya as a gift. Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya took it and fulfilled</b> his obligation <b>with it and gave it to Rabbi Akiva as a gift. Rabbi Akiva took it and fulfilled</b> his obligation <b>with it and returned it to Rabban Gamliel.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need <b>to say</b> that Rabbi Akiva <b>returned</b> the <i>lulav</i> to Rabban Gamliel? The crux of the story is that each of the Sages fulfilled his obligation with the same <i>lulav</i> after receiving it as a gift. The Gemara answers: By including that detail, the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches us</b> another <b>matter in passing,</b> namely that <b>a gift</b> given <b>on the condition that it be returned is considered</b> a full-fledged <b>gift.</b> Even if the owner stipulates from the outset that the gift would be returned, since he gives it as a gift in the interim, its halakhic status is that of a full-fledged gift.
This is <b>like that which Rava said,</b> that in the case of one who says to another: <b>Here is an <i>etrog</i> for you on condition that you return it to me, and</b> the recipient <b>took it and fulfilled his obligation with it,</b> if <b>he returned</b> the <i>etrog</i>, <b>he fulfilled</b> his obligation of taking the <i>etrog</i>. However, if <b>he did not return</b> the <i>etrog</i>, <b>he did not fulfill</b> his obligation. Since he did not fulfill the condition, retroactively he never acquired the gift at all.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need <b>to say that</b> Rabban Gamliel <b>bought</b> this <i>lulav</i> <b>for one thousand <i>zuz</i>?</b> The Gemara answers: It is <b>to inform you how beloved mitzvot were to them</b> to the extent that he was willing to pay an exorbitant sum to purchase a <i>lulav</i>.
§ <b>Mar bar Ameimar said to Rav Ashi:</b> My <b>father would pray with</b> the four species in his hand in an expression of his love for the mitzva. The Gemara <b>raises an objection: A person</b> should <b>not hold phylacteries in his hand or a Torah scroll in his lap and pray</b> while doing so; <b>neither should he urinate with them</b> in his hand; <b>nor should he sleep with them</b> in his hand, <b>neither a deep sleep nor a brief nap.</b>
<b>And Shmuel said:</b> With regard to <b>a knife, a bowl</b> full of food, <b>a loaf</b> of bread, <b>or money, these</b> items <b>are similar to those</b> mentioned above; since he is concerned lest these items fall from his hand, he is distracted and he unable to concentrate on his prayers. Why, then, is that not the case with regard to <i>lulav</i>? It should be prohibited to hold the <i>lulav</i> during prayer for the same reason. The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> in the cases listed above, <b>they are not</b> related to performance of <b>a mitzva, and he is preoccupied with them.</b> Therefore, that preoccupation distracts his focus from his prayers. <b>Here,</b> in the case of the four species, <b>they are</b> related to performance of <b>a mitzva,</b> so <b>he is not preoccupied with them</b> in a manner that will distract him from his prayers.
The Gemara cites support for the custom mentioned above, as <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok says: This was the custom of the people of Jerusalem</b> during the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>. <b>A person leaves his house, and his <i>lulav</i> is in his hand; he goes to the synagogue,</b> and <b>his <i>lulav</i> is in his hand; he recites <i>Shema</i> and prays, and his <i>lulav</i> is in his hand; he reads the Torah and</b> a priest <b>lifts his hands</b> to recite the priestly benediction, and <b>he places it on the ground</b> because he cannot perform those tasks while holding the <i>lulav</i>. <b>He goes to visit the ill or to console mourners,</b> and <b>his <i>lulav</i> is in his hand; he enters the study hall</b> to study Torah, and <b>he sends his <i>lulav</i></b> home <b>in the hands of his son, in the hands of his slave, or in the hands of his agent.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaching us</b> by relating all these details that appear to establish the same practice? The Gemara explains: It is <b>to inform you how vigilant they were in</b> the performance of <b>mitzvot</b> and how much they cherished them.
§ The mishna continues: <b>Rabbi Yosei says</b> that if the first <b>day</b> of the <b>Festival</b> occurs on Shabbat, and one forgot and carried the <i>lulav</i> out into the public domain, he is exempt from liability to bring a sin-offering. <b>Abaye said:</b>

Daf 42a

The Sages <b>taught</b> that he is exempt <b>only</b> in a case <b>where he did not</b> yet <b>fulfill</b> his obligation. <b>However,</b> if <b>he</b> already <b>fulfilled</b> his obligation and he carries the <i>lulav</i> out, he is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin-offering. The Gemara asks: Is it possible to carry the <i>lulav</i> without fulfilling one’s obligation? <b>Didn’t he, from</b> the moment <b>that he lifted it, fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with it? Abaye said:</b> It is referring to a case <b>where he overturned it</b> and lifted it. One fulfills his obligation only when lifting it in the manner in which it grows.
<b>Rava said: Even if you say that</b> it is referring to a case where <b>he did not invert it, with what are we dealing here?</b> It is with a case <b>where he took</b> the <i>lulav</i> <b>out in a vessel</b> and did not fulfill his obligation. The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t Rava the one who said</b> that <b>taking by means of another object is considered taking?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>This applies only</b> when the addition is in <b>a deferential manner,</b> for wrapping or ornamentation. <b>But</b> if the addition is in <b>a degrading manner,</b> as in this case, where one places the <i>lulav</i> into a vessel and carries it that way, <b>no,</b> it is not considered taking.
<b>Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yosei would say:</b> In the case of <b>a bird</b> sacrificed as <b>a burnt-offering that is found among</b> other <b>birds</b> in one of the corners of the altar, <b>and</b> the priest <b>thought that it was a bird</b> sacrificed as <b>a sin-offering and he ate it,</b> as sin-offerings are eaten by priests, <b>he is exempt</b> from liability to bring a guilt-offering for misuse of consecrated items. The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> Rav Huna <b>teaching us?</b> Is it <b>that</b> if <b>one erred in the matter of a mitzva, he is exempt? This is</b> identical to <b>that</b> statement of Rabbi Yosei; what novel element is introduced by Rav Huna?
The Gemara answers: It is <b>lest you say</b> that <b>it is</b> only <b>there,</b> in the case of carrying the <i>lulav</i>, <b>that one who erred in a matter of a mitzva is exempt</b> and <b>that is</b> because <b>he performed a mitzva; however, here,</b> with regard to mistakenly identifying the bird-offerings, <b>where he erred in the matter of a mitzva but did not perform a mitzva</b> at all, <b>say no,</b> in that case he would not be exempt from liability to bring a guilt-offering. Therefore, Rav Huna <b>teaches us</b> that he is in fact exempt.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection. Rabbi Yosei says:</b> With regard to <b>one who slaughters the daily offering on Shabbat that is not properly inspected,</b> and a blemish is discovered that disqualifies the sacrifice, he unwittingly performed the prohibited labor of slaughtering on Shabbat. <b>He is liable</b> to bring <b>a sin-offering, and he needs</b> to bring <b>a different daily offering.</b> Even though he erred in a matter of a mitzva, he is liable.
Rav Huna <b>said</b> in response to the objection: Proof may be cited, <b>except</b> not <b>from that</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>as it was stated concerning</b> that <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rav Shmuel bar Ḥatai said</b> that <b>Rav Hamnuna Sava said</b> that <b>Rav Yitzḥak bar Ashian said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> It is referring to <b>a case where they brought</b> the sheep for the daily offering <b>from a chamber</b> in which there were sheep <b>that are not inspected.</b> Since under no circumstances should one take a sheep for the daily offering from uninspected sheep, his error cannot be attributed to preoccupation with the mitzva. Therefore, although he was engaged in performance of a mitzva, he is not exempt from liability to bring a sin-offering.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A woman</b> may <b>receive</b> a <i>lulav</i> <b>from her son or from her husband and return it on Shabbat to the water</b> in which it had been placed. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: On Shabbat one may return</b> the <i>lulav</i> to the water; <b>and on the Festival one may</b> even <b>add</b> fresh water to the vessel so the <i>lulav</i> will not wilt; and <b>during</b> the intermediate days of <b>the Festival, one may</b> even <b>change</b> the water.
<b>A minor who knows</b> how <b>to wave</b> the <i>lulav</i> <b>is obligated in</b> the mitzva of <b><i>lulav</i></b> due to the requirement to train him in the performance of mitzvot.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna that a woman may receive the <i>lulav</i>, the Gemara asks: That is <b>obvious.</b> Why would it be prohibited? The Gemara answers that it is necessary to state this <b>lest you say: Since a woman is not subject to the obligation</b> of the four species, as it is a time-bound, positive mitzva, <b>say</b> that <b>she should not receive</b> the <i>lulav</i>, as for her, moving the <i>lulav</i> is tantamount to moving set-aside objects and would therefore be prohibited. Therefore, the mishna <b>teaches us</b> that it is permitted.
§ It is taught in the mishna: <b>A minor who knows</b> how <b>to wave</b> the <i>lulav</i> is obligated in the mitzva of <i>lulav</i>. <b>The Sages taught: A minor who knows how to wave</b> the <i>lulav</i> <b>is obligated</b> in the mitzva of <b><i>lulav</i>;</b> one who knows how <b>to wrap himself</b> in a garment, <b>is obligated in</b> the mitzva of <b>ritual fringes;</b> if he knows <b>to preserve</b> the sanctity of <b>phylacteries</b> in a state of cleanliness, <b>his father buys him phylacteries;</b> if he <b>knows</b> how <b>to speak, his father</b> immediately <b>teaches him Torah and <i>Shema</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> in this context, <b>what is</b> the <b>Torah</b> taught to a child who has just learned to speak? <b>Rav Hamnuna said:</b> It is referring to the verse: <b>“Moses commanded us Torah, an inheritance of the congregation of Jacob”</b> (Deuteronomy 33:4), underscoring the relationship between the Jewish people and the Torah. The Gemara asks further: <b>And what is <i>Shema</i></b> taught to a child who has just learned to speak? The Gemara answers: It is referring to the <b>first verse</b> of <i>Shema</i>.
The Sages continued: If the minor is one <b>who knows to protect his body</b> from ritual impurity, it is permitted to <b>eat ritually pure</b> food that came into contact <b>with his body.</b> If he is one who knows <b>to protect his hands</b> from ritual impurity, it is permitted to <b>eat ritually pure</b> food that came into contact <b>with his hands.</b> If he is one <b>who knows to be asked</b> and accurately clarify which objects he touched, his status is like that of an adult based on the following distinction: If the question was with regard to ritual impurity <b>in the private domain</b> and it is a case of <b>uncertainty,</b> the item in question is deemed <b>impure.</b> However, if the question was with regard to ritual impurity <b>in the public domain</b> and it is a case of <b>uncertainty,</b> the item in question is deemed <b>pure.</b> If the minor is a priest <b>who knows</b> how <b>to spread his hands</b> and recite the priestly benediction, <b>one distributes <i>teruma</i> to him in the granary</b> as one would to any other priest.

Daf 42b

If he is one <b>who knows</b> how <b>to slaughter</b> an animal, <b>one may eat from</b> animals <b>that he slaughtered. Rav Huna said:</b> That is the <i>halakha</i> <b>provided that an adult is standing over him</b> overseeing the slaughter.
If he is one who <b>is able to eat an olive-bulk of grain, one distances</b> himself <b>four cubits from his feces and from his urine</b> before praying or reciting <i>Shema</i>, as the feces and urine of a child at that stage of development produce offensive odors like those of an adult. <b>Rav Ḥisda said:</b> That is the <i>halakha</i> <b>provided that</b> the minor <b>can eat</b> the olive-bulk of grain <b>in</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread. However, if it takes him longer, one need not distance himself before praying. <b>Rav Ḥiyya, son of Rav Yeiva, said: And with regard to an adult, even if he is unable to eat</b> an olive-bulk of grain <b>in</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, one must distance himself from his feces before praying, <b>as it is written: “And he that increases knowledge, increases sorrow”</b> (Ecclesiastes 1:18), meaning that as one grows older he becomes more flawed.
If he is one who <b>is able to eat an olive-bulk of roasted</b> meat, <b>one slaughters the Paschal lamb on his</b> behalf, and he is included in the group assembled to eat the Paschal lamb, <b>as it is stated: “According to every man’s eating</b> you shall make your count for the lamb” (Exodus 12:4). <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> Ability to consume an olive-bulk is insufficient to include him in the group; rather, he is not included <b>until he is able to discern</b> what he is <b>eating. How</b> is that determined? If <b>one gives him a pebble and he throws it</b> away, and if one gives him <b>a nut and he takes it,</b> he may be included in the group for eating the Paschal lamb.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The <b><i>lulav</i></b> is taken <b>and</b> the altar is encircled together with the <b>willow branch</b> either <b>six or seven</b> days, depending on which day of the Festival occurs on Shabbat. The obligation to recite <b>the</b> full <b><i>hallel</i> and the</b> mitzva of <b>rejoicing,</b> i.e., eating the meat of the peace-offering, <b>is in effect for eight</b> days, seven days of <i>Sukkot</i> and the Eighth Day of Assembly. The mitzva of <b><i>sukka</i> and</b> the ritual of <b>the water libation</b> on the altar <b>are</b> in effect for <b>seven</b> days. <b>The flute</b> is played in the Temple for <b>five or six</b> days, depending on which day of the Festival occurs on Shabbat, to enhance the rejoicing on the Festival.
The mishna elaborates: The <b><i>lulav</i></b> is taken for <b>seven</b> days. <b>How</b> so? If <b>the first day of the Festival occurs on Shabbat,</b> since the mitzva to take the <i>lulav</i> on the first day is a mitzva by Torah law, it overrides Shabbat and one takes the <i>lulav</i> that day. As a result, the <b><i>lulav</i></b> is then taken for <b>seven</b> days. <b>And</b> if the first day occurs on one of the <b>rest of the days</b> of the week and one of the other days of the Festival coincides with Shabbat, the <i>lulav</i> is taken only <b>six</b> days.
